Commonwealth v. Lewis


No. 109 E.D. Appeal Docket 1984


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


523 Pa. 466; 567 A.2d 1376; 1989 Pa. LEXIS 427


April 12, 1989, Argued  
December 22, 1989, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of Death Dated April 2, 1985, by the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County at Nos. 585-586, February Term, 1983.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Criminal appellant sought review of a decision from the Court of Common Pleas, Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania), which found appellant guilty of first-degree murder and which imposed the death sentence.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted of first-degree murder and was sentenced to death after multiple eyewitnesses in a bar saw appellant stab the victim. Appellant sought review of his conviction and sentence on multiple grounds. The court affirmed. The court found that there was sufficient evidence to establish that appellant specifically intended to kill the victim. The court found that the trial court did not abuse its discretion when it disqualified jurors because jurors' views toward the death penalty would have substantially impaired or prevented them from performing their duties. The trial court did not err when it required appellant to wear glasses found at the crime scene because the display was not excessively suggestive and prejudicial. The trial court did not err when it refused to admit appellant's post-arrest statement because admission of statements made at the time of arrest to support appellant's version of the facts was clearly offensive to the hearsay rule. Admission of a photograph was not error because the photograph was relevant and non-inflammatory. Finally, the death sentence was not excessive or disproportionate when compared to similar cases.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed appellant's conviction for first-degree murder because the evidence was sufficient, because the trial court did not err when it disqualified jurors, and because a photograph of the crime scene was admissible. The court affirmed appellant's death sentence because the sentence was not excessive or disproportionate when compared to similar cases.

 

2 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. O'Shea


No. 15 W.D. Appeal Docket, 1987


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


523 Pa. 384; 567 A.2d 1023; 1989 Pa. LEXIS 403


Argued: September 25, 1989  
December 13, 1989, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Denied March 20, 1990.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence entered February 2, 1987 in the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County, Criminal Division, at Nos. CC 8512675 and CC 8512903 wherein the Court imposed the jury's sentence of death.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment entered in the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County (Pennsylvania), which found him guilty, after a jury trial, of robbery and of murder of the first degree, for which he was sentenced to death.


OVERVIEW: Defendant appealed from a death sentence after being convicted of first-degree murder. The court affirmed. It rejected a claim that police had no right under the Statewide Municipal Police Jurisdiction Act (SMPJA), 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 8953, to search and seize inculpating items in another jurisdiction, holding that SMPJA neither prohibited the conduct nor required suppression of the evidence. The homeowners had authorized the search, rendering it legal. The court upheld lower court rulings concerning the admission of photographs. The court rejected claims as to defendant's nonuse of certain mitigation evidence, upholding a lower court ruling that if defendant offered evidence that he had foiled a death plot against a detective, the jury would be permitted to hear that defendant tried to use his role to obtain leniency. The court also held that information relating to the death plot was not Brady material; that the trial judge, a former prosecutor, did not have to recuse himself; that the jury instructions were proper; and that the use of a death sentence for felony murder was constitutional.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the conviction and sentence because the lower court corrected refused to suppress evidence obtained without a warrant in a foreign jurisdiction; did not abuse its discretion in admitting certain photographs; correctly ruled concerning the scope of testimony on certain mitigating circumstances; and had not erred in denying defendant's recusal motion, based on the trial judge's prior employment as a prosecutor.

 

3 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Blackwell v. State Ethics Comm'n


No. 162 E.D. Appeal Docket 1988


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


523 Pa. 347; 567 A.2d 630; 1989 Pa. LEXIS 426


October 23, 1989, Argued  
December 13, 1989, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Commonwealth Court entered October 25, 1988 at No. 2222 C.D. 1988,     Pa. Cmwlth.    , 556 A.2d 988.



DISPOSITION: Appellees' Application to Quash the Appeal is granted. Jurisdiction is relinquished.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant state ethics commission sought review of a decision of the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania), which stayed a subpoena served on the bank of appellee councilman and denied appellee council members' request to enjoin appellant from continuing to investigate appellees. Appellant contended that the stay of the subpoena effectively granted a preliminary injunction of its investigation. Appellees filed an application to quash the appeal.


OVERVIEW: Appellant state ethics commission began an investigation of appellee council members and issued a subpoena to a bank where one of the appellees had accounts. Appellees' request for injunctive relief was denied, but their motion to stay the subpoena was granted. The court granted appellees' motion to quash an appeal from the stay order. The court found that during the period of time appellant was investigating appellees and issuing the subpoena, it was a legal non-entity, and any action taken by it was null and void. Legislative resolutions that extended the effective date of appellant's authority pursuant to the Sunset Act, 71 P.S. §§ 1795.1-1795.14, were also held to be null and void and without legal effect. 71 P.S. § 1795.4(4) was held to be an unconstitutional delegation of the legislative power under Pa. Const. art. II, § 1 because it did not merely delegate the power to administer a procedural mechanism of the Sunset Act but delegated complete control to six designated members of the general assembly.


OUTCOME: Appellee council members' application to quash the appeal of appellant state ethics commission was granted on the ground that appellant ceased to exist by operation of statute following a wind-up period and was a legal non-entity during the time of its investigation of appellees and the issuance of a subpoena.

 

4 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth, Dep't of Environmental Resources v. Blosenski Disposal Service


No. 139 Eastern District Appeal Docket 1988


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


523 Pa. 274; 566 A.2d 845; 1989 Pa. LEXIS 386; 30 ERC (BNA) 1835; 20 ELR 20384


April 13, 1989, Argued  
November 16, 1989, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Commonwealth Court Entered at No. 205 C.C. 1986, on October 9, 1987, Reversing and Remanding the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Chester County, Criminal Division, Entered at No. 54, 55-1983 on December 23, 1985.

    Pa. Cmwlth. Ct.    ,     A.2d     (198 ).



DISPOSITION: For the reasons set forth above, the judgment of the Commonwealth Court in the instant case must be, and the same is hereby, affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a case involving improper disposal of solid waste, appellant disposal service challenged an order of the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania), which reversed and remanded the lower court's order suppressing testimony grounded on information obtained without a search warrant.


OVERVIEW: Appellant disposal service, convicted of a summary offense for operating a transfer station without a permit, challenged the constitutionality of the warrantless inspection provisions of § 608 of the Solid Waste Management Act (act), 35 P.S. § 6018.608. The trial court's suppression order, entered on reconsideration, was reversed. On further appeal, the issue was whether suppression of the evidence was required when the inspector entered private property, not visible from the public road, and searched an enclosed area without first obtaining a search warrant. The court determined that the warrantless search provisions of the act were valid under the administrative search exception to the warrant requirement of U.S. Const. amend. IV. The court found that anyone involved in a business engaged in the disposal of solid waste was expected to be aware that, because of the vital public and statutory interest that society had in proper solid waste disposal from an environmental standpoint, unannounced inspections could be anticipated. Additionally, this result was required by Pa. Const. art. I, § 8. The judgment below was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the reversal of the suppression order because the risks solid waste posed to the public, coupled with appellant disposal service's knowledge of those risks, justified the warrantless search.

Commonwealth v. Ball


No. 145 Eastern District Appeal Docket 1988


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


523 Pa. 216; 565 A.2d 1143; 1989 Pa. LEXIS 360


April 11, 1989, Argued  
October 6, 1989, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari Denied January 16, 1990. See 110 S.Ct. 844.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the March 14, 1988 Superior Court Order at Nos. 2412, 2413, 2448, 2455, Philadelphia, 1984 Affirming the Suppression Orders of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County, Criminal Division, at Nos. 83-05-3641-3644 and 83-05-3645-3648, Entered August 9, and 14, 1984.

    Pa. Super Ct.    ,     A.2d     (19  ).



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded for further proceedings consistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The commonwealth appealed a decision of the Superior Court (Pennsylvania), which affirmed an order that suppressed evidence provided by defendants to their casualty insurer who, in turn, provided it to the commonwealth pursuant to 40 P.S. § 1610.3 of the Arson Reporting Immunity Act, finding defendants were economically coerced into waiving their rights under U.S. Const. amend. V.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were requested to submit to questioning under oath by their casualty insurance carrier investigating a fire that damaged defendants' business. The carrier agreed that the information obtained would not be divulged to any third party unless subpoenaed or subject to discovery by anyone with proper authority. The information was provided by the carrier to the commonwealth under 40 P.S. § 1610.3 of the Arson Reporting Immunity Act, and when defendants were charged with arson they sought and obtained the suppression of the aforesaid evidence on the basis that the waiver of their rights under U.S. Const. amend. V, was economically coerced by the insurer under the implied threat that they would forfeit their right to payment under the policy for failure to cooperate with the investigation. The superior court affirmed the suppression order and the commonwealth appealed. The court reversed the order and remanded the cause for further proceedings, finding that defendants were emphatically advised that the investigation information would be divulged to anyone with proper authority and that no discussion was had concerning defendants' rights against self-incrimination.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order that suppressed the evidence provided by defendants to their casualty insurance carrier who, in turn, provided it to the commonwealth under the Arson Reporting Immunity Act, finding that defendants were not economically coerced into waiving their Fifth Amendment rights by their insurance carrier acting as an arm of the state.

Commonwealth v. Strong


No. 41 E.D. Appeal Dkt. 1986


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


522 Pa. 445; 563 A.2d 479; 1989 Pa. LEXIS 331


January 28, 1987, Argued  
July 26, 1989, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Writ of certiorari denied Strong v. Pennsylvania, 494 U.S. 1060, 108 L. Ed. 2d 775, 110 S. Ct. 1536, 1990 U.S. LEXIS 1615, 58 U.S.L.W. 3614 (1990)
Post-conviction proceeding at, Motion for new trial granted by Commonwealth v. Strong, 563 Pa. 455, 761 A.2d 1167, 2000 Pa. LEXIS 2836 (2000)
Appeal after remand at, Remanded by Commonwealth v. Strong, 2003 Pa. Super. LEXIS 788 (Pa. Super. Ct., Apr. 23, 2003)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal From the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Luzerne County, entered February 28, 1986, at No. 490 Criminal Division 1984


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the Court of Common Pleas of Luzerne County (Pennsylvania), which entered convictions against him for murder in the first degree, kidnapping, robbery, theft of an automobile, and theft of personal property valued at $ 200.00 or more. Defendant was sentenced to death.


OVERVIEW: Defendant and codefendant were hitchhiking. The victim offered them a ride. At trial, codefendant testified that that defendant shot the victim. The jury found defendant guilt of murder in the first degree, kidnapping, robbery, theft of an automobile, and theft of personal property valued at $ 200 or more. The jury found three aggravating circumstances but no mitigating circumstances. Accordingly, the trial court sentenced defendant to death. On appeal, the court found no error in the trial court's admission of defendant's most recent robbery conviction for impeachment purposes. The court also upheld the admissibility of a pre-death photo of the victim and several post-mortem photos. The court determined that defendant was not entitled to a mistrial on the basis of certain comments made by the prosecutor. Also, the court agreed with the jury's findings of aggravating circumstances. Finally, the court rejected defendant's challenges to the constitutionality of the death penalty statute. Thus, defendant's convictions and death sentence were affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions for murder in the first degree, kidnapping, robbery, theft of an automobile, and theft of personal property valued at $ 200.00 and the death sentence. The court rejected defendant's challenges on appeal, which included issues of admissibility of evidence, prosecutorial misconduct, mitigating circumstances, and the constitutionality of the death penalty.

Pa. Pub. Util. Comm'n v. Phila. Elec. Co.


Nos. 116 E.D. Appeal Docket 1988, 117 E.D. Appeal Docket 1988


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


522 Pa. 338; 561 A.2d 1224; 1989 Pa. LEXIS 330


January 19, 1989, Argued  
July 18, 1989, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Denied January 26, 1990.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Order of the Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania entered February 24, 1988 at No. 3204 C.D. 1985, reversing the Order of the Pennsylvania Public Utility Commission entered October 30, 1985 at Commission Docket Nos. P-830453, M-840375 and M-FACE 8408.

 114 Pa. Commw. 22, 538 A.2d 98 (1988).



DISPOSITION: The 4 Order of the Commonwealth Court is affirmed in part and reversed in part. 2



2 Commonwealth Court also vacated and remanded a disallowance of energy replacement costs incurred as a result of an outage at Eddystone I. Because neither party sought our review of this determination, this limited issue must be remanded to the Commission in accordance with the Order of Commonwealth Court.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, Public Utility Commission (PUC) and consumer advocate, challenged an order of the Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania which reversed the denial of a rate increase requested by appellee electric utility. The reversal was based on PUC's alleged application of the incorrect standard of care to appellee under 66 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 1301.


OVERVIEW: Appellants, Public Utility Commission (PUC) and consumer advocate, challenged an order which reversed PUC's denial of a rate increase requested by appellee electric utility. PUC had determined that the various outages for which appellee sought cost recovery were its own fault and the result of mismanagement. Appellee obtained reversal on the basis that PUC applied an incorrect heightened standard of care to appellee when it was determining whether the requested rate increase was just and reasonable under 66 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 1301. Appellants sought review of the reversal and the court affirmed in part and reversed in part. The court found that PUC had correctly applied an ordinary negligence standard in determining whether appellee was at fault for the outages and that the term "heightened standard of care" was simply a mis-naming of the principle applied. The court found that the only portion of PUC's determination that was not supported by substantial evidence was its refusal to allow cost recovery for an unforeseeable gasket leak.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed in part and reversed in part the ruling which reversed the denial of a rate increase requested by appellee electric utility and opposed by appellants, Public Utility Commission (PUC) and consumer advocate. The court found that PUC had applied the correct negligence standard to appellee, although it used the wrong term, and that the only portion of its denial not supported by the record involved an unforeseeable gasket leak.

Pittsburgh v. Commonwealth


Nos. 2 W.D. Appeal Docket 1988, 25 W.D. Appeal Docket 1988


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


522 Pa. 20; 559 A.2d 513; 1989 Pa. LEXIS 243


September 29, 1988, Argued  
May 31, 1989, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Order of Commonwealth Court Entered at No. 3293 C.D. 1986, dated December 24, 1987, Sustaining Appellees' Preliminary Objection to Appellant's Petition for Review, 112 Pa. Commw. 188, 535 A.2d 680 (1987) 



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. 3



3 The City also raises the issue of whether it has standing to attack the constitutionality of the noted provisions of LTEA and NRC. Because of our disposition of the constitutional issues in the Mayor's appeal, in which no one questions his standing, it is unnecessary to address the standing issue.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, city and its mayor, challenged the order of Commonwealth Court that sustained the preliminary objection of appellees, commonwealth and its secretary of revenue, to appellants' petition for review. Appellants alleged that § 14 of the Local Tax Enabling Act, 53 P.S. § 6914, and § 302(a)(7) of the Home Rule Charter and Optional Plans Law, 53 P.S. § 1-302(a)(7), were unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Appellants, city and its mayor, filed a petition for review seeking a declaration that § 14 of the Local Tax Enabling Act (LTEA), 53 P.S. § 6914, and § 302(a)(7) of the Home Rule Charter and Optional Plans Law (HRC), 53 P.S. § 1-302(a)(7), which prevented appellants from collecting its wage tax from non-city residents who worked within the city's boundaries, were unconstitutional as violative of the uniformity clause, Pa. Const. art. VIII, § 1, and the equal protection clause, U.S. Const. amend. XIV. Appellees, commonwealth and its secretary of revenue, filed preliminary objections contending that appellant city lacked standing to assert the constitutional rights of its residents and that classifications distinguishing between resident and non-resident wage earners were valid. The trial court sustained all the preliminary objections and dismissed the petition for review. Appellant challenged that decision. Affirming the dismissal, the court found that there was a reasonable basis for the classification based upon residency and that neither of the challenged statutes violated constitutional provisions.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's decision that affirmed the trial court's dismissal of the petition for review filed by appellants, city and its mayor. The court held that there was a reasonable basis for the classification based upon residency in connection with the tax regulations.

In re Stout


Nos. 55 E.D. Misc. Docket 1989, 61 E.D. Misc. Docket 1989, 73 E.D. Misc. Docket 1989


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


521 Pa. 571; 559 A.2d 489; 1989 Pa. LEXIS 241; 49 Fair Empl. Prac. Cas. (BNA) 1419


April 12, 1989, Argued  
May 18, 1989, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state of Pennsylvaniacommenced a quo warranto and mandamus action to remove petitioner judge from office due to mandatory retirement age based on Pa. Const. art. V, § 16. Petitioner court administrator requested for the court to assume original jurisdiction regarding the rights of petitioner judge and petitioner judge was deemed an indispensable party, thus she was granted leave to intervene in the matter.


OVERVIEW: A judicial vacancy was created on the highest state court and pursuant to constitutional mandate was filled by the appointment of petitioner judge. Petitioner judge reached the age of seventy years. The state commenced a quo warranto and mandamus action to remove petitioner judge from office based on Pa. Const. art. V, § 16 that dictated the length of the term of office for which an appointee to fill a vacancy may serve. Petitioner court administrator requested for the highest state court to assume original jurisdiction regarding the rights of petitioner judge and to provide advice as to whether to continue her on the payroll. Because petitioner judge was deemed an indispensable party she was granted leave to intervene in the matter commenced by petitioner court administrator since her rights were at issue. Petitioner judge argued that appointees to the bench were not subject to mandatory retirement age. The highest state court granted summary judgment in favor of the state and petitioner court administrator was directed to remove petitioner judge as an active justice because gubernatorial appointees were not exempt from constitutionally valid mandatory retirement provisions.


OUTCOME: The highest state court granted summary judgment in favor of the state and petitioner court administrator was directed to remove petitioner judge as an active justice because all members of the judiciary, whether appointed, elected or retained were mandated by the state constitution to retire upon reaching the age of seventy years, thus petitioner judge was also required to retire upon reaching the age of seventy years.

Commonwealth v. Melilli


Nos. 133 E.D. Appeal Docket 1987, 134 E.D. Appeal Docket 1987, 135 E.D. Appeal Docket 1987, 136 E.D. Appeal Docket 1987, 137 E.D. Appeal Docket 1987, 138 E.D. Appeal Docket 1987, 139 E.D. Appeal Docket 1987, 140 E.D. Appeal Docket 1987


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


521 Pa. 405; 555 A.2d 1254; 1989 Pa. LEXIS 105


October 24, 1988, Argued  
March 13, 1989, Decided *


* This decision was considered and rendered prior to March 7, 1989.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Superior Court Entered on January 29, 1987, at Nos. 3147, 3148 Philadelphia, 1984 and 1121 Philadelphia, 1985 Reversing the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County, Criminal Division, Entered October 29, 1984, at No. 84-03-1075-1079.

Appeal from the Order of the Superior Court Entered on January 29, 1987, at Nos. 3149, 3150 Philadelphia, 1984 and 1116 Philadelphia, 1985 Reversing the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County, Criminal Division, Entered October 29, 1984, at No. 84-03-1405-1408.

Appeal from the Order of the Superior Court Entered on January 29, 1987, at Nos. 3151, 3152 Philadelphia, 1984 and 1118 Philadelphia, 1985 Reversing the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County, Criminal Division, Entered October 29, 1984, at No. 84-03-1409-1413.

Appeal from the Order of the Superior Court Entered on January 29, 1987, at Nos. 3153, 3154 Philadelphia, 1984 and 1117 Philadelphia, 1985 Reversing the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County, Criminal Division,  [***2] Entered October 29, 1984, at No. 84-03-1424-1428.

Appeal from the Order of the Superior Court Entered on January 29, 1987, at Nos. 3155, 3156 Philadelphia, 1984 and 1120 Philadelphia, 1985 Reversing the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County, Criminal Division, Entered October 29, 1984, at No. 84-03-1429-1433.

Appeal from the Order of the Superior Court Entered on January 29, 1987, at Nos. 3157, 3158 Philadelphia, 1984 and 1119 Philadelphia, 1985 Reversing the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County, Criminal Division, Entered October 29, 1984, at No. 84-03-1434-1438.

Appeal from the Order of the Superior Court Entered on January 29, 1987, at Nos. 3161, 3162 Philadelphia, 1984 and 1122 Philadelphia, 1985 Reversing the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County, Criminal Division, Entered October 29, 1984, at No. 84-03-1472-1476.

Appeal from the Order of the Superior Court Entered on January 29, 1987, at Nos. 3159, 3160 Philadelphia, 1984 and 1123 Philadelphia, 1985 Reversing the Order of the Court of Common [***3] Pleas of Philadelphia County, Criminal Division, Entered october 29, 1984, at No. 84-03-1444-1447.

     Pa. Superior Ct.    , 522 A.2d 1106 (1987) 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants in consolidated cases challenged orders of the Superior Court (Pennsylvania), which reversed orders granting appellants' motions to suppress all telephonic evidence gained from pen registers and related wiretaps under the Wiretapping and Electronic Surveillance Control Act, 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. §§ 5701-5726.


OVERVIEW: Appellants were charged with crimes related to organized illegal gambling operations. The police had placed pen registers on appellants' telephone lines to record all outgoing calls and, based on evidence received in this manner, the police had obtained warrants to put wiretaps on the same lines. Appellants successfully moved to suppress all the telephonic evidence and the state sought review, where the order granting suppression was reversed. Appellants then challenged the reversal, and the court reversed, reinstating the suppression order. The court held that probable cause was required to place a pen register on a telephone line under the Wiretapping and Electronic Surveillance Control Act, 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. §§ 5701-5726, and that none had been shown in any of the consolidated cases. In addition, the court held that there was no "good faith" exception to the probable cause requirement. The court acknowledged that these standards were in excess of those required under the federal constitution. Finally, the court found that the wiretap information was the fruit of the poisonous tree because it had no reliable basis outside of the illegal pen registers.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the lower court, reinstating the order granting appellants' motions to suppress evidence unlawfully obtained from pen registers. The court held that probable cause was required to place a pen register on appellants' telephone lines and that there was no "good faith" exception to the probable cause requirement.

Commonwealth v. Abu-Jamal


No. 51 E.D. Appeal Dkt. 1983


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


521 Pa. 188; 555 A.2d 846; 1989 Pa. LEXIS 79


January 19, 1988, Argued  
March 6, 1989, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas, Trial Division, Criminal Section, of Philadelphia County, as of Information Nos. 1357-1358, January Sessions, 1982.



DISPOSITION: The judgments 2 of sentence are affirmed. The prothonotary of the Eastern District is directed to transmit a full and complete record of the trial, sentencing hearing, imposition of sentence, and review by this Court to the Governor. 42 Pa.C.S. § 9711(i).



2 The appellant has not challenged the sentence of two and one-half to five years' imprisonment imposed on the conviction of possession of an instrument of crime.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the judgment of the Court of Common Pleas, Trial Division, Criminal Section, of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania), which convicted defendant, following a jury trial, of first degree murder and which sentenced him to death.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted, following a jury trial, of first degree murder, and was sentenced to death. Defendant appealed the trial court judgment, arguing numerous errors, including that he was entitled to a new trial on account of several errors occurring during jury selection. On appeal, the court affirmed the judgment of the trial court, holding that mere disparity of number in the racial make-up of the jury, though relevant, was inadequate to establish a prima facie case of discrimination in jury selection. The court also held that defendant's implied claim that peremptory challenges not used in selecting the jury may be carried over for use in the selection of alternates was prohibited by Pa. R. Crim. P. 1108(b). The court additionally held that the court's decision to undertake the voir dire questioning was not a violation of his U.S. Const. amend. VI right to self-representation because a defendant appearing pro se was subject to the same rules of procedure as was a counseled defendant.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court that convicted defendant of first degree murder, holding that he failed to establish a prima facie case of discrimination in jury selection.

 

12 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Goodheart v. Casey


No. 33 M.D. Appeal Docket, 1988


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


521 Pa. 316; 555 A.2d 1210; 1989 Pa. LEXIS 55; 10 Employee Benefits Cas. (BNA) 2122


Argued: January 19, 1989  
March 3, 1989, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Granted May 9, 1989.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Order of the Commonwealth Court entered on July 20, 1988, at No. 3255 C.D. 1986.

     Pa. Commonwealth Ct.    , 545 A.2d 329 (1988).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, the State Employee's Retirement Board, challenged the judgment of the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania), which entered summary judgment in favor of appellees, judges who took office after March 1, 1974, and the Pennsylvania Conference of Trial Judges. Appellees prevailed on their argument that they were entitled to the same range of retirement benefit packages as were available to judges who entered service before that date.


OVERVIEW: The legislature enacted a statute that eliminated a retirement benefit package that was available to judges who took office prior to March 1, 1974. Appellees, judges who took office after March 1, 1974, and the Pennsylvania Conference of Trial Judges, filed an action that challenged the law. The trial court entered summary judgment in appellee's favor. Appellant, the State Employer's Retirement Board, challenged the judgment and the court affirmed. The court ruled that it had inherent power to require payment of those sums necessary to carry out the court system's mandated functions. The court ruled that the retirement package was part of a judge's overall compensation. The court reviewed issues of inflation and the discrepancy between what state court and federal court judges were paid. The court stressed the need for qualified judges. The court ruled that there was no basis that justified treating the judges who took office after March 1, 1974, differently from those who assumed office prior to that date. The court ruled that appellee judges must be given the opportunity to obtain the same retirement options as those available to the other judges.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the grant of summary judgment in favor of appellees, judges who took office after March 1, 1974, and the Pennsylvania Conference of Trial Judges. The court ruled that appellee judges were entitled to the same retirement package options that were enjoyed by judges who took office prior to March 1, 1974. The court ruled that there was no justification for treating appellee judges differently.

 

13 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Klein v. Commonwealth


No. 111 E.D. Appeal Dkt. 1987


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


521 Pa. 330; 555 A.2d 1216; 1989 Pa. LEXIS 72; 10 Employee Benefits Cas. (BNA) 2129


April 11, 1988, Submitted  
March 3, 1989, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Granted May 9, 1989.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal by permission from the Order of the Commonwealth Court of July 27, 1987 at No. 2374 C.D. 1986 affirming the Order of the State Employees' Retirement Board dated January 22, 1986,     Pa.Cmwlth.    , 528 A.2d 1071 (1987)

Appeal from the Order of the Commonwealth Court of July 27, 1987 at No. 101 C.D. 1981 granting preliminary objections and dismissing petition for enforcement,     Pa.Cmwlth.     528 A.2d 1074 (1987) 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant judge sought review of the judgment of the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania), which affirmed an order of the State Employees' Retirement Board denying his request for reinstatement to class E-1 contributor status under the State Employees' Retirement Code of 1959, 71 P. S. §§ 1725-101, 1725-809.


OVERVIEW: Appellant judge sought reinstatement to the retirement status he held during a prior term as judge. The State Employees' Retirement Board denied his request for reinstatement, and the trial court affirmed. Appellant challenged the judgment, contending that the board's refusal to allow him to reenter the retirement system at his prior contributor status was an unconstitutional impairment of his contractual rights. The court agreed with appellant's contention, finding that the legislature was not authorized to compensate judges of the same class/level of court who perform the same functions at different rates of compensation based solely on the dates on which the judges began their respective terms of office. The court ruled that the two-tier retirement compensation system was at-odds with a unified judicial system because the classifications created a disparity in deferred compensation benefits. The court ruled that the system violated equal protection provisions of the state constitution because there was no rational basis for the system since financial expediency was not a sufficient justification. Accordingly, the court reversed the holding of the trial court.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the trial court that found in favor of the State Employees' Retirement Board. The court held that disparate retirement compensation benefits for judges of the same level performing the same duties and exercising the same authority violated equal protection provisions of the state constitution because there was no rational basis for the classification system.

Gondelman v. Commonwealth


No. 49 W.D. Misc. Dkt. 1988, Nos. 97, 98 W.D. App. Dkt. 1988


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


520 Pa. 451; 554 A.2d 896; 1989 Pa. LEXIS 37; 49 Fair Empl. Prac. Cas. (BNA) 408; 50 Empl. Prac. Dec. (CCH) P39,039


December 6, 1988, Argued  
March 1, 1989, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Denied April 18, 1989.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeals from the Order of the Commonwealth Court entered November 3, 1988, at No. 1420 C.D., 1988.

     P.A. Commw.     550 A.2d 814 (1988) 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant judges sought review of the judgment from the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania), which denied appellant's motion for summary judgment in appellant's action seeking a judicial declaration that the compulsory retirement provision of Pa. Const. art. V, § 16(b) was invalid.


OVERVIEW: Appellant judges contended that the compulsory retirement provision of Pa. Const. art. V, § 16(b) was invalid because it amounted to impermissible age discrimination. The lower court denied appellants' motion for summary judgment. Appellants challenged the judgment, contending that the retirement provision violated constitutional equal protection and due process provisions. The court rejected appellants' contentions, finding that the aged were not entitled to special judicial protection because there was no basis to justify heightened scrutiny of the retirement provision. The court ruled that the rational basis test was the appropriate standard concerning classifications based on age because old age was not a discrete and insular group that required extraordinary protection. The court noted that old age marked a stage of life each person reached if they lived out a normal span. The court held that the state constitution did not prohibit the retirement provision because the constitution restrained governmental classifications not classifications used by the people to structure the government. Accordingly, the court dismissed appellants' action.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed appellant judges' action that sought a judicial declaration that the compulsory retirement provision of the state constitution was invalid. The court held that the provision was not a violation of due process or equal protection of the laws because the aged were not a discrete and insular group that required heightened judicial protection.

First Nat'l Bank v. Commonwealth


No. 29 M.D. Appeal Dkt. 1987


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


520 Pa. 244; 553 A.2d 937; 1989 Pa. LEXIS 10


May 11, 1988, Argued  
February 3, 1989, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Commonwealth Court at No. 562 C.D. 1985, entered April 21, 1987, reversing and remanding the Order of the Board of Finance and Revenue at Board Docket No. R-8481, entered January 30, 1985.

     Pa. Commw.    , 520 A.2d 895 (1987) 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant commonwealth sought review from a decision of the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania) that remanded the action to the Department of Revenue for a resettlement of the Single Excise Tax, 72 P.S. § 8301 et seq., against appellee bank. Appellant argued that the appellate court incorrectly gave a certain court decision retroactive effect and that appellee was only entitled to the bank shares taxes paid reduced on a pro rata basis.


OVERVIEW: Appellant commonwealth challenged an appellate court's decision that remanded the action to the Department of Revenue for a resettlement of the Single Excise Tax, 72 P.S. § 8301 et seq., assessed against appellee bank. Appellant contended that the appellate court incorrectly applied a court decision retroactively and alternatively that appellee was only entitled to the shares taxes paid reduced on a pro rata basis, not a complete refund of those taxes. The court affirmed the decision of the appellate court. The court decision in question provided taxpayers subject to the bank shares tax with a legal right to a refund of the taxes erroneously assessed by including United States obligations in their tax base. Appellee had an immediate, legally enforceable right as a result of the court decision in question to a refund of taxes improperly paid pursuant to 72 P.S. § 503(a)(4). The Single Excise Tax was a contradiction of federal law in violation of U.S. Const. amend. VI, cl. 2 because it improperly discriminated against certain taxpayers.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed an appellate court's decision that held appellee bank was entitled to a resettlement of the Single Excise Tax assessed against it. Appellee had an immediate, legally enforceable right as a result of a certain court decision to a refund of erroneously assessed bank shares taxes. The Single Excise Tax violated federal law by improperly discriminating against certain taxpayers.

Commonwealth v. Frey


No. 81-3-393


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


520 Pa. 338; 554 A.2d 27; 1989 Pa. LEXIS 27

February 3, 1989, Decided

PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of Death of the Court of Common Pleas of Lancaster County, entered on March 20, 1981 at No. 169 of 1980.



DISPOSITION: Judgment of sentence affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of the decision of the Court of Common Pleas of Lancaster County (Pennsylvania), which affirmed his conviction for murder in the first degree in connection with the contract murder of his wife. A warrant for his execution was then issued by the governor, following which appellant filed a petition for post-conviction relief. The petition and request for a stay of execution were denied.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted of murder in the first degree in connection with the contract murder of his wife. A verdict of death was returned by the jury pursuant to 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 9711(f). On appeal, the sentence of death was affirmed. Subsequently, a motion for a new trial was denied, and, on appeal, the sentence was again affirmed. A warrant for his execution was then issued by the governor, following which appellant filed a petition for post-conviction relief. The petition and request for a stay of execution were denied by the lower court. The court affirmed the lower court's sentence of death because appellant was not sentenced to death under the Act of 1913, 61 P.S. § 2121 but under 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 9711(f), which was proper. More importantly, it was constitutional. The court further held that the jury instruction regarding mitigating circumstances was proper as the jurors were free to weigh whatever mitigating circumstances they perceived, regardless of whether they agreed that those circumstances were established by the evidence.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's decision, which affirmed appellant accused's sentence of death because appellant was not sentenced to death under the Act of 1913 as he alleged, but under the proper capital punishment statute.

 

17 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Sprague v. Casey


No. 116 E.D. Misc. Dkt. 1988


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


520 Pa. 38; 550 A.2d 184; 1988 Pa. LEXIS 295


September 27, 1988, Argued  
October 25, 1988, Decided



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The petitions for reargument filed in this matter are denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner taxpayer sought summary relief from the Supreme Court of Pennsylvania, and requested that the court exercise its plenary jurisdiction upon petitioner's action for declaratory and injunctive relief challenging the placement on the general election ballot one seat on the Supreme Court and one seat on the Superior Court.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner challenged the placing of one seat on the Supreme Court and one seat on the Superior Court on the general election ballot. The Supreme Court granted petitioner's motion for the exercise of plenary jurisdiction. The court granted petitioner's application for summary relief and directed the removal of the offices of Justice of the Supreme Court and Judge of the Superior Court from the general election ballot. The court held that an election to fill Supreme Court and Superior Court offices may not be placed on the ballot during a general election because Pa. Const. art. V, § 13(a) mandated that all judicial officers were to be elected at the municipal election next preceding the commencement of their respective terms. The court ruled that the governor was required to make an appointment to fill vacancies that occurred during the term of office because the constitutional provision was explicit and comprehensive in its direction as to how the transition in office was to occur in the event of a vacancy. The court noted that the governor's appointments were erroneous because the term of the appointment should have run until the next municipal election.


OUTCOME: The court granted petitioner taxpayer's application for summary relief and directed the removal of the offices of Justice of the Supreme Court and Judge of the Superior Court from the general election ballot because the relevant constitutional provision required the election of those offices at the next municipal election.

Commonwealth v. Reilly


No. 108 E.D. Apl. Dkt. 1987


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


519 Pa. 550; 549 A.2d 503; 1988 Pa. LEXIS 258


January 21, 1988, Argued  
October 17, 1988, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of Superior Court at No. 827 Philadelphia 1986, dated October 23, 1986, affirming the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Bucks County, Criminal Division at Nos. 4584-85/1983, entered February 18, 1986. 361 Pa. Super. 637, 517 A.2d 1366 (1986) 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the order from the Superior Court, affirming the judgment of Sentence from the Court of Common Pleas of Bucks County, Criminal Division (Pennsylvania) finding defendant guilty of murder in the third degree and endangering the welfare of a child. The court granted allocatur to consider the constitutionality of the insanity statute, Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 18, § 315.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was found guilty of murder in connection with the death of her newborn child. On appeal, defendant attacked the constitutionality of the state insanity statute, Pa. Stat. tit. 18, § 315, which placed the burden of proving insanity upon the criminal defendant by a preponderance of the evidence. Defendant contended that Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 18, § 315 violated Pa. Const. art. I, § 9, which assures all persons due process of law. The court disagreed and affirmed. The court held that there was more than sufficient evidence that defendant beat her newborn child to death. With regard to the evidence defendant presented in support of her insanity defense, the court believed a factfinder could reasonably conclude that defendant failed to meet her burden of proof. Neither expert presented by the defense testified that defendant was acting pursuant to a mental defect at the time of the killing; instead, each admitted that defendant's psychosis did not develop until after the homicide. Thus the court as finder of fact could reject the conclusion reached by the psychiatrists that defendant did not know the nature and quality of wrongfulness of her act because of a mental defect.


OUTCOME: The order of the superior court was affirmed. The court held that the commonwealth presented more than sufficient evidence indicating that defendant maliciously beat her newborn child and that these blows caused the death of the child. Further, in support of defendant's insanity defense, the court believed that the factfinder could reasonably conclude that defendant failed to meet her burden of proof.

Commonwealth v. Duffey


No. 105 E.D. Appeal Dkt. 1986


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


519 Pa. 348; 548 A.2d 1178; 1988 Pa. LEXIS 252


January 19, 1988, Argued  
October 14, 1988, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence order by the Court of Common Pleas of Lackawanna County entered May 22, 1986 at No. 84 Criminal 176.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from the judgment from the Court of Common Pleas of Lackawanna County (Pennsylvania), which convicted him of first-degree murder and robbery.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of first-degree murder and robbery in connection with the stabbing death of a 20 year-old woman. On appeal, defendant made several arguments, including the trial court's error in allowing the testimony of a state trooper regarding the theory of high-speed blood splatter, and in not granting a mistrial during prosecution's opening argument because of the audible scream and crying by the murder victim's mother. The court affirmed both convictions and the death sentence. The court held that although the trial court erred in allowing a state trooper to testify when he was not properly qualified as an expert witness, it was harmless error. The noted that there was uncontradicted evidence establishing defendant's intent to kill the victim. Also, the audible scream and crying by murder victim's mother, during prosecutor's opening argument recital of number of times victim had been stabbed, did not require mistrial where trial judge gave general cautionary instruction to jury and, after polling them individually in chambers, determined that they had been unswayed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for first-degree murder and robbery. However, the court held that the trial judge erred in allowing a state trooper to testify regarding the theory of high-speed blood splatter. The court determined that the admission of the expert testimony constituted harmless error, noting that uncontradicted evidence clearly established defendant's intent to kill the victim.

Maunus v. Commonwealth


No. 38 M.D. Appeal Dkt. 1987


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


518 Pa. 592; 544 A.2d 1324; 1988 Pa. LEXIS 226


April 11, 1988, Argued  
August 10, 1988, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania dated September 12, 1986 at No. 277 C.D. 1985, reversing the opinions of the State Ethics Commission, 84-020-A and 84-020-B.

100 Pa. Cmwlth. Ct. 494, 515 A.2d 83 (1986) 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant State Ethics Commission challenged an order of the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania) reversing its opinions that appellee attorneys were required to file a Statement of Financial Interest pursuant to the State Ethics Act (Act), 65 P.S. § 401 et seq. Appellant contended the court erred in finding that the Act's financial reporting and disclosure requirements were inapplicable to publicly employed attorneys.


OVERVIEW: Appellee attorneys were employed by the state liquor control board. Both were advised that they were required to file a Statement of Financial Interest pursuant to the State Ethics Act (Act), 65 P.S. § 401 et seq. Appellees refused to comply based on their belief that the ethical conduct of attorneys was governed solely by the Supreme Court. After a hearing, appellant State Ethics Commission concluded that appellees were not exempt from the Act's financial reporting and disclosure requirements. Thereafter, appellees sought review in the courts, and the trial court reversed. On review, appellant contended the trial court erred in finding that the Act was inapplicable to publicly employed attorneys. The appeals court reversed the trial court order and reinstated appellant's opinions. The court ruled that publicly employed attorneys were subject to the reasonable regulation of their governmental employer as long as it did not conflict with the authority of the state Supreme Court in regulating attorney conduct. Because the financial disclosure requirement imposed by the Act was not incompatible with any of the rules applicable to attorneys in the Commonwealth, it was valid.


OUTCOME: The appeals court reversed the trial court order and reinstated appellant State Ethics Commission's opinions requiring appellees to file a financial disclosure statement in accordance with the Ethics Act. The court ruled that publicly employed attorneys were not exempt from the Act's financial reporting and disclosure requirements because the requirements were compatible with the rules applicable to attorneys.

Bensalem Township Sch. Dist. v. Commonwealth


No. 70 E.D. Appeal Dkt. 1987


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


518 Pa. 581; 544 A.2d 1318; 1988 Pa. LEXIS 211


December 11, 1987, Argued  
July 29, 1988, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Commonwealth Court entered April 22, 1987 at No. 1580 C.D. 1984

 105 Pa.Cmwlth 388, 524 A.2d 1027 (1987) 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants challenged an order of the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania) entering summary judgment in favor of appellee state, treasurer, and secretary of education in an action challenging the statutory scheme for the funding of public schools as unconstitutional. Appellants argued the court improperly granted summary judgment in favor of a non-moving party.


OVERVIEW: Appellants filed suit against appellee state, treasurer, and secretary of education for declaratory and equitable relief seeking to have the statutory scheme for the funding of Commonwealth public schools declared unconstitutional. Appellants later filed a motion for summary judgment, asserting that the statute was unconstitutional on its face. There were no supporting affidavits or depositions filed with the motion, except for an affidavit from appellants' counsel in which he made various allegations of proof but offered no concrete support. Appellees resisted appellants' motion, but did not file their own motion. Nevertheless, the trial court denied appellants' motion, and then entered summary judgment for appellees. On review, appellants argued that the trial court improperly granted summary judgment in favor of a non-moving party. The appeals court reversed and remanded. The court reasoned that Pa. R. Civ. P. 1035 did not contain any provision allowing a trial court to grant summary judgment in favor of a non-moving party. The court noted that in a motion for summary judgment only the moving party can prevail, because if he fails, a dispute continues on the facts themselves.


OUTCOME: The appeals court reversed the grant of summary judgment in favor of appellee state, treasurer, and secretary of education and remanded for further proceedings. The court ruled that the trial court was not authorized under the rules of civil procedure to grant summary judgment in favor of a non-moving party.

Leventhal v. Philadelphia


No. 3 E.D. Misc. Docket 1988


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


518 Pa. 233; 542 A.2d 1328; 1988 Pa. LEXIS 170


January 21, 1988, Argued  
May 24, 1988, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County, Trial Division, Civil Section, Dated December 14, 1987, at No. 1849 June Term, 1987.
Leventhal v. Philadelphia, 517 Pa. 324, 536 A.2d 339, 1988 Pa. LEXIS 22 (1988)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff, a hotel owner, appealed from an order of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania), which upheld the constitutionality of the Convention Center Authority Act of 1986, 53 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 16201, et seq., under both the Pennsylvania and the United States Constitutions.


OVERVIEW: Pursuant to the Convention Center Authority Act (Act), 53 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 16201, et seq., a city council enacted a hotel room rental tax (tax). The tax required all hotel operators to collect the tax from its patrons and pay the tax to the city. The tax was earmarked for convention center purposes. Plaintiff, a hotel owner in the city, challenged the tax. The court affirmed the decision upholding the constitutionality of the Act and the tax. The tax and Act did not violate the Equal Protection Clause or Due Process Clauses of the United States Constitution and did not violate the Uniformity Clause of the Pennsylvania Constitution because the benefit received and the burden imposed were proportionate. Plaintiff failed to show that the tax or convention center would hurt its hotel business. Plaintiff could not say that its business was hurt. Nor could plaintiff support its claim that it would not benefit from the convention center. In fact, evidence showed that hotels were likely to profit from the convention center. The Act did not violate the Special Law Clause, Pa. Const. art. 3, § 32.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's decision upholding the constitutionality of the Convention Center Authority Act (Act) because plaintiff, a hotel owner, failed to show that the benefit received and the burden imposed was palpably disproportionate. The evidence showed that plaintiff was not hurt by the Act and the hotel room tax, and in fact, benefitted from the convention center paid for by the tax.

Commonwealth v. Larkin


No. 127 E.D. Appeal Docket 1986


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


518 Pa. 225; 542 A.2d 1324; 1988 Pa. LEXIS 160


November 12, 1987, Submitted  
May 20, 1988, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of the Superior Court Entered on November 29, 1985 at No. 01754 Philadelphia, 1984, Affirming the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Bucks County, Pennsylvania, Criminal Division, Entered on June 7, 1984, at No. 3645 of 1983.

   Pa. Superior Ct.    ,    A.2d     (198 )



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the decision of the Superior Court (Pennsylvania), which affirmed his sentence for robbery, theft, receiving stolen property, assault, reckless endangerment, and carrying a firearm without a license. Defendant argued that 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 9712, the Mandatory Sentencing Statute, was unconstitutional and improperly applied where a defendant was found guilty but mentally ill under 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 314.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of robbery, theft by unlawful taking, receiving stolen property, simple assault, recklessly endangering another person, possessing an instrument of crime, and carrying a firearm without a license in violation of 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. §§ 3921, 3925, 2701, 2705, 907, 6106, and 314. He was convicted by a jury that found him to be guilty, but mentally ill. The trial court sentenced him to 5 to 10 years in prison under the Mandatory Sentencing Statute, 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 9712, because he was in visible possession of a firearm while committing robbery. The lower court affirmed. On appeal, the court held that the Mandatory Minimum Sentencing Statute was not unconstitutional and that the sentence was not inconsistent with the finding of guilty but mentally ill because the sentencing statute applied "notwithstanding any other provision of this title or other statute to the contrary." The court held that the sentencing statute was unambiguous and could not be construed any other way. Therefore, the court affirmed the sentence as imposed by the trial court and as affirmed by the lower court.


OUTCOME: The court concluded that both the trial court and the lower court were correct in concluding that the finding that defendant was guilty but mentally ill did not permit a reduction in the mandatory minimum sentence of five years for commission of a robbery while in visible possession of a firearm. Therefore, the court affirmed defendant's conviction and sentence.

In re Seegrist


No. 23 W.D. Appeal Docket 1986


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


517 Pa. 568; 539 A.2d 799; 1988 Pa. LEXIS 99; 15 Media L. Rep. 1329


March 10, 1987, Argued  
March 30, 1988, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County, Pennsylvania, Criminal Division, Entered March 13, 1986, at No. Misc. 404 November, 1985.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant mental patient challenged the order from the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County, Pennsylvania, which refused the exclude a reporter from appellee newspaper from an informal conference conducted pursuant to 50 P.S. § 7303 of the Mental Health Procedures Act. The trial court also applied 50 P.S. § 7304(e)(4) and found the conference to be unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Appellant mental patient was accused of shooting several persons. A petition for involuntary treatment was filed pursuant to 50 P.S. § 7303 of the Mental Health Procedures Act. At the hearing to determine whether appellant should be confined pursuant to that section, appellant sought a private hearing pursuant to 50 P.S. § 7304(e)(4). Appellee newspaper's reporter objected. The trial court agreed to keep the hearing public but found that section 7304(e)(4) was unconstitutional, and committed appellant. The two statutory sections covered different proceedings. Section 7303 hearings were for a 20-day commitment and were fairly informal. Section § 7304(e)(4) covered a 90-day commitment and required greater formality. The hearing below was a section 7303 hearing and section 7304(e)(4) had no applicability. Appellant was not subjected to the deprivation of a constitutional right to a private hearing. No information was released at the hearing that was of a confidential nature. The appellate court affirmed the opening of the hearing to the public but rejected the conclusion that section 7304(e)(4) was unconstitutional or applied to a section 7303 hearing.


OUTCOME: Order that a hearing concerning the involuntary commitment of appellant mental patient would remain public following appellee newspaper's objection to a private hearing was affirmed. There was nothing of a confidential nature revealed at the hearing that would have triggered a privacy right but the trial court's application of a separate statute covering 90-day commitments and its finding that that statute was unconstitutional was in error.

Lewis v. School Dist.

November 9, 1987, Argued  
March 1, 1988, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Judgment of the Superior Court entered October 25, 1985, at No. 1271 Philadelphia 1984, affirming the order of Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County entered April 26, 1984 at No. 3597 of March Term 1984.

 347 Pa. Super. 32, 500 A.2d 141 (1985).

Appeal from the Judgment of the Superior Court entered November 15, 1985 at No. 3386 Philadelphia 1984, affirming order of Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County entered November 21, 1984 at No. 3082 of September Term 1984.

 352 Pa. Super. 622,     A.2d     (1985).

Appeal from the Judgment of the Superior Court entered July 30, 1986 at No. 36 Philadelphia 1986, affirming order of Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County entered November 21, 1984 at No. 1778 of April Term 1985.

 358 Pa. Super. 624,     A.2d     (1986).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, employees and insurer, sought review of judgments of the Superior Court (Pennsylvania), which held that the Workmen's Compensation Act, 77 P.S. § 1 et seq., immunized appellee employers from appellants' claims for uninsured motorist benefits. Appellant employees each brought actions at law for damages, based on the Uninsured Motorist Act, 40 P.S. § 2000, for personal injuries sustained in vehicle accidents with uninsured motorists.


OVERVIEW: Appellant employees sought to recover damages from appellees employers based on the Uninsured Motorist Act, 40 P.S. § 2000, resulting from personal injuries sustained in work-related automobile accidents with uninsured drivers. The lower court found in favor of appellees because they were immunized from appellants' claims for uninsured motorist benefits under the Workmen's' Compensation Act. 77 P.S. § 1 et seq. On appeal, the court found that the Uninsured Motorist Act required that automobile insurance policies covering personal-injury liability also provide protection against uninsured motorists. However, the court also found that the availability of this protection to employees of the owner of the insured vehicle implicated the Workmen's Compensation Act, which stated that it is the exclusive source of an employer's liability to an employee for work-related injury. The court held that the dual capacity exception, in which an employer could have a dual capacity with respect to its employee in such circumstances in which the employer's relationship to the injury went beyond that of mere employer, did not apply. Accordingly, the court affirmed the judgment of the lower court.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgments of the lower court in favor of appellee employers in the actions at law brought by appellants, employees and insurer, for damages based on the Uninsured Motorist Act for personal injuries sustained in vehicle accidents with uninsured motorists. The court held that appellees were immunized from appellants' claims for uninsured motorist benefits by the Workmen's Compensation Act.

 

29 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Heck


No. 16 E.D. Appeal Docket 1986


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


517 Pa. 192; 535 A.2d 575; 1987 Pa. LEXIS 872


October 20, 1986, Argued  
December 28, 1987, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Judgment of the Superior Court of Pennsylvania, No. 1457 Philadelphia 1983, Reversing the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Lancaster County, Pennsylvania, Entered on May 9, 1983, on No. 1574 of 1982.

 341 Pa. Super. 183, 491 A.2d 212 (1983) 



DISPOSITION: Accordingly, the Appellee's conviction in the instant case may be sustained only if the Commonwealth's evidence established that he acted recklessly, or criminally negligently. The Commonwealth has not attempted to argue that the conviction should be upheld because the evidence was sufficient to establish even criminal negligence, the minimal level of culpability required. Indeed, it could not do so based upon this record. The order of the Superior Court reversing the judgment of sentence and discharging the Appellee is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Commonwealth appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of Pennsylvania, reversing the trial court's judgment that entered defendant's conviction for vehicular homicide.


OVERVIEW: Defendant failed to properly yield when making a left turn, in violation of 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 3322, and the other driver died as a result of the collision. Defendant was convicted of homicide by vehicle, 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. 3732, and sentenced to a one-year probation and a fine. On appeal, the superior court concluded that the evidence was sufficient to support the conviction, but reversed the conviction, reasoning that it deprived defendant of his U.S. Const. amend. IV due process rights by imposing criminal liability upon a showing of ordinary negligence. On appeal, the court rejected the superior court's analysis of the due process issue, but affirmed the superior court order reversing the conviction, on other grounds. The court held that ordinary negligence would not sustain a conviction for the offense of homicide by vehicle. The phrase "negligently" in 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 302(b)(4) applied and meant criminal negligence and did not encompass the tort liability concept of negligence. Accordingly, the court affirmed the judgment of the superior court.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the superior court that found that reversed the judgment of the trial court and discharged defendant. The court held that although the superior court's analysis regarding the constitutionality of the vehicular homicide statute was incorrect, the evidence only showed ordinary negligence, and this could not support the conviction under the proper interpretation of the statute.

Commonwealth v. Wysocki


No. 104 E.D. Appeal Dkt. 1986


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


517 Pa. 175; 535 A.2d 77; 1987 Pa. LEXIS 876


May 15, 1987, Argued  
December 28, 1987, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Order of Commonwealth Court entered august 9, 1985, at No. 235 C.D. 1984, affirming Order of Court of Common Pleas of Northampton County entered January 12, 1984, at No. 1983-C-7720.

 91 Pa. Commw. 42, 496 A.2d 897 (1985) 



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant driver challenged the order of the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania), which affirmed the order of the lower trial court, which affirmed the decision by appellee Pennsylvania Department of Transportation to suspend appellant's operator's license pursuant to 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 1547 for failing to submit to a breathalyzer test following appellant's arrest for driving while intoxicated.


OVERVIEW: Appellant driver was stopped at a roadblock. The trooper who stopped appellant noticed the odor of alcohol, and appellant failed two sobriety tests. Defendant was arrested and taken to the police station where he refused to take a breathalyzer test. Criminal charges were dismissed, but appellee Pennsylvania Department of Transportation suspended appellant's operator's license, pursuant to 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 1547. Both the lower trial court and the trial court affirmed. The appellate court affirmed. Permission to operate a vehicle was subject to certain conditions, including the implied consent to an alcohol test under Pa. Cons. Stat. § 1547(a). Seventy-five Pa. Cons. Stat. § 1547(b) clearly stated that a license could be submitted for failure to submit to the test. Whether the roadblock was unconstitutional was not an issue because the suspension was for the refusal to take the test, not because appellant was arrested for driving while intoxicated. The legality of the arrest was immaterial. Seventy-five Pa. Cons. Stat. § 1547(a) also required reasonable grounds to believe a person was under the influence, which was established by appellant's actions at the roadblock.


OUTCOME: The affirmation of appellant driver's license suspension by appellee Pennsylvania Department of Transportation was affirmed. Whether a police roadblock was constitutional was immaterial because the license was suspended for failing to submit to a breathalyzer test, not for driving under the influence. The police had reasonable grounds to believe appellant was intoxicated by the odor of alcohol and his failing of sobriety tests.

Related proceeding at, Writ of mandamus granted Pennsylvania State Ass'n of County Comm'rs v. Commonwealth, 545 Pa. 324, 681 A.2d 699, 1996 Pa. LEXIS 1487 (1996)

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant county sought review of the decision from the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania), which sustained a demurrer on the grounds that appellant's application for declaratory judgment that it was not obligated by statute to fund its common pleas court presented no justiciable controversy and the lower court was without power to fashion that remedy.

Barasch v. Pa. Public Util. Com


Nos. 33 W.D. Appeal Docket, 1986, 34 W.D. Appeal Docket, 1986


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


516 Pa. 142; 532 A.2d 325; 1987 Pa. LEXIS 791


March 11, 1987, Argued  
October 15, 1987, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Order of the Commonwealth Court dated June 13, 1985 at No. 553 C.D. 1983, affirming the decision of Pennsylvania Public Utility Commission entered January 28, 1983 at R-821945.

Appeal from Order of the Commonwealth Court dated June 13, 1985 at No. 1364 C.D. 1984, affirming the decision of Pennsylvania Public Utility Commission entered April 11, 1984 at R-832409.

 90 Pa. Cmwlth. 98, 494 A.2d 58 (1985).



DISPOSITION: REVERSED and REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant consumer advocates sought review of the judgment of the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania) affirming the decision of appellee Pennsylvania Public Utility Commission, which permitted a public utility to include in its reported operating expenses a portion of the construction costs of four cancelled electric plants.


OVERVIEW: The Pennsylvania Public Utility Code was modified to add a section, 66 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 1315, which prohibited making the cost of construction or expansion of an electric public utility facility until such time as the facility is used and useful in service to the public. Appellant consumer advocates appealed the judgment of the appellate court which affirmed the decision of appellee Pennsylvania Public Utility Commission permitting a public utility to include in its reported operating expenses a portion of the construction costs of four cancelled electric plants. On appeal, the court reversed the judgment of the trial court, holding that § 1315 must be read as prohibiting an electric utility from recovering the costs of cancelled plants from ratepayers, either by making such costs part of its rate base or by converting them into operating expenses through amortization. The court further held that to reach any other conclusion, it would have to treat as surplusage that part of the section's proscription which says: "nor otherwise included in the rates charged by the electric utility."


OUTCOME: The court reversed the appellate court judgment in favor of appellee Pennsylvania Public Utility Commission, holding that the newly enacted provision of the Public Utility Code must be read as prohibiting an electric utility from recovering the costs of cancelled plants from ratepayers.

Commonwealth v. Samuels


Nos. 156 E.D. Appeal Docket 1986, 157 E.D. Appeal Docket 1986


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


516 Pa. 300; 532 A.2d 404; 1987 Pa. LEXIS 795


April 9, 1987, Argued  
October 15, 1987, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Superior Court of Pennsylvania, No. 02657 Philadelphia 1984, dated April 22, 1986, Reversing the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Bucks County, Pennsylvania, Criminal Division, No. 3261 of 1983, dated August 29, 1984.

 354 Pa. Super. 128 511 A.2d 221 (1986) 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The prosecution sought review of an order from the Superior Court of Philadelphia (Pennsylvania), which reversed a judgment of sentence for statutory rape, indecent assault, indecent exposure, and corruption of a minor.


OVERVIEW: In a prosecution for statutory rape, indecent assault, indecent exposure, and corruption of a minor, defendant was convicted and a judgment of sentence was entered against him. The superior court vacated the judgment of sentence and remanded for resentencing on the ground that the state commission on sentencing exceeded its statutory grant of authority by adopting guidelines that increased the severity of sentences on the basis of prior conviction misdemeanors that did not involve deadly weapons. On review the court agreed with the prosecution that the legislation insisted that the commission treat a person with prior weapons misdemeanors more severely, but left it to the commission, in the extent of its broad grant of general authority, to determine what other conditions, including non-weapons misdemeanors, might have also justified increased severity. The court held that, whether 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 2154 was strictly or liberally construed, it in no way followed that the mandatory term that "shall" was also an exclusive. Accordingly, the order of the superior court was vacated and the judgment of sentence was reinstated.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the superior court's order and the judgment of sentence was reinstated because the state commission on sentencing had broad discretion in determining what conditions would warrant a more severe sentence with respect to defendant's convictions for statutory rape, indecent assault, indecent exposure, and corruption of a minor.

Fawber v. Cohen


No. 45 M.D. Appeal Dkt. 1986


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


516 Pa. 352; 532 A.2d 429; 1987 Pa. LEXIS 807


May 15, 1987, Argued  
October 15, 1987, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Commonwealth Court at No. 579 C.D. 1984, 91 Pa. Commonwealth Ct. 559, 497 A.2d 697 (1985).



DISPOSITION: The order of Commonwealth Court transferring this suit to Cumberland County Common Pleas is reversed and the record remanded to Commonwealth Court for further proceedings consistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant Secretary of the Department of Public Welfare challenged the decision from the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania), which held that it lacked jurisdiction to hear appellee class' action under 42 U.S.C.S. 1983. Appellant contended that 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 761(a)(1)(v) did not preclude the trial court from asserting jurisdiction over appellee's action for injunctive and declaratory relief.


OVERVIEW: Appellee class brought an action on behalf of county residents who had participated in the Community Work Experience Program (CWEP). Three of appellee's four claims were brought under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983. Appellees and appellant Secretary of the Department of Public Welfare filed cross motions for summary judgment. The trial court held that, since the action was largely based on 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983, it lacked original jurisdiction to hear the case and vacated its order to certify the class. Appellant was permitted to seek immediate review of the interlocutory transfer order. Both parties contended that the case should remain with the trial court. The court held that, as long as appellant secretary was a party to the suit, the trial court would normally have jurisdiction under 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 761(a). The trial court found that the § 1983 actions were in the nature of a trespass, therefore falling under the exception of 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 761(a)(1)(v). However, the court held that appellee's action was for injunctive and declaratory relief, which excluded it from § 761(A)(1)(v).


OUTCOME: The trial court's judgment that it lacked the jurisdiction to hear appellee class' action against appellant Secretary of the Department of Public Welfare was reversed. The court held that the trial court was the proper forum for the case where appellees were seeking injunctive and declaratory relief rather than pursuing a claim in the nature of a trespass action, i.e., for legal relief.

Commonwealth v. Sessoms


No. 80 E.D. Appeal Docket 1986


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


516 Pa. 365; 532 A.2d 775; 1987 Pa. LEXIS 770


December 1, 1986, Argued  
October 7, 1987, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Judgment of the Superior Court of Pennsylvania, dated November 15, 1985, as of No. 1999 Philadelphia 1983, affirming the Judgment of sentence of the Honorable Charles F. Greevy, Senior Judge, specially assigned to the Court of Common Pleas of Montgomery County, dated July 6, 1983, as of No. 3340-82.

 352 Pa. Super. 619, 505 A.2d 1036 (1985) 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of Pennsylvania, which affirmed the sentence imposed by the trial court for an assault charge and a weapons charge.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was sentenced for an assault charge and a weapons charge. On appeal, appellant contended that the method by which the sentencing guidelines, 204 Pa. Code Ch. 303, were established violated either or both constitutional requirements of bicameral consideration and presentment for gubernatorial approval. The court determined that the Pennsylvania Commission on Sentencing was not an administrative agency, that is, an agency charged with administering the laws. The court also found that the commission's guidelines could not, without more, be given the effect of law, either as legislation or regulation, so as to by themselves alter the legal rights and duties of the defendant, the prosecutor, and the sentencing court. The court held that because the guidelines were not adopted according to the proper scheme, however, but were the product of a rejection resolution that was not presented to the governor in violation of Pa. Const. art. III, § 9, they had to be declared to be of no force at all. Accordingly, the court vacated appellant's sentence and remanded for resentencing pursuant to the remaining valid provisions of the sentencing code.


OUTCOME: The court vacated appellant's sentence and remanded the case for resentencing pursuant to the remaining valid provisions of the sentencing code. The court held that because the sentencing guidelines were not adopted according to the proper scheme, but were the product of a rejection resolution that was not presented to the governor in violation of the state constitution, they had no force.
