First Nat'l Bank v. Flanagan


No. 69 Western District Appeal Docket, 1986


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


515 Pa. 263; 528 A.2d 134; 1987 Pa. LEXIS 739


March 10, 1987, Submitted  
July 2, 1987, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Judgment Entered June 4, 1986, in the Court of Common Pleas of Erie County, Civil Division, at No. 812-A-1982.



DISPOSITION: Accordingly, the order of the Court of Common Pleas of Erie County is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant guarantors sought review of a judgment from the Court of Common Pleas of Erie County (Pennsylvania), which granted judgment against appellants for principal plus interest due on a business loan guarantee both appellants had executed. The trial court held unconstitutional a legislative attempt to give retroactive effect to an amendment to the disclosure provisions in the Loan Law, 41 P.S. § 101.


OVERVIEW: Appellee bank filed suit against appellant guarantors, seeking payment on the agreement of guarantee of a business loan they both signed. Appellants disputed liability and argued that appellee did not comply with the disclosure laws on loans secured by residential real estate, 41 P.S. § 401 (1986). Specifically, appellants cited appellee's failure to inform them that they could lose their home if they did not repay the loan. The trial court granted appellee's motion for summary judgment and held that the legislature's attempt to apply the disclosure requirements retroactively to a business loan secured by residential real estate violated the contracts clauses of Pennsylvania and United States constitutions, U.S. Const. art. I, § 10 and Pa. Const. art. I, § 17. On appeal, the court held that, if applied to this transaction, the amendment to the law would void the agreement of guarantee and change the intention of the parties and the bargained-for consideration. Because this would violate the contract clause, it was unconstitutional. Therefore, the court affirmed the trial court's judgment.


OUTCOME: The court held that retroactive application of the amendment, which restored disclosure requirements in the Loan Law, to appellee bank's agreement of guarantee of a business loan secured by residential real estate would violate the contract clause of state and federal constitutions. Accordingly, the trial court's judgment against appellant guarantors was affirmed.

Pivirotto v. Pittsburgh


No. 71 W.D. Appeal Docket, 1986


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


515 Pa. 246; 528 A.2d 125; 1987 Pa. LEXIS 736


March 9, 1987, Argued  
June 22, 1987, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of Commonwealth Court dated December 19, 1985, at No. 445 C.D. 1984, affirming the Order of the Court of Common Pleas, Allegheny County, Civil Division dated January 31, 1984 at No. GD 80-14421

 93 Pa.Commw. 563, 502 A.2d 747 (1985) 



DISPOSITION: The order of Commonwealth Court is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city sought review of the decision of the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania), which affirmed a trial court judgment for negligent demolition in favor of appellee building owner.


OVERVIEW: Appellant city challenged the decision of an appellate court which affirmed a trial court judgment for negligent demolition in favor of appellee building owner. Appellant ordered demolition of a property sold to appellee at a tax sale, but failed to notify appellant. Appellee argued that as the successful bidder at the tax sale, he had an ownership interest, to wit equitable title, and, thus, had a legally protected property interest which entitled him to actual notice of condemnation and demolition. The court agreed, holding that appellee was the equitable owner of the property. The court evaluated appellant's notification efforts and found them insufficient. Appellee was required to expend reasonable efforts to ascertain the identity of the purchaser of the property at the tax sale. The record demonstrated clearly that the office of the city treasurer maintained a list of all properties sold at tax sales and actually forwarded a list of properties sold to the agency responsible for condemnation. Appellant failed to investigate whether the status or ownership of the property changed despite having the information readily available. The court affirmed the judgment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the appellate court decision, which affirmed the negligent demolition judgment entered against appellant city and in favor of appellee building owner. Appellee, as a tax sale purchaser, was equitable owner of the property and was entitled to actual notice of demolition. Appellant had ready access to the fact that the property was sold, but failed to investigate whether a new party required notice.

In re Glancey


Nos. 116, 118


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


515 Pa. 201; 527 A.2d 997; 1987 Pa. LEXIS 735


April 8, 1987, Argued  
June 17, 1987, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Judicial Inquiry and Review Board, Docket No. 1



DISPOSITION: Accordingly, failure of the Respondents to comply within the specified period will result in our adoption, without further notice, of the Board's recommendation that they be removed from their judicial offices. If response is made within the specified period, the instant charges will be dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent judges sought review of an order entered by petitioner, the Judicial Inquiry and Review Board (Pennsylvania), that recommended respondents be removed from their judicial offices for failing to make financial disclosures mandated by Pennsylvania Supreme Court Order No. 47 which also resulted in violations of 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 3302, Model Code of Judicial Conduct Canons 1 and 2, and Pa. Const. art. V, § 17(b).


OVERVIEW: Respondent judges challenged petitioner Judicial Inquiry and Review Board's order that recommended respondents' removal from their judicial offices for failing to make financial disclosures mandated by Pennsylvania Supreme Court Order No. 47. Petitioner held that such failure resulted in violations of 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 3302, Model Code of Judicial Conduct Canons 1 and 2, and Pa. Const. art. V, § 17(b). Respondents argued that ordered financial disclosures violated their Fifth Amendment, U.S. Const. amend. V, rights against self-incrimination. The court adopted petitioner's recommendation. The Fifth Amendment privilege was not an acceptable answer to any question on the Statement of Financial Interest required by Order No. 47. Order No. 47 was designed to preserve the integrity and impartiality of the judicial system. Requiring disclosure of certain valuable gifts was designed to assure citizens of the impartiality and honesty of public officials and to promote public confidence. Further, removal of respondents from office after they invoked the privilege against self-incrimination did not amount to an inference that respondents were guilty of receiving improper gifts.


OUTCOME: The court adopted petitioner Judicial Inquiry and Review Board's recommendation that respondent judges be removed from their judicial offices for nondisclosure of court ordered financial disclosures. The Fifth Amendment's self-incrimination privilege did not bar removal of respondents from their judicial offices. The ordered disclosure was an attempt to promote public confidence and show judicial officers' honesty and impartiality.

Mascaro v. Youth Study Center


No. 2 E.D. Appeal Docket 1986


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


514 Pa. 351; 523 A.2d 1118; 1987 Pa. LEXIS 679


October 20, 1986, Argued  
April 7, 1987, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Commonwealth Court Entered May 22, 1985, at No. 21T.D. 1884, Reversing, as to the City of Philadelphia and its Youth Study Center, the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County, Entered on January 18, 1984, at No. 6068, April Term, 1983.

 89 Pa. Commonwealth Ct. 388, 492 A.2d 786 (1985) 



DISPOSITION: The Order of Commonwealth Court is reversed, and the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County is reinstated.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In appellee crime victims' negligence action, appellant city sought review of an order of the Pennsylvania Commonwealth Court, which reversed an order of the trial court granting appellant a judgment on the pleadings.


OVERVIEW: Appellee crime victims were injured by an intruder who had escaped from appellant city's detention center for juvenile criminal offenders. Appellees alleged that appellant negligently maintained the real estate, thereby facilitating the escape. The trial court granted appellant judgment on the pleadings but the Commonwealth Court reversed, holding that appellees' complaint stated a cause of action against appellant under an exception to governmental immunity covering the care, custody, and control of real estate by a local agency. The Supreme Court held that as a precondition to maintaining an action against appellant, appellees had to satisfy the three statutory requirements and found that appellees stated a cause of action which could be maintained at common law against one not having an immunity defense, and thus satisfied the first requirement. The court further held that appellant's negligence was sufficiently implicated in appellees' injuries so as to meet the second requirement. The court finally held, however, that appellees had not met the third requirement and that appellant was not liable for the criminal acts of others.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the Commonwealth Court and reinstated the trial court's order on the basis that appellant city could not be held liable for the acts of criminals who took advantage of defects in municipal property to commit their own crimes.

Hughes v. Commonwealth Dep't of Transp.


Nos. 79 W.D. Appeal Dkt. 1985, 80 W.D. Appeal Dkt. 1985, 81 W.D. Appeal Dkt. 1985

514 Pa. 300; 523 A.2d 747; 1987 Pa. LEXIS 655


September 15, 1986, Argued  
April 6, 1987, Decided

PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Judgment entered May 17, 1985 of the Court of Common Pleas of Somerset County, Civil Division, at No. 593 Civil 1981.

Appeal from the Judgment entered May 17, 1985 of the Court of Common Pleas of Somerset County, Civil Division, at No. 260 Civil 1982.

Appeal from the Judgment entered May 17, 1985 of the Court of Common Pleas of Somerset County, Civil Division, at No. 261 Civil 1982.



DISPOSITION: Reversed in part and affirmed in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant department of transportation challenged the decision of the Court of Common Pleas of Somerset County, Civil Division (Pennsylvania), which entered an order declaring the condemnation of appellees landowners' land and found, in response to appellant's refusal to pay delay damages, that 26 Pa. Stat. Ann. §1-611 was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Appellant department of transportation filed a declaration of the taking of appellees landowners' property. Estimated just compensation was offered to each appellee. After the condemnation appellees were unable to make use of their land. The trial court awarded compensation and ordered appellant to pay delay damages. In post-trial motions, appellant asserted 26 Pa. Stat. § 1-611. The trial court determined that such statute was unconstitutional because it did not provide compensation for the diminished use of appellees' land. The court held that 26 Pa. Stat. § 611 did pass constitutional muster in determining when delay damages go into effect. The record established that appellant's declaration of taking, in conjunction with its subsequent actions, infringed on landowner's full and normal use of their property, and that delay damages were properly awarded. Furthermore, the trial court acted appropriately in refusing to impose a six percent interest rate, and choosing instead the prevailing commercial loan rate. Accordingly, the court reversed the lower court's decision in part and affirmed part of its decision.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the portion of the lower court's decision that declared an eminent domain statute unconstitutional, but affirmed the portion of the judgment that awarded appellee landowners' delay damages and imposed the prevailing commercial loan rate of interest on the damages, because such an award was supported by the record.

Commonwealth v. Nelson


No. 43 W.D. Appeal Docket 1984


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


514 Pa. 262; 523 A.2d 728; 1987 Pa. LEXIS 658


September 15, 1986, Argued  
April 3, 1987, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Denied June 23, 1987.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Judgment of Sentence of Court of Common Pleas of Erie County, Criminal Division, entered May 7, 1984 at No. 559 A&B of 1983.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: By way of a direct appeal pursuant 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 722(4), defendant sought review of the judgment of sentence entered by the Court of Common Pleas of Erie County (Pennsylvania) after a jury found defendant guilty of murder in the first degree and possession of an instrument of crime and determined that the death penalty was appropriate.


OVERVIEW: A jury convicted defendant of murder in the first degree and possession of an instrument of crime. The jury also decided that imposition of the death sentence was appropriate, having found the aggravating circumstance of torture. The trial court formally imposed the death sentence. The court affirmed the conviction, concluding that the evidence was sufficient to support the verdict, as defendant admitted to several people that he killed his wife, he confessed to the killing after being advised of his rights, and the circumstances of the crime established that he acted with the specific intent to kill. The court also found that the trial court did not prejudice defendant by refusing to grant defense counsel a recess prior to calling defendant to the stand. Further, the court found that defendant failed to establish that counsel was ineffective prior to trial or during the guilt stage. The court vacated the death sentence, holding that counsel was ineffective for failing to object to the trial court's jury instruction on the aggravating circumstance of torture because the instruction failed to provide any definition of the term torture and was therefore prejudicially deficient.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed in part the judgment of sentence entered by the trial court because the evidence was sufficient to support defendant's convictions for first degree murder and possession of an instrument of crime and counsel was not ineffective prior to or at trial. The court reversed the sentence of death and remanded to the trial court for imposition of a life sentence because counsel was ineffective during the punishment phase.

 

43 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Replogle v. Commonwealth


No. 18 M.D. Appeal Docket, 1986


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


514 Pa. 209; 523 A.2d 327; 1987 Pa. LEXIS 649


January 29, 1987, Submitted  
March 30, 1987, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania, No. 2467 C.D. 1985.
Replogle v. Commonwealth, Pennsylvania Liquor Control Bd., 95 Pa. Commw. 614, 506 A.2d 499, 1986 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 2008 (1986)



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant licensee sought review of a decision of the Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania, which dismissed appellant's suit against appellee liquor control commission challenging the constitutional validity of the local option provision embodied in 47 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 4-472.


OVERVIEW: Appellant licensee operated a bar under a liquor license issued by appellee liquor control commission. The local electorate voted the area in which the bar was located dry, pursuant to the local option provision of 47 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 4-472, and appellant was unable to renew his license. Appellant brought suit against appellee to have § 4-472 declared violative of Pa. Const. art. I, § 10 and U.S. Const. amends. V, XIV. The lower court dismissed appellant's suit, and appellant sought review of the decision. The court affirmed the lower court's judgment. The court stated that neither appellant nor anyone else had any property interest in the right to sell liquor, and that a vote to prohibit the sale of alcoholic beverages within a locality was not a taking of private property for public use requiring compensation to those who were unable to renew a license. The court held that the local option law was not violative of due process because it was a reasonable exercise of police powers and was rationally related to the object and purposes of the statutory scheme.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the lower court dismissing appellant licensee's suit against appellee liquor control commission, because the decision of a local referendum to ban liquor sales, precluding the renewal of appellant's license, did not constitute a taking of private property or violate the right to due process.

Commonwealth v. Aulisio


No. 105 E.D. Appeal Docket 1984


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


514 Pa. 84; 522 A.2d 1075; 1987 Pa. LEXIS 675


October 20, 1986, Argued  
March 19, 1987, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Denied June 2, 1987.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of Death imposed by the Court of Common Pleas, Criminal Division, of Lackawanna County on May 17, 1984, at No. 1386 A. thru I. of 1981.



DISPOSITION: The convictions as to the two counts of murder of the first degree are affirmed, the sentence of death is vacated, and the case is remanded to the Court of Common Pleas of Lackawanna County for appellant to be sentenced to life imprisonment. The convictions as to the two counts of kidnapping are reversed, and the sentences imposed with respect thereto are vacated.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a judgment of the Court of Common Pleas of Lackawanna County (Pennsylvania), which convicted him of two counts of murder of the first degree and two counts of kidnapping. A separate sentencing hearing was held, as required by 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 9711, and defendant was sentenced to death in connection with the two murder convictions. Defendant was also sentenced for the kidnapping convictions.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of two counts of murder of the first degree and two counts of kidnapping. He was sentenced to death for the murders. Defendant, 15 years old, fatally shot two younger children with a shotgun, after having taken them to a vacant house owned by defendant's family. On appeal, the court affirmed the murder convictions, but vacated the death sentence and reversed the kidnapping convictions. The evidence of defendant's guilt as to murder of the first degree was so strong as to leave no question that defendant's guilt was established beyond a reasonable doubt. The jury found that kidnapping was an aggravating circumstance justifying imposition of the death penalty. However, the evidence was insufficient to support the verdict of guilt as to kidnapping; thus, one of the statutory aggravating circumstances found by the jury was without proper basis. There was no proof that the victims were confined or removed a substantial distance from their original location. There was no proof that any movement of the children was accomplished by force, threat, deception, or without parental consent.


OUTCOME: The convictions as to the two counts of murder of the first degree were affirmed, and the sentence of death was vacated. The convictions as to the two counts of kidnapping were reversed. The jury relied on the aggravating circumstance of kidnapping to impose the death sentence; however, the elements of the crime of kidnapping were not established beyond a reasonable doubt.

Ridley Arms, Inc. v. Ridley


No. 51 E.D. Appeal Docket, 1986


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


515 Pa. 542; 531 A.2d 414; 1987 Pa. LEXIS 661


October 21, 1986, Argued  
March 17, 1987, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected September 28, 1987.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Order of the Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania at Nos. 2988 C.D. 1983, 2837 C.D. 1983 (Consolidated) affirming in part and reversing in part the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Delaware County, Pennsylvania, dated September 23, 1983 in Civil Action No. 79-4268-Equity.

 90 Pa. Commw. Ct. 143; 494 A.2d 870 (1985).



DISPOSITION: Vacated and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayers brought an action in equity challenging the constitutionality of a refuse collection ordinance imposed by appellee township. Appellants sought review of a judgment of the Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania, which affirmed that part of the trial court's judgment upholding the constitutionality of the statute and reversed that part of the judgment requiring repayment of moneys paid for refuse collection that was not performed.


OVERVIEW: Appellant taxpayers, an apartment complex, chose to use a private contractor for its garbage collection instead of using appellee township services, for health, safety, and financial reasons. Appellants challenged the constitutionality of an ordinance that allowed appellee to impose upon appellants a tax for refuse collection that was never performed. Appellants brought an action in equity, seeking to enjoin appellee from collecting the tax and seeking repayment of such fees. The trial court upheld the constitutionality of the ordinance, but ordered a partial refund of moneys paid to the township. The appellate court affirmed that part of the trial court's judgment upholding the constitutionality of the statute and reversed that part of the judgment requiring repayment. The parts of the ordinance that imposed the fees were declared void and of no effect. Appellants did not show unconstitutionality, but the ordinance violated a local code, as the costs were unreasonable compared to costs of using a private contractor.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the judgment of the appellate court and remanded the cause to the trial court with directions for repayment of refuse collection taxes. The segments of the ordinance that imposed the taxes were declared void and of no effect because the apportionment of costs was unreasonable and in violation of a local code, as applied to appellants.

48 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Church


No. 48 W.D. Appeal Dkt. 1985


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


513 Pa. 534; 522 A.2d 30; 1987 Pa. LEXIS 632


September 17, 1986, Submitted  
March 10, 1987, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Lawrence County, entered July 19, 1985, at No. 1002 of 1984, Criminal Division.



DISPOSITION: For the reasons set forth above, the instant judgment must be reversed, and this case must be remanded to the Court of Common Pleas of Lawrence County for re-sentencing and other further proceedings as may be necessary, not inconsistent with this opinion.

It is so ordered.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a case regarding the imposition of a fine for an overweight vehicle violation, Commonwealth sought review of an order of the Court of Common Pleas of Lawrence County (Pennsylvania), which substantially reduced appellee's mandatory fine based on his inability to pay.


OVERVIEW: Appellee pleaded guilty to driving an overweight truck. The district justice imposed the fine mandated by statute and the matter was appealed where appellee again pleaded guilty. In spite of the mandatory nature of the sentencing statute, however, appellee's fine was substantially reduced. Commonwealth appealed. On review, the court held that mandatory sentences based upon statutory classifications were constitutional if they conformed to the equal protection clause of U.S. Const. amend. XIV. The court stated that the classification must be reasonable, not arbitrary, and must rest upon some rational difference having a fair, substantial and reasonable relationship to the objective or objectives of the legislation at issue so that all persons similarly situated will be treated alike. Applying the rational basis test the court held it was apparent that the legislative scheme or classification whereby overweight fines could not have been reduced or forgiven because of inability to pay was not arbitrary and bore a perfectly rational relationship to a legitimate government interest.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment that reduced appellee's fine for an overweight vehicle violation. The court found that the mandatory sentencing provision of the applicable statute was constitutional and remanded the matter for re-sentencing.

Commonwealth v. Terry


No. 179 Eastern District Appeal Docket, 1984


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


513 Pa. 381; 521 A.2d 398; 1987 Pa. LEXIS 619


April 15, 1986, Argued  
February 17, 1987, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Judgment of Sentence of Court of Common Pleas of Montgomery County, Criminal Division, No. 1563-79, Entered November 14, 1984.



DISPOSITION: For the foregoing reasons, appellant's conviction and sentence of death are affirmed. 23



23 The Prothonotary of the Eastern District is directed to transmit the full and complete record of the proceedings below and our review to the Governor as soon as possible. 42 Pa.C.S. § 9711(i).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought a second review of a judgment of sentence from the Court Common Pleas of Montgomery County, Criminal Division (Pennsylvania) after he was convicted of first degree murder, assault by a life prisoner, aggravated assault, and recklessly endangering another person. The original conviction was vacated and remanded for a new trial after the jury was permitted to have a copy of appellant's written confession during deliberations.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted of murder and sentenced to death. The conviction was vacated and the cause was remanded for a new trial because the jury was permitted to have a copy of appellant's written confession during deliberations. During the second trial, appellant requested the judge instruct the jury that it could find appellant guilty of third degree murder or voluntary manslaughter as well as first degree murder. The judge refused to give the instruction based on a recent holding by the court stating that a jury verdict of not guilty of lesser included homicides along with a guilty verdict on murder precluded re-trial on the lesser included homicides if the murder conviction was vacated. Upon review, the court held that such an instruction was required only when the evidence permitted a jury to determine the lesser included offenses were the offenses actually committed. Appellant did not meet his burden of producing evidence which required the instruction nor was there any other evidence of the lesser included offenses. The court held that if there was an error in failing to charge the jury on lesser included offenses, it was harmless and the judgment was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the conviction and sentence of death because appellant failed to produce evidence which required the trial court to give an instruction on the lesser included offenses.

Commonwealth v. Sims

513 Pa. 366; 521 A.2d 391; 1987 Pa. LEXIS 621


October 25, 1985, Argued  
February 17, 1987, Decided

DISPOSITION: Accordingly, the judgment of sentence is reversed and a new trial is awarded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged the decision of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania), which found defendant guilty of first degree murder, robbery, and criminal conspiracy, and imposed the death penalty upon defendant.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of first degree murder, robbery, and criminal conspiracy. The jury voted to sentence defendant to death. Defendant asserted that the trial court's application of the state's attorney-client privilege violated defendant's constitutional right to confront witnesses against him. Defendant claimed that at the very least, the witness should have claimed his attorney-client privilege in front of the jury. The court determined that the trial court's refusal to require the witness to invoke privilege in the presence of the jury was an unacceptable infringement upon defendant's right of confrontation. Accordingly, the court reversed defendant's sentence and awarded him a new trial.


OUTCOME: The court reversed defendant's conviction and sentence and awarded him a new trial because the trial court's refusal to require a witness to invoke attorney-client privilege in the presence of the jury was an unacceptable infringement upon defendant's right of confrontation.

Clairol, Inc. v. Commonwealth


No. 36 M.D. Appeal Dkt. 1985


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


513 Pa. 74; 518 A.2d 1165; 1986 Pa. LEXIS 965


June 3, 1986, Argued  
December 11, 1986, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Commonwealth Court at Nos. 690, 691, and 692 C.D. 1982; 88 Cmwlth Ct. 153, 489 A.2d 286 (1985).



DISPOSITION: ON CONSIDERATION WHEREOF, it is now hereby ordered and adjudged by this Court that the Order of the Commonwealth Court is reversed, and the orders of the Board of Finance and Review are reinstated.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant state challenged the decision of the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania), which reversed the orders of the Board of Finance and Revenue, which assessed a franchise tax against appellee corporation for its business activities within the state.


OVERVIEW: The Department of Revenue determined that appellee corporation was subject to a franchise tax under 72 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 7601. The Board of Finance and Revenue affirmed this determination. On appeal, the Commonwealth Court reversed because it determined that appellee's activities consisted solely of soliciting orders for its products and, thus, fell short of "doing business". Appellant contended that the lower court erred in its holding that mere solicitation did not constitute doing business for the purposes of 72 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 7601. Appellee employed a vast network of representatives, whose primary functions were to drum up business. The court determined that it was not incorrect for the Board of Finance and Review to conclude that appellee was doing business for purposes of the franchise tax. Appellee alleged that the statute was unconstitutional due to an insufficient nexus between appellee and state to justify exercise of the taxing power. The court found this argument to be without merit. Accordingly, the court reversed the judgment of the Commonwealth Court, and reinstated the decision of the Board of Finance and Review.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the Commonwealth Court and reinstated the decision of the Board of Finance and Review, which imposed a franchise tax upon appellee corporation because the court determined that appellee's actions within the state were sufficient to constitute "doing business" for the purpose of imposing the franchise tax.

Commonwealth v. Bell


No. 29 E.D. Appeal Docket 1984


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


512 Pa. 334; 516 A.2d 1172; 1986 Pa. LEXIS 887


December 3, 1985, Submitted  
October 24, 1986, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied December 29, 1986.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County, Trial Division, Criminal Section, Entered on January 24, 1984 at No. 1404 August Session, 1982.
Commonwealth v. Bell, 10 Phila. 276, 1984 Phila. Cty. Rptr. LEXIS 23 (Pa. C.P., 1984)



DISPOSITION: Accordingly, for all of the foregoing reasons, the Order of the Court of Common Pleas is reversed and the matter is remanded for resentencing.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant commonwealth challenged the judgment of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania), which convicted appellee in a bench trial of voluntary manslaughter, and thereafter found 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 9712(d) inapplicable and unconstitutional as applied to appellee, and imposed a sentence of four years probation. Appellant contended that § 9712(d) was constitutional and applicable to the facts of appellee's conviction.


OVERVIEW: Appellee was convicted of voluntary manslaughter, and the trial court thereafter found a provision of the Mandatory Minimum Sentencing Act, 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 9712(d), inapplicable and unconstitutional as applied to appellee, and imposed four years probation. Appellant commonwealth sought appellate review of the action of the sentencing court. The court had jurisdiction to entertain the appeal under § 9712(d) and 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 722(7). Section 9712 required the imposition of a minimum prison sentence of at least five years upon conviction of certain enumerated felonies, where it was proven at sentencing by a preponderance of the evidence that appellee visibly possessed a firearm during the commission of the offense. The court reversed the judgment and remanded the matter for resentencing, because § 9712(d) was both applicable to the facts surrounding appellee's conviction and constitutional in general. The court held that § 9712(d) was clear and unambiguous, and accordingly applied its plain meaning and common usage to determine that it did not implicate appellee's fundamental liberty interest, because his fundamental right to freedom from confinement was already forfeited.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded for resentencing the trial court's judgment that convicted appellee of voluntary manslaughter, and thereafter found a sentencing guideline statute inapplicable and unconstitutional as applied, and imposed four years probation. The court held that the statute was applicable to appellant, did not offend the separation of powers principle or amount to cruel and unusual punishment, and was therefore constitutional.

Commonwealth v. Fahy


No. 112 Eastern District Appeal Docket 1983


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


512 Pa. 298; 516 A.2d 689; 1986 Pa. LEXIS 890


January 22, 1985, Argued  
October 21, 1986, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County, Criminal Division, at Nos. 2284, 2286, 2288, 2289 February Term 1981.



DISPOSITION: The convictions and the sentence of death are affirmed. 14



14 The Prothonotary of the Eastern District is directed to transmit the full and complete record of the trial, sentencing hearing, imposition of sentence and review by this Court to the Governor. 42 Pa. C.S. §9711(i).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of a judgment of sentence from the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania) after being convicted of murder of the first degree, rape, burglary, and possession of an instrument of crime and was assessed a penalty of death.


OVERVIEW: After appellant was convicted of murder of the first degree, rape, burglary, and possession of an instrument of crime in connection with the violent death of a 12 year old girl, the jury imposed a sentence of death. Upon review, appellant argued the evidence was insufficient to support a conviction of murder of the first degree, that the suppression court committed error in denying his motion to suppress his confession, that he should have been granted a mistrial as a result of prosecutorial misconduct, and that the death penalty was unconstitutional. The court held that there was sufficient evidence to support the conviction of murder and that the waiver of appellant's constitutional rights was knowing and intelligent. The suppression court found the evidence introduced by the prosecution more credible than that of appellant, and thus refused to grant the motion to suppress. Moreover, appellant's improper response to a prosecutor's question on cross-examination was unsolicited and promptly stricken from the record. Lastly, in affirming the conviction and sentence, appellant's contention that the death penalty statute was vague and overbroad was dismissed as meritless.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the conviction and sentence imposed on appellant by the trial court because the evidence was sufficient to support the conviction, alleged prejudicial statements made by appellant on cross-examination were unsolicited, appellant's confession was knowing and intelligent, and there was sufficient aggravating circumstances, including appellant's history of violence, to support he imposition of the death penalty.

Commonwealth v. Lutz


No. 59 WESTERN DISTRICT APPEAL DOCKET, 1985


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


512 Pa. 192; 516 A.2d 339; 1986 Pa. LEXIS 882; 25 ERC (BNA) 1162; 17 ELR 20228


March 7, 1986, Argued  
October 17, 1986, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Order of Court of Common Pleas of Westmoreland County, Criminal Division, Dated April 29, 1983, at No. 1222 C of 1982, Transferred by Commonwealth Court on May 17, 1985 From No. 1447 C.D. 1983.



DISPOSITION: The order of the Court of Common Pleas of Westmoreland County is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant state sought review of an order entered by the Court of Common Pleas of Westmoreland County (Pennsylvania), dismissing criminal charges filed against appellee, who was charged with violating § 610(7) of the Solid Waste Management Act, 35 P.S. § 6018.610(7), obstructing an employee of the Department of Environmental Resources, and obstruction of justice, 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 5101.


OVERVIEW: Two agents of the Department of Environmental Resources (Department) attempted to conduct a warrantless search of appellee's commercial property to determine if solid waste had been dumped there. Appellee ordered the agents off the property and confiscated a camera and two sample bottles. Appellant was charged with violating § 610(7) of the Solid Waste Management Act (Act), 35 P.S. § 6018.610(7), obstructing a Department employee, and obstruction of justice, 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 5101. Appellee filed a motion to dismiss the charges, contending that the warrantless search provision of the Act was unconstitutional. The trial court agreed and dismissed the charges. The court affirmed, holding that U.S. Const. amend IV prohibited the Department from conducting random, warrantless searches of commercial property under the Act in order to enforce ordinary solid waste regulations because such waste did not pose the same danger to public health as hazardous waste, for which such searches were permissible, and the solid waste industry lacked the special characteristics that would ordinarily put an operator on notice of the potential for random regulatory searches.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the trial court dismissing criminal charges against appellee because the warrantless search provision of the Solid Waste Management Act was unconstitutional as it applied to ordinary solid waste.

Commonwealth v. Parker White Metal Co.


No. 58 W.D. Appeal Docket 1985


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


512 Pa. 74; 515 A.2d 1358; 1986 Pa. LEXIS 869; 25 ERC (BNA) 1173; 17 ELR 20352


December 6, 1985, Argued  
October 9, 1986, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Erie County, Criminal Division, at Nos. 437, 452, 455 and 494 of 1984, entered August 29, 1984.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant attorney general sought relief from the order of the Court of Common Pleas of Erie County (Pennsylvania), which dismissed charges against appellees, individual and corporation, for dumping or permitting the dumping of hazardous wastes without a permit and for illegally transporting solid wastes to a facility. The trial court found the underlying statute was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Appellees, individual and corporation, were charged with violations of the Solid Waste Management Act, 35 Pa. Cons. Stat. §§ 6018.101-6018.1003. The trial court dismissed the charges on constitutional grounds. The court reversed, holding that, although two provisions of the act proscribed identical conduct yet provided for vastly different penalties, it did not carve out classifications or single out any distinct class of persons for unequal treatment, but by its terms applies equally to all. The court held that the act did not confer upon the prosecutor discretion other than that already routinely exercised in the enforcement of other statutes imposing criminal sanctions, and did not violate equal protection principles. The court held that the decision of whether to prosecute an alleged offender was a matter legitimately committed to the sound discretion of the prosecutor or agency charged with enforcement of the act, that the act provided clear notice to all as to the conduct proscribed and the possible penalties for its violation, and that it did not offend due process. The court held that the act did not improperly delegate legislative powers to prosecutors.


OUTCOME: The court reversed, holding that the statute in question did not violate equal protection or due process principles by conferring upon prosecutors unfettered discretion in choosing between significantly different penalties provided by the statute for identical crimes. The court held that this discretion did not constitute an improper delegation of legislative powers.

Craig v. Magee Memorial Rehabilitation Center


No. 105 E.D. Appeal Dkt. 1985


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


512 Pa. 60; 515 A.2d 1350; 1986 Pa. LEXIS 878


December 2, 1985, Argued  
October 8, 1986, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Denied March 24, 1987.

Super. 622, 491 A.2d 923 (1985).



DISPOSITION: Accordingly, the order of the Superior Court is reversed, and the matter is remanded to the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia for proceedings consistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant rehabilitation center challenged the decision of a superior court (Pennsylvania) that affirmed the trial court' order assessing additional damages in favor of appellee patient for delay pursuant to Pa. R. Civ. P. 238 after the trial court held that appellant was liable for medical malpractice.


OVERVIEW: Appellee patient filed a medical malpractice action against appellant rehabilitation center. Approximately four years later, the case came to trial. At which time, a mistrial was declared. Three years later, the case was tried and the jury found in favor of appellee. After the jury's verdict was entered, the trial court added damages for delay pursuant to Pa. R. Civ. P. 238. On appeal, the superior court affirmed the trial court's decision. Appellant then sought review of the superior court's decision. The court reversed the superior court's decision. The court further directed that the mandatory provisions of R. 238, which assess delay damages against defendants without regard to fault, were suspended for all cases pending in the courts and for any cases instituted thereafter because R. 238 had become an uncontestable presumption that all fault lies with a defendant.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the superior court's decision affirming the trial court's decision assessing additional damages, in favor of appellee patient, for delay. It further suspended all cases pending in the court under the rule because it determined that the rule had become an uncontestable presumption that all fault lay with a defendant.

In re Commonwealth, Dep't of Transp.


Nos. 74, 92 W.D. Appeal Docket 1985


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


511 Pa. 620; 515 A.2d 899; 1986 Pa. LEXIS 863


April 15, 1986, Argued  
October 3, 1986, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Order December 5, 1986.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeals from the Orders of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County, Civil division, entered August 30, 1985 and September 27, 1985, at No. G.D. 84-6940.



DISPOSITION: Accordingly, we reverse the orders of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County and remand this case to that tribunal for further proceedings consistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The commonwealth sought review of a judgment from the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County (Pennsylvania), which denied its request for a declaration of taking to acquire a fee simple interest in 50 percent of the property owned by appellee property owners.


OVERVIEW: The commonwealth filed a declaration of taking to acquire a fee simple interest in approximately 50 percent of the property owned by appellee property owners for the construction of a highway. Appellees filed preliminary objections, which the trial court sustained. The commonwealth challenged the trial court's decision, contending the statute authorizing the transportation t take the property was unconstitutional. Reversing the trial court's judgment, the court held that one who sought to declare a title unconstitutional pursuant to Pa. Const. art. III, § 3 must have demonstrated either that the legislators and the public were deceived as to the act's contents at the time of passage or that the title on its face was such that no reasonable person would have been on notice as to the act's contents. The court ruled that the act's title in this case was not such that a reasonable person was not on notice that the act would concern acquisition of lands for transportation purposes.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgment that denied the commonwealth's request for a declaration of taking because the governing act did not violate the state constitution.

 

60 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. DeHart


Nos. 77-78 M. D. Appeal Docket 1984


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


512 Pa. 235; 516 A.2d 656; 1986 Pa. LEXIS 870


May 17, 1985, Argued  
October 3, 1986, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeals from Judgments of Sentence of Court of Common Pleas of Huntingdon County, Criminal Division entered September 28, 1984 at Nos. 98, 144 C.A. 1983.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant murderer's conviction from the Court of Common Pleas of Huntingdon County (Pennsylvania) for first degree murder and his death sentence was automatically directly reviewed pursuant to 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. §§ 722(4), 9711(h)(1).


OVERVIEW: Appellant murderer was convicted of first degree murder based upon the facts that appellant escaped from prison, ambushed the decedent, and stole money, a car, clothing, and the weapon used to kill the decedent. Pursuant to the automatic direct review by the court, 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. §§ 722(4), 9711(h)(1), the conviction was affirmed. The consolidation of the escape charge with other charges was proper pursuant to Pa. R. Crim. P. 1127(A)(1)(b), because the murder, robbery, and burglary were perpetrated in continuance of appellant's escape and therefore part of the same transaction. The prosecution's use of peremptory challenges to eliminate prospective jurors that wavered on the question of the death penalty did not violate appellant's right to a jury drawn from a fair cross-section of the community, under the U.S. Const. amends. VI and XIV. Potential jurors that opposed the death penalty were not a distinctive group, and therefore, much less so were venire persons who wavered on their response to their ability to impose the death sentence. Similarly, the court rejected the notion that by excluding such jurors, the impaneled jury was biased in favor of the death penalty.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the first degree murder conviction and sentence to death. The evidence established beyond a reasonable doubt that appellant murderer committed the crime during the course of escaping from prison. Further, all of appellant's challenges to the conduct of the trial and picking the jury were without merit.

Smith v. Philadelphia

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, the city and associated others, sought review of a judgment from the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania), which declared unconstitutional the statute limiting recovery of damages against political subdivisions to $ 500,000 in the aggregate for legally cognizable tort injuries arising from the same transaction.


OVERVIEW: A gas explosion in Philadelphia killed seven people, injured many others, and caused extensive property damage. Appellees filed a declaratory judgment action seeking a declaration that the Political Subdivision Tort Claims Act, 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. Reversing the trial court's judgment, the court held that the statute did not exceed the power granted to the legislature in Pa. Const. art. 1, § 11 or art. 3, § 18. The court further held that under an intermediate standard of review, the statute was constitutional.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgment and held that the statutory limitation on actions brought against political subdivisions was constitutional because it was not violative of the state or federal constitution.

 

62 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Jumper


No. 22 Middle District Appeal Docket, 1985


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


511 Pa. 446; 515 A.2d 540; 1986 Pa. LEXIS 849


May 15, 1986, Submitted  
September 25, 1986, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order Entered November 1, 1983, By the Court of Common Pleas of Cumberland County, Pennsylvania at 640 Criminal 1983



DISPOSITION: Order reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The commonwealth sought review of an order of the Court of Common Pleas of Cumberland County (Pennsylvania), which quashed an information that charged defendant with the offense of homicide by vehicle. The court found 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 3732 unconstitutional as applied to traffic light violations because defendant could not have known that death would be a probable consequence.


OVERVIEW: Defendant allegedly ran a red light causing a fatal automobile accident. He was charged with the offense of homicide by vehicle, 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 3732, but the information was quashed on the trial court's reasoning that a traffic light violation could not be the basis for a homicide charge. Because the decision rested on constitutional grounds of equal protection, review was transferred to the supreme court. The court noted that traffic light violations were no less likely to result in fatalities than were many other offenses that involved a potential for violent collisions. On the issue of equal protection, the court rejected defendant's argument that statutory scheme § 3732 impermissibly created differences in criminal liability among persons who unintentionally caused fatalities and that those differences rested upon the arbitrary factor of whether the person causing any given fatality was a motorist. The court found that the two classes of offenders were not similarly situated. Reversing the order that quashed the information, the court held that there was a rational basis for the difference in penalties imposed for involuntary manslaughter and homicide by vehicle.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order that quashed the information charging defendant with the offense of homicide by vehicle. The court rejected the equal protection attack on applying a homicide statute against defendant for running a red light because the commonwealth had a legitimate interest in reducing highway fatalities and persons guilty of involuntary manslaughter were not similarly situated to require the same punishment.

Commonwealth v. Peterkin

Supreme Court of Pennsylvania

PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged the decision of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia (Pennsylvania), which convicted appellant of robbery, possession of an instrument of crime-generally, and two counts of murder in the first degree for the shooting deaths of two persons. Appellant was sentenced to death in accordance with the jury's findings.


OVERVIEW: Appellant criminal was sentenced to death after a jury convicted appellant of robbery, possession of an instrument of crime-generally, and two counts of murder in the first degree for the shooting deaths of two persons. OUTCOME: The court affirmed the sentence of death and the conviction of appellant for robbery, possession of an instrument of crime-generally, and two counts of murder in the first degree because there was sufficient evidence to support the jury's verdicts, and the lower court's error in admitting hearsay testimony was harmless.

Fisher v. Commonwealth, Dep't of Public Welfare

67 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Buckley


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


510 Pa. 326; 508 A.2d 281; 1986 Pa. LEXIS 754


May 15, 1985, Argued  
April 14, 1986, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Commonwealth Court at Docket No. 265 C.D. 1981. 81 Pa. Commw. 530, 475 A.2d 160 (1984) 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant New Jersey resident sought review of an order from the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania) that affirmed a decision of the Board of Finance and Revenue. A deficiency was assessed against appellant for not paying personal income taxes on his medical practice. Appellant argued that the Reciprocal Personal Income Tax Agreement between Pennsylvania and New Jersey violated the equal protection clause of U.S. Const. amend. XIV, § 1.


OVERVIEW: Appellant New Jersey resident conducted his medical practice from an office in Pennsylvania. Based on the Reciprocal Personal Income Tax Agreement between Pennsylvania and New Jersey, appellant argued that as a non-resident he was exempt from personal income tax. The commonwealth court affirmed the decision of the Board of Finance and Revenue assessing a tax deficiency. On review, the court noted that although the agreement exempted residents of one state who worked in the other from filing returns and paying personal income taxes, the exemption only applied to income in the form of compensation. Because appellant's entire income earned in Pennsylvania was net profits from his practice, the court held that he was not entitled to the exemption. The court rejected appellant's equal protection attack on the agreement finding that appellant did little to demonstrate that the taxing scheme was unreasonable. The court found that a worker who could not chose the location of his employer's business was not similarly situated to a person who owned the business and that easing administrative burdens on compensated non-residents did not require a similar reduction on self-employed persons.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order assessing a tax deficiency against appellant New Jersey resident for personal income tax on net profits from his Pennsylvania medical practice. The court held that the reciprocal agreement between the two states exempted only nonresidents who earned income in the form of compensation and that the taxing scheme did not violate equal protection because the two types of income earners were not similarly situated.

Consumer Party of Pa. v. Commonwealth


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


510 Pa. 158; 507 A.2d 323; 1986 Pa. LEXIS 748


January 24, 1985, Argued  
March 27, 1986, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Order of Commonwealth Court dated April 19, 1984, entered at No. 2841 C.D. 1983. 81 Pa. Commw. 609, 475 A.2d 850 (1984) 



DISPOSITION: Accordingly, the order of the Commonwealth Court is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, a political party and several citizen-taxpayers, challenged the order of the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania), which upheld the constitutionality of a statute governing compensation of local government officials. Appellants challenged the statute on grounds that it's meaning had changed after passage and it changed compensation of a public officer after election or appointment.


OVERVIEW: Appellants, a political party and several citizen-taxpayers, challenged the constitutionality of the Public Official Compensation Law of 1983 (Law), 65 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 366.1 et seq. The lower court upheld its constitutionality and granted summary judgment to appellees, the commonwealth, legislature, legislators and several officials. Before addressing the order, the court held that appellants had standing to maintain the action because it presented governmental action that would otherwise go unchallenged, the determination of the Law's constitutionality was a function ultimately left to the courts, redress through other channels was unavailable, and there were no other persons better situated to assert the claim. The court held that appellee legislators were immune from suit under the Speech and Debate Clause of Pa. Const. art. II, §15 and properly dismissed. The court affirmed the order, holding that the Law was not altered during passage in violation of Pa. Const. art. III, § 1. The court also held that the Law did not improperly change the legislators' salary in violation of Pa. Const. art. II, § 8, and Pa. Const. art. III, § 27.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order, holding that appellee legislators were immune from suit, and that the statute in question did not violate constitutional prohibitions against altering a bill during passage in the legislature, and altering public officials' salary while they were in office.

 

69 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Minnich v. Rivera


No. 161


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


509 Pa. 588; 506 A.2d 879; 1986 Pa. LEXIS 721


October 22, 1985, Argued  
March 21, 1986, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Judgment of the Court of Common Pleas of Lancaster County, Pennsylvania, Domestic Relations Division, No. 1435 of 1983. No. 161 E.D. Appeal Docket 1984.



DISPOSITION: The respective interests of the putative father, the child and the mother are clear. Also clear is the interest of the Commonwealth in seeing that fathers support their children who are born out of wedlock so that those children do not become public charges. The standard of proof must be one that recognizes these interests and does not unduly risk the erroneous deprivation of any of them. On balance, the preponderance standard meets these demands and satisfies the requirements of due process. The order of the Common Pleas Court of Lancaster County is reversed and this case is remanded for proceedings consistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant mother sought review of a judgment of the Court of Common Pleas of Lancaster County (Pennsylvania), which reversed itself, holding that due process required a standard of proof of clear and convincing evidence in a paternity case. The trial court held that 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 6704(d), requiring only proof by a preponderance of evidence, was unconstitutional and ordered a new trial.


OVERVIEW: Appellant mother sought support for her infant son from appellee father, who denied paternity. The jury found that appellee was the father. Appellee claimed that the burden of proof as set forth in 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 6704(d), proof by a preponderance of the evidence, offended the due process clause of U.S. Const. amend. XIV. Appellee argued that the burden of proof in paternity cases should have been proof by clear and convincing evidence. The trial court denied appellee's motion but later reversed itself, holding that due process required proof by clear and convincing evidence in a paternity case. The trial court held that § 6704(d) was unconstitutional and ordered a new trial. On appeal, the court reversed the judgment of the trial court and remanded the case. Considering the respective interests of the parties, including the child and state, the burden of proof by a preponderance of the evidence, which required the litigants to share the risk of error in "roughly equal fashion," satisfied the minimum requirements of due process.


OUTCOME: The order of the trial court was reversed and the case was remanded. The evidentiary standard in a paternity trial, proof by a preponderance of the evidence, as prescribed by statute, did not violate the due process clause of the Fourteenth Amendment.

Jackson v. Hendrick


No. 167


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


509 Pa. 456; 503 A.2d 400; 1986 Pa. LEXIS 713


February 15, 1985, Submitted  
January 16, 1986, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Consolidated Appeals from the Orders of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia, Trial Division, Civil Section, dated June 22, 1984, and October 11, 1984, entered at No. 2437 February Term, 1971.

No. 167 E.D. Appeal Docket 1984



DISPOSITION: Remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant city officials challenged orders of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia (Pennsylvania), which required defendants to reduce prison population according to the "one man, one cell" standard, and which later held defendants in contempt, in plaintiff inmates' class action for injunctive relief arising from violation of their U.S. Const. amend. VIII rights.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff inmates filed a class action in equity against defendant city officials seeking injunctive relief from confinement conditions allegedly violating plaintiffs' U.S. Const. amend. VIII rights. After the court of common pleas required defendants to establish a "one man, one cell" cap on the prison population, and approved stipulations and agreements between the parties, the common pleas court amended its order to provide a rated capacity for each prison. Fernley v. Bd. of Supervisors


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


509 Pa. 413; 502 A.2d 585; 1985 Pa. LEXIS 507


October 22, 1984, Argued  
December 27, 1985, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Denied July 31, 1986.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Order of the Commonwealth Court at No. 573 C.D. 1982, filed August 16, 1983, Affirming Order of Chester County Court of Common Pleas at No. 17 April Term, 1978, Filed February 25, 1982. 76 Pa. Commonwealth Ct. 409, 464 A.2d 587 (1983).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant landowners challenged an order of the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania), which affirmed an order of the trial court finding appellee township board of supervisors' denial of appellants' petition for a curative amendment under the Pennsylvania Municipalities Planning Code, 53 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 10609.1 (1972), proper pursuant to a fair share analysis.


OVERVIEW: Appellant landowners sought a curative amendment under the Pennsylvania Municipalities Planning Code, 53 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 10609.1 (1972). Appellants challenged the total prohibition of multifamily dwellings contained in the Schuylkill Township, Chester County, Pa., Zoning Ordinance (1955) on exclusionary grounds and sought establishment of a new residential district in which appellants could construct such dwellings. Appellee township board of supervisor's finding that the ordinance was not unconstitutionally exclusionary because the disputed area was not a logical area for growth and development was affirmed by a trial court and an intermediate appellate court on the basis of a fair-share analysis. The court held that the fair-share analysis did not apply where the ordinance totally excluded a basic form of housing such as apartments and where appellee failed to establish a legitimate public purpose for such exclusion. The court therefore reversed and remanded to the trial court for approval of appellants' proposed development unless appellee could show that the plan was incompatible with the site or reasonable preexisting health and safety codes and regulations.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded to the trial court. The zoning ordinance was unconstitutionally restrictive where it banned all multifamily housing; therefore, fair-share analysis did not apply and appellant landowners could develop their land as proposed within preexisting codes.

Snyder v. Commonwealth, Unemployment Compensation Bd. of Review


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


509 Pa. 438; 502 A.2d 1232; 1985 Pa. LEXIS 508


October 21, 1985, Argued  
December 26, 1985, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant court employee challenged an order of the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania), which affirmed appellee unemployment compensation board of review's denial of benefits to appellant where appellant quit her job to pursue a political candidacy pursuant to an administrative directive that proscribed court employees from partisan political activity.


OVERVIEW: Appellant court employee sought unemployment benefits after she left her job as required by administrative directive when she desired to pursue a political candidacy. The denial of appellant's claim was affirmed by appellee unemployment compensation board of review and the commonwealth court on the ground that she voluntarily left her position. The court granted allocatur to determine the validity of its administrative directives as against the Ethics Act, 65 P.S. § 401 et seq., and to decide whether appellant could be said to have voluntarily quit under the circumstances. In affirming, the court held that its administrative directives, as internal housekeeping matters, had the full force and effect of rules as described in Pa. Const. art. V, § 10(c); therefore, 65 P.S. § 410 was suspended as applied to appellant. Forbidding partisan political activity by a court employee did not violate appellant's U.S. Const. amend. I rights. The court also found that appellant's desire to act in conflict to the lawful directive was the cause of her unemployment; therefore, she was not eligible for benefits.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed. The court's administrative directives were constitutional rules that superseded statutes when applied to court employees. Because appellant court employee voluntarily quit her job to pursue a political candidacy and was not forced to do so by administrative directive, she was ineligible for unemployment benefits.

Commonwealth v. Ritchie


No. 69


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


509 Pa. 357; 502 A.2d 148; 1985 Pa. LEXIS 449


March 4, 1985, Argued  
December 11, 1985, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 69 W.D. Appeal Dkt. 1984. Appeal from the Order of the Superior Court of February 3, 1984, at No. 137 Pittsburgh 1981, vacating the Judgment of Sentence entered January 8, 1981, in the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County, Pennsylvania, Criminal Division at No. CC7903887A.



DISPOSITION: The case is remanded for proceedings consistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The commonwealth sought review of an order of the Superior Court of Pennsylvania, which vacated and remanded the judgment of sentence against defendant that convicted him of rape, involuntary deviate sexual intercourse, incest, and corruption of minors.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of rape, involuntary deviate sexual intercourse, incest, and corruption of minors. The superior court vacated and remanded the case. The court affirmed the superior court's remand of the case and ordered that the Child Welfare Services (CWS) files that pertained to the examination of the complainant be made available to defendant's counsel. The court held that unless the trial court was convinced that any error was necessarily harmless, it had to vacate the judgment of sentence and grant defendant a new trial. The court held that despite 11 P. S. 2215(a) that provided for confidentiality of child abuse summaries, the trial court erred in when it refused to allow the defense access to the CWS files. The court held that the prosecution's interest in maintaining the confidentiality of those records did not override defendant's U.S. Const. amend. VI right to effectively confront and cross-examine the witnesses against him.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's order that vacated and remanded the judgment of sentence against defendant that convicted him of rape, involuntary deviate sexual intercourse, incest, and corruption of minors. The court ordered that defendant be granted access to the Child Welfare Services files. The court held that defendant was unjustifiably denied the opportunity to have the files reviewed with the eyes and perspective of an advocate.

Fischer v. Dep't of Public Welfare


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


509 Pa. 293; 502 A.2d 114; 1985 Pa. LEXIS 443


April 18, 1985, Argued  
December 5, 1985, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Commonwealth Court at 283 C.D. 1981, No. 67 M.D. Appeal Dkt. 1984, 85 Pa. Comwlth. 240, 482 A.2d 1148 (1984) 



DISPOSITION: Decree affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, taxpayer and medical assistance recipients, challenged an order of the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania), which held that the restriction of abortion funding to life endangering situations was constitutional and permanently enjoined appellee the Department of Public Welfare from enforcing the rape and incest reporting provisions.


OVERVIEW: Appellants, taxpayer and medical assistance recipients, sought review of a decree, which held that funding restrictions on abortions under the Abortion Control Act, 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 3201 were constitutional in favor of appellee Department of Public Welfare. The court found that the right at issue was the right to have the state subsidize the individual exercise of a constitutionally protected right, when it chose to subsidize alternative constitutional rights. The court held that because § 3201 did not affect a fundamental right or a suspect class, the interest of the state did not need to be compelling in order to sustain the burden of justifying the disparate treatment of two classes of women. On appeal, the court affirmed the decree, and found that appellants did not establish that the commonwealth's denial of funds would fail to accomplish its purpose of preserving the life of the unborn child. Lastly, the court held that § 3201 did not violate Pa. Const. art. I § 28 because it did not accord varying benefits to men and women because of their sex, but accorded varying benefits to one class of women, as distinct from another, based on a voluntary choice made by the women.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's final decree, which held that the restriction of abortion funding to life endangering situations was constitutional and permanently enjoined appellee the Department of Public Welfare from enforcing the rape and incest reporting provisions. The court rejected appellants', taxpayer and medical assistance recipients, theory and found that the funding restriction did not violate the Pennsylvania Constitution.

Allegheny County v. Monzo


No. 5 and No. 6


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


509 Pa. 26; 500 A.2d 1096; 1985 Pa. LEXIS 420


September 17, 1985, Argued  
November 13, 1985, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Nos. 2378 C.D. 1984; 2422 C.D. 1984

Appeal from the order entered July 17, 1984, in the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny county, Civil Division at No. GD 81-5649, Nos. 5 W.D. Appeal Docket 1985; 6 W.D. Appeal Docket 1985.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant county challenged an order of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County (Pennsylvania), which found that appellant's 1 percent hotel room rental tax ordinance was unconstitutional and dismissed the complaint in assumpsit in favor of appellee motel.


OVERVIEW: Appellant county sought review of an order that found appellant's 1 percent hotel room rental tax unconstitutional. The court found that appellee motel was subject to penalties for noncompliance with the ordinance; that the ordinance placed motels competing with out-of-county establishments at an economic disadvantage; and that a substantial portion of the class taxed was afforded no benefits whatsoever. The court held that appellee had standing to challenge the constitutionality of the ordinance because there was sufficient evidence whereby the fact finder could reasonably have concluded that appellee was significantly affected and harmed by the operation of the tax. The court affirmed the order, holding that a substantial portion of the class taxed was afforded no benefits whatsoever while being significantly burdened. The court further held that the ordinance excluded all other counties from the tax authorization and prevented them from enjoying the benefit, which inured to appellant. Moreover, the court found that the ordinance was a special tax and violated Pa. Cons. Art. 3, § 32 which prohibited the passing of a local law that could be provided for by general law.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order finding appellant county's 1 percent hotel rental tax ordinance unconstitutional. The court found that the ordinance was unequal in its operation or effect upon similar businesses and was an appropriation of money from one group to the benefit of another.

Commonwealth v. Lesko


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


509 Pa. 67; 501 A.2d 200; 1985 Pa. LEXIS 421


September 16, 1985, Argued  
November 12, 1985, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Order Entered June 11, 1985 in Court of Common Pleas of Westmoreland County, Pennsylvania, Criminal Division, at No. 681 C of 1980. No. 42 Western District Appeal Docket, 1985.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged the order of the Court of Common Pleas of Westmoreland County (Pennsylvania), which denied his petition for postconviction relief from a death sentence. He argued that the court lacked jurisdiction over his appeal under the Post-Conviction Hearing Act, 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. §§ 9541-9551. On the merits, appellant argued that he was denied the right to withdraw a guilty plea and that his sentence was disproportionate.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted of first degree murder. During sentencing, the trial court admitted appellant's guilty plea in another killing. Appellant was sentenced to death, with his plea used as an aggravating circumstance. In two prior appeals, appellant had unsuccessfully challenged the use of his guilty plea and the proportionality of his sentence. Under the Post-Conviction Hearing Act (PCHA), 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 9543(4) and § 9549, if an issue had already been litigated by the court, postconviction relief was not available. Appellant's other arguments had been waived. The court addressed the jurisdictional issue at length. The Judicial Code, 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 722, gave the court exclusive jurisdiction over all direct appeals of capital cases. Once, the code had also placed exclusive jurisdiction for PCHA cases and felonious homicides with the court. Amendments to the code now placed exclusive jurisdiction for collateral attacks under the PCHA with the Superior Court under 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 742. The court retained exclusive jurisdiction over direct appeals of capital cases and discretionary jurisdiction to hear new issues arising in unusual PCHA cases.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the Court of Common Pleas because all of the issues had been decided on the merits or waived in other appeals. The court had taken this Post-Conviction Hearing Act (PCHA) appeal under its discretionary jurisdiction as a matter of judicial economy. Recent amendments to the Judicial Code had given the Superior Court exclusive jurisdiction over collateral attacks in PCHA cases.

Commonwealth v. Holcomb


No. 41 WESTERN DISTRICT APPEAL DOCKET, 1983


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


508 Pa. 425; 498 A.2d 833; 1985 Pa. LEXIS 465


September 10, 1984, Argued  
October 4, 1985, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Denied January 8, 1986.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Judgment of Sentence of Court of Common Pleas of Beaver County, Pennsylvania (Criminal Division) entered May 25, 1983, at Nos. 114, 115 and 115A of 1982.



DISPOSITION: The sentence is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged the judgment of sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Beaver County (Pennsylvania), which convicted him of multiple offenses, including first degree murder, and sentenced him to death. Among appellant's many issues on appeal were whether his Miranda rights were violated, whether the trial court erred by refusing to grant a motion to change venue, and whether the commonwealth's death penalty laws were constitutional.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted of kidnapping, theft, rape, and first degree murder. Appellant argued that his Miranda rights were violated during questioning. The court found that although appellant was the focus of the investigation when he came in for questioning, he was not under arrest and not entitled to Miranda warnings. Appellant argued that he should have been granted a change of venue because of pretrial publicity. The court found that the trial court did not abuse its discretion by denying a change of venue. There was no showing that the empanelled jurors were prejudiced. The pretrial reporting was factual, not inflammatory. The effect of the publicity had dissipated over time. Appellant argued that the commonwealth's death penalty laws were unconstitutional and that the evidence did not support its use. The court noted that the death penalty statute, 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 9711, had been previously held constitutional. Defendant's single conviction for what were in fact several crimes met the requirement that the jury rely on more than one prior conviction to apply the death penalty. The other aggravating factor, that the murder occurred in commission of a felony, was met.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the conviction and death sentence. When appellant was not under arrest, he was not entitled to Miranda warnings. Pretrial publicity did not require a change of venue because the empanelled jurors were not prejudiced, the reporting was not inflammatory, and the effect was dissipated by time. The death penalty statute's requirement that jurors balance aggravating and mitigating factors met constitutional muster.
