Commonwealth v. Cross


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


508 Pa. 322; 496 A.2d 1144; 1985 Pa. LEXIS 395


September 11, 1984, Argued  
August 14, 1985, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Denied November 12, 1985.


PRIOR HISTORY: No. 64 W.D. Appeal Docket 1982

Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Beaver County, Pennsylvania, (Criminal v. Division) entered on December 1, 1982, at No. 565 of 1981



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of his conviction in the Court of Common Pleas of Beaver County (Pennsylvania) for three counts of murder of the first degree. The jury, in a separate hearing, found that the two aggravating circumstances outweighed any mitigating circumstances and sentenced defendant to death upon all three counts of murder.


OVERVIEW: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for three counts of murder of the first degree and upheld his sentence of death. The court held that the evidence proved beyond a reasonable doubt that defendant committed the murders with premeditation and malice aforethought. The jury had the right to disbelieve defendant's version of what happened. A warrantless seizure of the hair samples and the fingernail clippings and scrapings was reasonable to protect possible evidence connected with the deeds because defendant could have easily destroyed evidence. Pennsylvania's Death Penalty Statute, 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 9711, provided that if the jury unanimously found that the mitigating circumstances outweighed the aggravating circumstances, the sentence must be life. If the aggravating circumstances outweighed the mitigating circumstances, the sentence must be death. The statutory language did not render the statute mandatory. The court held that § 9711 clearly met the requirements of U.S. Const. amends. VIII and XIV by providing the sentencing authority with the means to give an individualized sentence that considered defendant's character and record and the circumstances of his offense.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions for three counts of murder of the first degree because the evidence proved beyond a reasonable doubt that defendant committed the murders with premeditation and malice aforethought. The court upheld defendant's sentence of death because Pennsylvania's Death Penalty Statute was constitutional and the sentence was not the product of passion, prejudice, or any other arbitrary factor.

Commonwealth v. Goins


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


508 Pa. 270; 495 A.2d 527; 1985 Pa. LEXIS 359


January 22, 1985, Argued  
July 11, 1985, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Later proceeding at Commonwealth v. Goins, 368 Pa. Super. 638, 531 A.2d 31, 1987 Pa. Super. LEXIS 8139 (1987)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 30 E.D. Appeal Docket 1984

Appeal from the Judgments of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas, Trial Division, Criminal Section, of Philadelphia County, Imposed on Information Nos. 2942 and 2943, August Session, 1981.
Commonwealth v. Goins, 11 Phila. 250, 1984 Phila. Cty. Rptr. LEXIS 83 (Pa. C.P., 1984)



DISPOSITION: Sentence vacated and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgments of sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania), which convicted defendant of first degree murder and imposed the death penalty under 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 9711(h). This was a direct appeal from the trial court in accordance with Pa. R. App. P. 1941 and 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. 722(4).


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of first degree murder and sentenced to death. The sole aggravating circumstance introduced by the prosecution was a prior conviction of second degree murder. The trial court held that the past conviction supported a jury finding that defendant had a substantial history of felony convictions involving the use or threat of violence to the person, under 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 9711(d)(9). On direct appeal, the court affirmed the conviction because the evidence clearly supported the finding of guilty, and trial counsel was not ineffective because he declined to pursue lines of defense that were incredible and fantastic and concentrated on the theory originally put forth by defendant. The court reversed the death sentence after it applied the rules of construction to determine that a significant history of violent felonies was not the equivalent of a history of significant felony convictions, and that the legislature did not intend that a single past offense, although serious, would be considered to be an aggravating circumstance. The court remanded the case so that the trial court could impose a life sentence.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction of first degree murder but reversed the imposition of the death sentence and remanded for imposition of a sentence of life imprisonment, because a single prior conviction of murder was not a significant history of convictions of violent felonies and did not support a finding of an aggravating circumstance needed for the death penalty.

Commonwealth v. Bellis


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


508 Pa. 122; 494 A.2d 1072; 1985 Pa. LEXIS 390


January 22, 1985, Argued  
June 28, 1985, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 118 Appeal Docket 1984

Appeal from the January 20, 1984 Order of the Superior Court at No. 178, October Term, 1978, Reversing the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County, Trial Division, Criminal Section, at October Session, 1976, No. 1203 and Remanding for a New Trial.

  Pa.Super. , 472 A.2d 194 (1984).

 324 Pa. Superior Ct. 506, 472 A.2d 194 (1984) 



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The commonwealth appealed an order of the Superior Court (Pennsylvania), which granted defendant a new trial due to error committed by the trial judge in instructing the jury as to the circumstances under which defendant could be found guilty of the common law crimes of misfeasance, malfeasance and nonfeasance while in office.


OVERVIEW: The jury found defendant guilty of the common law crimes of misfeasance, malfeasance, and nonfeasance while he served as a city councilman. Defendant appealed his conviction alleging, inter alia, that the trial court erred in charging the jury that he could be found guilty of the common law crimes if they found that he had breached the positive statutory duty imposed by the Philadelphia Home Rule Charter, 351 Pa. Code § 10.10-100, the breach of which carried a penal sanction. The court affirmed the appellate court's conclusion that the trial court erred. The court held that a common law offense could not include a statutory offense that provided a separate penalty for its violation as an element, where culpability could be based solely on violation of the statutory proscription. The court found that the trial court erred in instructing the jury that defendant could be found guilty of the common law offense if it were found that he breached the positive duty set forth in the statute. The court added that, where a trial court charges both correctly and incorrectly on the same proposition, it is impossible to determine which instruction the jury followed and reversal is necessary.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the appellate court granting defendant a new trial due to error committed by the trial judge in instructing the jury that defendant could be found guilty of the common law crimes of misfeasance, malfeasance and nonfeasance while in office if the jury found that he had breached a positive statutory duty that provided a separate penalty for its violation.

Commonwealth v. Allen


No. 40 E.D. Appeal Docket 1984


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


508 Pa. 114; 494 A.2d 1067; 1985 Pa. LEXIS 321


April 17, 1985, Submitted  
June 27, 1985, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia, Trial Division, Criminal Section, dated February 8, 1984, entered at No. 41 March Term, 1983.



DISPOSITION: Accordingly, the judgment of sentence is vacated and the matter is remanded to the court of common pleas for resentencing pursuant to 42 Pa. C.S. §9714.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The commonwealth appealed from a judgment of sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia (Pennsylvania), which held that § 9714 of the Mandatory Minimum Sentencing Act, 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 9714, was unconstitutional because it did not require prior convictions to be proven beyond a reasonable doubt. The trial court imposed a sentence that was less than the minimum under § 9714.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted in the trial court of aggravated assault, reckless endangerment, and possession of an instrument of crime. The trial court concluded that § 9714 of the Mandatory Minimum Sentencing Act, 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 9714, which mandated a five-year minimum sentence if the defendant had a prior conviction of an enumerated violent felony within the preceding seven years, was unconstitutional. The trial court held that the prior conviction was an element of the current offense that should proven beyond a reasonable doubt, and declined to apply it. On appeal, the court reversed and remanded because it held that the prior conviction was not an element of the offense, but rather a sentencing restriction that removed the judge's discretion as to the minimum sentence but did not change the maximum sentence. The court found that the commonwealth's interest in protecting the public by punishing violent repeat offenders and deterring violent crime was at least as great as a convicted defendant's interest in leniency. The court held that the preponderance standard prescribed in § 9714 satisfied the minimum requirements of due process.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court and held that proof of a prior conviction for mandatory minimum sentencing purposes was not an element of the instant offense, so that proof by a preponderance of the evidence satisfied due process. The court remanded for resentencing in accordance with the mandatory minimum requirements.

Commonwealth v. Pursell


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


508 Pa. 212; 495 A.2d 183; 1985 Pa. LEXIS 393


September 11, 1984, Argued  
June 26, 1985, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Denied September 24, 1985.


PRIOR HISTORY: No. 15 W.D. Appeal Docket 1984, Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Erie County, Criminal Division, at No. 1016 of 1981, entered February 1, 1984.



DISPOSITION: For the foregoing reasons, we sustain the conviction of murder of the first degree and affirm the sentence of death. 15



15 The Prothonotary of the Western District is directed to transmit the full and complete record of the trial, sentencing hearing, imposition of sentence and review by this Court to the Governor. 42 Pa.C.S. §9711(i).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged the judgment of the Court of Common Pleas of Erie County (Pennsylvania), which convicted appellant of murder of the first degree and sentenced appellant to death.


OVERVIEW: Appellant sought review of his conviction for murder in the first degree and death sentence, and the court sustained the conviction and affirmed the sentence. Evidence sufficiently supported the murder conviction; appellant's glasses were found at the crime scene and blood on his shoes matched the victim's blood. It was not error to deny appellant's motion for change of venue because any sensational publicity about appellant was published for a three-day period and followed by a six-month "cooling off period" that dissipated any resulting prejudice. Appellant was not denied effective assistance of counsel by counsel's failure to object to appellee's comments regarding the death penalty; such comments were not reversible error unless they prevented the jury from weighing evidence objectively. It was not error to deny a mistrial when appellee's witness volunteered prejudicial testimony because the jury was promptly instructed to disregard. The "torture" requirement for aggravating circumstances found in 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 9711(d)(8) was not unconstitutionally vague because the term was commonly understood, and the jury instructions and evidence submitted on torture were sufficient.


OUTCOME: The court sustained appellant's conviction for murder in the first degree and affirmed the death sentence. Evidence supported the conviction; venue was proper because a "cooling off period" had followed prejudicial publicity; questionable comments from appellee and witnesses did not prejudice appellant; the torture requirement for aggravating circumstances was not vague; and related jury instructions and evidence were proper and sufficient.

Commonwealth v. Lutz


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


508 Pa. 297; 495 A.2d 928; 1985 Pa. LEXIS 399


March 5, 1985, Argued; March 5, 1985, Submitted  
June 26, 1985, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 60 W.D. Misc. Docket, 1984, Application for Extraordinary Relief and/or Petition for Review from the Order dated September 4, 1984, entered by the Court of Common Pleas of Westmoreland County, Pennsylvania, Criminal Division at No. 492 C 1984.

No. 61 W.D. Misc. Docket, 1984, Application for Extraordinary Relief and/or Petition for Review from the Order dated September 18, 1984, entered by the Court of Common Pleas of Westmoreland County, Pennsylvania, Criminal Division, at No. 1459 C 1983.

No. 64 W.D. Misc. Docket, 1984, Application for Extraordinary Relief and/or Petition for Review from the Order dated September 11, 1984, entered by the Court of Common Pleas of Westmoreland County,  [***2] Pennsylvania, Criminal Division, at No. 1212 C 1984.

DISPOSITION: We conclude, therefore, that the rules of this Court governing ARD, as modified by Section 1337 (d) (prohibiting the attorney for the Commonwealth [***3] from moving certain cases to ARD), are the sole applicable procedure by which a person charged with drunk driving may be admitted to ARD, and that no criminal defendant, including a person charged with drunk driving, may move his own admission to ARD.

                                   ORDER

 


The orders in No. 61 (Nestor) and 62 (Cline) directing the district attorneys to present the cases for ARD consideration are vacated, and the adjudication of guilt on the charges brought in these cases is reinstated. No. 61 and No. 62 are remanded for sentencing under the act.

The orders in No. 63 (Frick) and No. 64 (Conway) directing that the defendants be accepted into the ARD program are vacated and the cases are remanded for trial.

The orders in No. 60 (Lutz), No. 66 (Giamp), No. 75 (Faust), No. 76 (Emert), and No. 77 (MaCallister) directing that the defendants be considered for ARD are vacated and the cases are remanded for trial.

All appeals to Superior Court are quashed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: On application by the commonwealth's attorneys, the court assumed plenary jurisdiction of cases, in various procedural postures, from the Courts of Common Pleas for Westmoreland and Allegheny Counties (Pennsylvania) to determine whether, in a prosecution for drunk driving under 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 3731, a defendant could be admitted to accelerated rehabilitative disposition over the objection of the attorney for the commonwealth.


OVERVIEW: In all nine cases, consolidated by the court, the attorneys for the commonwealth had declined to move for the defendant's admission into the accelerated rehabilitative disposition (ARD) program, but the court, over the objection of the commonwealth and upon motion of defense counsel, ordered that defendants be considered for admission to the program or, in two cases, actually ordered that defendants be admitted to ARD. On application of attorneys for the commonwealth, the court assumed plenary jurisdiction to determine whether, in a prosecution for drunk driving under 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 3731, a defendant may be admitted to ARD over the objection of the attorney for the commonwealth. The court quashed all appeals to the superior court. The court held that district attorneys, under the rules promulgated by the court, had the sole discretion to move for the admission of a defendant into ARD and the failure to have submitted a case for ARD had not imposed a sentence any more than the exercise of prosecutorial discretion in deciding to prosecute the case imposed a sentence. The court held that admission to an ARD program was not a matter of right, but a privilege.


OUTCOME: The court quashed all appeals to the superior court because the rules governing accelerated rehabilitative disposition (ARD) were the sole procedure by which a person charged with drunk driving may have been admitted to ARD, and no criminal defendant could have moved for their own admission to ARD.

Commonwealth v. Morales

Post-conviction proceeding at, Decision reached on appeal by Commonwealth v. Pirela, 593 Pa. 312, 929 A.2d 629, 2007 Pa. LEXIS 1681 (Aug. 20, 2007)
Habeas corpus proceeding at, Magistrate's recommendation at Pirela v. Vaughn, 2013 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 190604 (E.D. Pa., Apr. 29, 2013)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 135 E.D. Appeal Dkt. 1983, Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County, Criminal Division, dated November 3, 1983, October Term, 1982 at Nos. 1292-1295.
Commonwealth v. Tirado, 341 Pa. Super. 620, 491 A.2d 922, 1985 Pa. Super. LEXIS 6078 (Feb. 27, 1985)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: By automatic appeal pursuant to 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. §§ 9711(h)(1) and 722(4) and Pa. R. App. P. 702(b), appellant challenged the judgment of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania), which convicted appellant of murder of the first degree, criminal conspiracy, and possession of an instrument of crime. Appellant was sentenced to death for the murder charge and imprisonment for the other charges.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted of murder in the first degree, criminal conspiracy, and possession of an instrument of crime, and he was sentenced to death for the murder charge. Appellant challenged the conviction and sentence and the court affirmed. Under 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 9711(h), no circumstances warranted vacating appellant's death sentence. Moreover, the trial court did not abuse its discretion in denying appellant's motion to sever the consolidated trial with his codefendant. Pa. R. Crim. P. 1127 permitted defendants who participated in the same act to be tried together; evidence against appellant and his codefendant was virtually identical; and appellant failed to articulate the nature of prejudice he would suffer. It was not error to give appellant 10 peremptory challenges instead of the 20 he would have received in a nonconsolidated trial because appellant did not allege or demonstrate prejudice. Nor was it error to refuse to instruct the jury that appellant's murder conviction in a collateral matter, which the jury found to be an aggravating circumstance, was not final. The conviction's nonfinality did not alter its character as an aggravating circumstance.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed appellant's convictions of murder in the first degree, criminal conspiracy, and possession of an instrument of crime and his death and imprisonment sentences. Appellant did not show prejudice from being tried with his codefendant or from receiving fewer peremptory challenges than normal, and lack of jury instruction on a nonfinal collateral conviction was not error where the conviction was still an aggravating circumstance.

Commonwealth v. Wright

508 Pa. 25; 494 A.2d 354; 1985 Pa. LEXIS 338


December 6, 1984, Argued  
June 10, 1985, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Writ of certiorari granted McMillan v. Pennsylvania, 474 U.S. 815, 88 L. Ed. 2d 47, 106 S. Ct. 58, 1985 U.S. LEXIS 3187 (1985)

PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 4 E.D. Appeal Docket 1984 Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Chester County, Criminal Division, Dated January 24, 1983, entered at No. 1444 of 1982

No. 28 E.D. Appeal Docket 1984 Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia, Trial Division, Criminal Section, dated January 30, 1984, entered at No. 2544 December Term, 1982

Commonwealth v. Dennison, 10 Phila. 531, 1984 Phila. Cty. Rptr. LEXIS 49 (Pa. C.P., 1984)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants sought review of decisions from the Court of Common Pleas of Chester County and the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia (Pennsylvania), which had convicted each of the five defendants for one of the enumerated offenses found in 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 9712.


OVERVIEW: Each of five defendants was convicted of an enumerated offense in 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 9712. In four cases, the trial court held the section was unconstitutional and declined to apply it because the preponderance standard violated due process. In the remaining case, defendant was sentenced to a five-year term of imprisonment. On appeal, the court affirmed the conviction and remanded the other four cases so that § 9712 would be applied. The court found that the legislature had explicitly provided in § 9712(b) that the provisions of the section were not an element of the crime, and that because the legislature was charged with defining the elements of crimes, it was within their power to have done so. The court held that § 9712 merely mandated a minimum sentence of five years where defendant stood convicted of a serious felony upon proof beyond a reasonable doubt, and therefore his right to remain free from confinement had been extinguished. The court further held that § 9712 had satisfied the minimum requirements of U.S. Const. amend. X IV, § 1 and was therefore constitutional.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed one conviction and remanded the other four because the section in question had satisfied the minimum requirements of the due process clause and was therefore constitutional, where defendant's fundamental right, i.e., freedom from confinement, had already been forfeited and the determination to be made would at most have had bearing only on the duration of confinement.

Commonwealth v. Colson


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


507 Pa. 440; 490 A.2d 811; 1985 Pa. LEXIS 397


October 25, 1984, Argued  
April 4, 1985, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 124 E.D. Appeal Docket 1983

Appeal from judgment of conviction on criminal information numbers 2271 and 2271-01 of 1980, and from judgment of sentence of death in the Bucks County Court of Common Pleas, October 27, 1983.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of sentence of death for murder of the first degree imposed by the Court of Common Pleas of Bucks County (Pennsylvania).


OVERVIEW: Defendant challenged a judgment of sentence imposing the death penalty for his conviction of murder in the first degree. The court affirmed the sentence, finding that although defendant was arrested more than three years after the crime; his due process rights were not violated because he had notice that he was a suspect, he departed from the commonwealth, and the police made diligent efforts to locate all of the suspects. The court also found that the trial court did not abuse its discretion in refusing defendant's challenge for cause of a juror who had a remote relationship to persons involved in the case. The court also held that the trial court properly excluded a potential juror who established an irrevocable commitment to vote against the death penalty. The court further held that the prosecutor did not engage in misconduct by making certain remarks in his opening and closing statements because the fact that the prosecutor had evidence that he chose not to present did not render the opening statement improper. The court, however, modified defendant's death penalty sentence to life imprisonment because he was convicted before the death penalty statute came into effect.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of sentence but modified the sentence from the death penalty to life imprisonment. The court found that the death penalty could not be imposed because he was convicted before the effective date of the death penalty statute.

Presbytery of Beaver-Butler of United Presbyterian Church v. Middlesex Presbyterian Church


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


507 Pa. 255; 489 A.2d 1317; 1985 Pa. LEXIS 309


September 13, 1984, Argued  
April 3, 1985, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 36 W.D. Appeal Dkt. 1984, Appeal from the Order of the Commonwealth Court dated February 10, 1984, No. 1740 C.D. 1982, Reversing the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Butler County, No. 81-024, 80 Pa. Cmwlth. Ct. 211, 471 A.2d 1271 (1984).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant church sought review of a judgment from the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania), which overturned a holding from the trial court and granted judgment for appellee national church to take control or ownership of appellant's local church property after appellant left the national church.


OVERVIEW: Appellant church was founded individually and only later joined appellee national church. When appellant disaffiliated from appellee, appellee sought delivery of all church property, an accounting of all church assets, and an injunction against the seceding members from in any way using or dissipating the assets of the church. The trial court granted summary judgment for appellants, finding no original agreement of trust placing control or ownership of the church property in appellee. The appeals court overturned that holding finding an intent to create a trust in appellee. On appeal, the court reversed judgment. The court held that there are neutral principles of law, developed for use in all property disputes, that could have been applied without "establishing" churches to which property was awarded, and U.S. Const. amend I commanded civil courts to decide church property disputes without resolving underlying controversies over religious doctrine. The court held that like the trial court, they had found no evidence that appellant had ever intended to convey their property interests to appellee because they had always retained all property in their own corporate name.


OUTCOME: The court overturned the decision for appellant church, finding that no evidence was cited that appellant had ever intended to convey their property interests to appellee national church and that to the contrary, throughout their entire affiliation appellant had retained all property in their own corporate name.

Commonwealth v. Mason


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


507 Pa. 396; 490 A.2d 421; 1985 Pa. LEXIS 345


January 22, 1985, Argued  
April 3, 1985, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 127 E.D. Appeal Docket 1984

Appeal from an Order of the Superior Court of Pennsylvania ( No. 175 Philadelphia, 1982), dated April 13, 1984, reversing the case and remanding same for a new trial in the Court of Common Pleas of Montgomery County, Pennsylvania, Criminal Division at No. 1796-78.

 Pa. Super. 476 A.2d 389 (1984) 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant commonwealth sought review of an order from the Superior Court of Pennsylvania, which reversed the judgments of sentence from the trial court for appellee defendant and remanded for a new trial.


OVERVIEW: Appellee defendant was convicted of burglary and criminal mischief. The trial court denied his postverdict motions. The superior court reversed the judgments of sentence and remanded for a new trial because the search of his apartment in one county by police from another county had violated Pa. R. Crim. P. 2004 and the evidence seized should have been suppressed. The court reversed for appellant commonwealth. The court held that the lower court erred in concluding that the search violated Rule 2004 and even if it had been violated, suppression of the evidence seized would have been a remedy all out of proportion to the benefits gained. The court found that while Rule 2004 required a law enforcement officer having primary jurisdiction to have actually had "participated" in the service, the requirement had been met by the presence of "jurisdictional police officers" at appellee's apartment, so his rights were not prejudiced by the officer's failure to fully comply with the rule. The court remanded the case because while the superior court addressed most of the issues and resolved them adversely to appellee, at least one issue had not been addressed.


OUTCOME: The court reversed judgment for appellee defendant because the requirement that the search warrant be served by a police officer had been met where the jurisdictional officers were present at the time of service. The court remanded for one issue that had remained unresolved.

Leonard v. Thornburgh


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


507 Pa. 317; 489 A.2d 1349; 1985 Pa. LEXIS 379


January 24, 1985, Argued  
April 3, 1985, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Commonwealth Court dated May 31, 1984 at No. 1724 C.D. 1983.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant secretary of revenue challenged the order of the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania), which found in favor of appellee resident and declared unconstitutional the Philadelphia Non-Resident Wage Tax Cap, Tax Reform Code of 1971, 72 P.S. § 7359(b) (1984) and Philadelphia, Pa., Ordinance 1716 (1983).


OVERVIEW: Appellee resident was assessed higher wage taxes than she would have been if she were a non-resident. Appellee challenged the Philadelphia Non-Resident Wage Tax Cap (cap), Tax Reform Code of 1971, 72 P.S. § 7359(b) (1984) and Philadelphia, Pa., Ordinance (ordinance) 1716 (1983). The cap limited to 4 5/16 percent the rate at which non-residents could be taxed by on income earned in the city. The ordinance levied the city's wage and net profits tax at the rates of 4 5/16 percent on non-residents and 4 96/100 percent on residents. The commonwealth court declared the cap and ordinance unconstitutional. Appellant secretary of revenue sought review. The court reversed because the wage tax scheme complied with the uniformity requirements of the equal protection clause and Pa. Const. art. VIII, § I. The court found a legitimate distinction between the taxed classes in addition to mere residence. The court held that non-residents and residents were not similarly situated because non-residents utilized the city's police, fire, and sanitation services to a lesser extent and did not enjoy a voice in the city council.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court in favor of appellant secretary of revenue because the differential tax treatment of appellee resident and non-residents was justified by a legitimate distinction between the classes and complied with the equal protection and state uniformity clauses.

County of Allegheny v. Commonwealth


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


507 Pa. 360; 490 A.2d 402; 1985 Pa. LEXIS 372


January 24, 1985, Argued  
March 29, 1985, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 47 W.D. Appeal Docket 1984 Appeal from the Order of the Commonwealth Court dated May 15, 1984, entered at No. 3649 C.D. 1983, denying Appellants' Motion for Preliminary Injunction.

No. 82 W.D. Appeal Docket 1984 Appeal from the Order of the Commonwealth Court dated September 6, 1984, entered at No. 3649 C.D. 1983, sustaining Appellees' Preliminary Objections and dismissing Appellants' Petition for Review   Pa. Comw.  , 480 A.2d 1330 (1984) 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, county and county prison board, challenged orders of the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania), which denied appellants' motion for preliminary injunction and sustained appellee state agencies' and associated individuals' preliminary objections in the form of a demurrer in appellants' case that sought help with county jail overcrowding.


OVERVIEW: Appellants, county and county prison board, filed in the commonwealth court a complaint in mandamus and equity and a motion for a preliminary injunction against appellees, state agencies and associated individuals, following appellees' refusal to authorize the transfer of 76 inmates from the county jail to state prisons so that appellants could comply with a federal district court order to reduce the jail population. Appellees filed preliminary objections in the nature of a demurrer. The commonwealth court denied appellants' motion and sustained appellees' preliminary objections that dismissed appellants' complaint. The court reversed. The court held that it was the state's obligation to maintain order and preserve the safety and welfare of all citizens; and, as appellees did not place the disputed prisoners in state custody by their own discretion, they had a duty to transfer the prisoners when appellants were unable to meet constitutional standards because of overcrowding. The court also found that the overcrowding and failure of appellees to transfer prisoners warranted the preliminary injunction.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the lower court. It was appellees', state agencies and associated individuals, duty to assist appellants, county and county prison board, in dealing with overcrowding by transferring state prisoners to state facilities; therefore, a preliminary injunction mandating transfer was proper.

In re Petition for Enforcement of Subpoenas to John Doe Corps. a


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


507 Pa. 137; 489 A.2d 182; 1985 Pa. LEXIS 302


October 30, 1984, Argued

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant companies challenged an order of the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania), which denied their preliminary objections to appellee crime commission's subpoenas issued pursuant to the Pennsylvania Crime Commission Act, 71 P.S. § 1190.7 and enforced through the Administrative Code, 71 P.S. § 200.


OVERVIEW: Appellant companies refused to comply with subpoenas served by appellee crime commission pursuant to the Pennsylvania Crime Commission Act (act), 71 P.S. § 1190.1 et seq., for production of documents. Appellee sought enforcement by the commonwealth court, and appellant filed preliminary objections in the nature of a demurrer. The commonwealth court sought to preserve appellee's subpoena power through application of the Administrative Code, 71 P.S. § 200. Appellants alleged that 71 P.S. § 1190.7 was unconstitutional for failing to provide a procedure for court enforcement of appellee's subpoenas as a prerequisite to a finding of contempt. The court, on plenary jurisdiction, exercised its power under Pa. Const. art. V, § 10(c), by suspending § 1190.7 and adopting § 200 as an interim procedure for enforcement of appellee's subpoena power. As the court acted prospectively, it required appellee to reissue the subpoenas.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court by adopting use of the administrative code provision to enforce appellee crime commission's subpoenas as an interim procedure; therefore, appellee was required to reissue the subpoenas to appellant companies.

Commonwealth v. Johnson


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


507 Pa. 27; 487 A.2d 1320; 1985 Pa. LEXIS 498


Submitted December 6, 1984  
February 21, 1985 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant commonwealth sought review from a judgment of the Superior Court of Pennsylvania, which affirmed the trial court's ruling that it had no authority to grant use immunity over the objection of the prosecutor in a case where defendant was convicted of criminal conspiracy, possession of instruments of a crime, terroristic threats, simple assault, and rape.


OVERVIEW: Appellant commonwealth challenged the trial court's ruling that it had no authority to grant use immunity over the objection of the prosecutor in a case where defendant was convicted of criminal conspiracy, possession of instruments of a crime, terroristic threats, simple assault, and rape. The commonwealth argued that witness immunity in Pennsylvania was governed by statute. Under the statute, courts have no power to grant immunity except on request of the prosecutor. Defendant sought a request from the prosecuting attorney for immunity for the compelled testimony of a defense witness. The district attorney declined, and the court had no authority to immunize the witness' testimony absent a request by the prosecutor. The commonwealth had a strong interest in prosecuting both the appellant and the witness, and it refused to go along with the immunity request. Therefore, the court held that the trial court was correct in concluding that it lacked inherent power to grant judicial use immunity to the co-defendant. The court affirmed the order.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's order in favor of appellant commonwealth and against defendant convicted of criminal conspiracy, possession of instruments of a crime, terroristic threats, simple assault, and rape. The court held that the trial court was correct in concluding that it lacked inherent power to grant judicial use immunity to the co-defendant witness.

Pennsylvania Liquor Control Bd. v. Spa Athletic Club


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


506 Pa. 364; 485 A.2d 732; 1984 Pa. LEXIS 357


September 11, 1984, Argued  
December 18, 1984, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 5 W.D. Appeal Docket 1984, Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Erie County entered on March 28, 1983 at No. 1482, 1982.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant Pennsylvania Liquor Control Board appealed the decision of the Court of Common Pleas of Erie County (Pennsylvania), which held unconstitutional a section of the Liquor Code, 47 P.S. § 1-102 (1968) which automatically revoked the license of appellee private club because appellee had been out of operation for two years. The case was remanded for a determination of whether appellee's liquor license should be revoked for other reasons.


OVERVIEW: Appellant Pennsylvania Liquor Control Board revoked appellee private club's liquor license under the Liquor Code, 47 P.S. § 1-102 (1968) because it had ceased operation for two years. Appellee filed suit. The appellate court held that § 1-102 violated the Equal Protection Clause under U.S. Const. amend. XIV and the Special Legislation Provision, Pa. Const. art. III, § 32. The appellate court found no rational basis for allowing inoperative commercial licensees an indefinite time period before revocation, but allowing inoperative club licensees only two years. The court reversed, and held that the liquor laws were rationally based in the state's legitimate interest of protecting public health and safety by regulating the sale of liquor to prevent its abuse. The court found that the lower court erred when it equated the absence of a statement of the reasons supporting § 1-102 with the absence of any rational reason. The court noted the many distinctions between club and nonclub licensees, which included the fact that the main purpose of commercial establishments was the sale of liquor for profit, while the sale of liquor was secondary to that of clubs.


OUTCOME: Appellant Pennsylvania Liquor Control Board's automatic revocation of appellee private club's liquor license under the statute was upheld because the statute's distinction between club licenses and commercial licenses was rationally based on the commonwealth's legitimate interest in protecting public health and safety by regulating the sale of liquor so as to prevent its abuse.

Hepps v. Philadelphia Newspapers


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


506 Pa. 304; 485 A.2d 374; 1984 Pa. LEXIS 356; 11 Media L. Rep. 1841


April 9, 1984, Argued  
December 14, 1984, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 18 E.D. Appeal Dkt. 1983, Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Chester County dated February 15, 1983, entered at No. 36 May Term, 1976.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, private corporations and individuals, sought review of an order of the Court of Common Pleas of Chester County (Pennsylvania), which held in favor of defendants, newspapers and reporters, in plaintiffs' civil action for libel, and held that 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 8343(b)(1) was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs, private corporations and individuals, brought a libel action against defendants, newspapers and reporters, for purporting to link plaintiffs to organized crime. The trial court held in favor of defendants. The court reversed the order and remanded the case for retrial. The court held that the defamatory character of the articles was apparent. The court held that the only restraint mandated by the U.S. Const. amend. I in plaintiffs' action for compensatory damages was that liability could not be imposed upon defendants without fault. The court held that defendants' constitutional freedom of expression was not offended by the burden of proving the truth of the articles about plaintiffs, as long as plaintiffs were required to establish defendants' willful or negligent conduct. The court held that the presumption of falsity remained, and defendants had the option of proving truth as an absolute defense to the action. The court held that 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 8343(b)(1) was constitutional and the trial court's jury instruction to the contrary was error. The court held that plaintiffs presented insufficient evidence of actual malice to support any issue of punitive damages.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's order and awarded a new trial to determine defendants', newspapers and reporters, liability and the assessment of compensatory damages. The court found that the First Amendment afforded plaintiffs, private corporations and individuals, a greater protection from defamation than public figures and officials and did not require plaintiffs to bear the burden of proving the falsity of defendants' articles.

No. 26 M.D. Appeal Docket 1984, Appeal from the Order of the Commonwealth Court dated March 7, 1984, entered at No. 2133 C.D. 1983

The court ruled that appellant's unilateral reduction of retirement benefits arising from the employment contracts unconstitutionally impaired the contract rights of all existing members of the system.

In re Upset Sale


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


505 Pa. 327; 479 A.2d 940; 1984 Pa. LEXIS 292


January 26, 1984, Argued  
June 28, 1984, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 75 E.D. Appeal Docket 1982, Appeal from the Orders of the Court of Common Pleas of Berks County dated July 16, 1982, October 12, 1982, and November 10, 1982


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant buyers, husband and wife, sought review of a final order of the Court of Common Pleas of Berks County (Pennsylvania), which invalidated a tax sale at which appellants purchased property; and it held that provisions of the Pennsylvania Real Estate Tax Sale Law, 72 P.S. § 5860.602 were unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Appellee estate, the holder of a judgment lien against property, filed objections to a tax sale at which appellant buyers, husband and wife, purchased the property. Appellee alleged that it had no notice of the sale from the county tax claim bureau which, pursuant to the Pennsylvania Real Estate Tax Sale Law, 72 P.S. § 5860.602, listed the property for sale in advertisements in a newspaper of general circulation and in the county legal journal. The trial court invalidated the sale and held that the notice provisions of § 5860.602 were unconstitutional. Appellants sought review. The appeals court transferred the case to the supreme court. The court affirmed. The court stated that a judgment is property, and a judgment creditor's interest can not be deprived without due process of law. The general notice provided for by § 5860.602 was inadequate to meet the requirements of due process as far as judgment creditors were concerned. When a judgment creditor was identified in a judgment that was publicly recorded, the constructive notice by publication had to be supplemented by notice mailed to the judgment creditor's last address, or by personal service.


OUTCOME: The trial court's order that invalidated the tax sale at which appellant buyers, husband and wife, purchased a property was affirmed. The constructive notice of the sale provided for by statute was inadequate to meet due process requirements as far as appellee estate, a judgment creditor, was concerned.

Cianfrani v. Commonwealth, State Employees' Retirement Bd.


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


505 Pa. 294; 479 A.2d 468; 1984 Pa. LEXIS 394


May 14, 1984, Submitted  
June 28, 1984, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant ex-employee challenged the decision of the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania), which sustained the demurrer of appellee State Employees' Retirement Board (board) and dismissed appellant's petition that sought interest on retirement benefits withheld by appellee in compliance with the Public Employee Pension Forfeiture Act, 43 P.S. § 1311 et seq., subsequently held by the court to be unconstitutional as applied to appellant.


OVERVIEW: In a prior action, the court held that the Public Employee Pension Forfeiture Act (act), 43 P.S. § 1311 et seq. was unconstitutional as applied to appellant ex-employee. Appellant filed a petition that sought interest on the benefits that were withheld. The appellate court dismissed appellant's petition. The court affirmed the appellate court's decision, and held that appellant's claim was unsupportable on either a statutory basis, a contractual basis, or as a matter of the common law. The court found that appellee State Employees' Retirement Board repaid the benefits, but did not pay the interest that accrued during the period benefits were withheld. The court held that until the act was declared unconstitutional, appellee's action was not only then lawful, but was affirmatively required by the act. Appellee's action could not be characterized as wrongful and, thus, the claim for interest could not proceed under the common law regarding the award of interest damages. The period during which appellee withheld payment was expressly beyond board's statutory and contractual duty to pay interest under the State Employees' Retirement Code, 71 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 5101 et seq.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the appellate court's decision to dismiss appellant ex-employee's petition that sought interest for benefits that were unconstitutionally withheld from him by appellee State Employees' Retirement Board because appellant's claim was unsupportable on either a statutory basis, a contractual basis, or as a matter of common law.

Arsenal Coal Co. v. Commonwealth


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


505 Pa. 198; 477 A.2d 1333; 1984 Pa. LEXIS 276; 14 ELR 20550


April 12, 1984, Argued  
May 31, 1984, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 17 E.D. Appeal Docket, 1983, Appeal from the Order of the Commonwealth Court, dated January 13, 1983, and docketed at No. 1937 C.D. 1982, 71 Pa. Commonwealth Ct. 187; 454 A.2d 658 (1983).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant mine operators sought review of an order of the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania), which sustained the preliminary objections of appellee Department of Environmental Resources to appellants' petition to enjoin appellee from implementing or enforcing regulations adopted by the Environmental Quality Board.


OVERVIEW: Appellant mine operators petitioned to enjoin appellee Department of Environmental Resources from implementing or enforcing regulations adopted by the Environmental Quality Board and appearing at Pa. Code chs. 86, 88. Appellants claimed that the regulations exceeded appellee's rulemaking power under the Surface Mining Conservation and Reclamation Act, 52 P.S. § 1396.1 et seq. Appellants' petition was denied, and appellants sought review. The court reversed and remanded. The court rejected the trial court's holding that opportunity for review by the Environmental Hearing Board upon piecemeal application and enforcement of the challenged regulations was an adequate remedy. Where the effects of the challenged regulations upon the industry was direct and immediate, the justiciability of the challenge in advance of enforcement was established. Thus the trial court erred when it declined to exercise its equitable jurisdiction and dismissed appellants' petition.


OUTCOME: The appellate court's order that sustained the preliminary objections of appellee Department of Environmental Resources to appellant mine operators' petition to enjoin appellee from implementing or enforcing regulations adopted by the Environmental Quality Board was reversed. The matter was remanded because the trial court erred when it declined to exercise jurisdiction over a pre-enforcement challenge to the validity of the regulations.

James v. Southeastern Pennsylvania Transp. Authority


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


505 Pa. 137; 477 A.2d 1302; 1984 Pa. LEXIS 266


April 10, 1984, Argued  
May 24, 1984, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 88 E.D. Appeal Docket, 1983, Appeal from the Judgment of Superior Court of Pennsylvania at No. 2308 Philadelphia, 1981 dated March 11, 1983, reversing and remanding Order of the Court of Common Pleas - Civil Division Philadelphia, at No. 1395, May Term, 1977,   Pa. Super.  , 459 A.2d 338 (1983).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant Southeastern Pennsylvania Transportation Authority sought review of a decision of the Superior Court of Pennsylvania reversing the lower court's decision and finding that the six-month requirement of 66 P.S. § 2036 violated the Equal Protection Clause, U.S. Const. amend. XIV. Appellee plaintiff allegedly injured himself on appellant's premises but failed to give notice of his injury within the proscribed six-month period.


OVERVIEW: Appellee plaintiff allegedly injured himself on appellant transportation authority's premises but failed to give notice of his injury within the statutorily proscribed six-month period, 66 P.S. § 2036. Appellant sought review of the lower court's decision that the statute violated the equal protection clause, U.S. Const. amend. XIV. The court reversed the lower court's decision, finding that the statute was constitutional. The court noted that Pa. Const. art. I, § 11 granted the legislature authority to choose cases in which the commonwealth of Pennsylvania would have immunity from actions. The court also noted that the equal protection clause, U.S. Const. amend. XIV, created different standards of inquiry for analyzing disputed rules. The court noted that the heightened scrutiny standard was inapplicable because the right involved did not flow directly from the constitution, and instead found that the intermediate standard of review was appropriate because an important interest was implicated. Applying this standard, the court found that the purpose of the statute was an important one and it therefore passed muster under this statute.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the lower court's decision, finding that the statute was constitutional. The court noted that the state constitution authorized immunity in certain cases and found that the action fit into the intermediate standard of scrutiny under the federal constitution in that it implicated an important but not fundamental right. The court found that the statute served an important government purpose, meeting the intermediate standard.

Commonwealth v. Maxwell


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


505 Pa. 152; 477 A.2d 1309; 1984 Pa. LEXIS 268


October 20, 1983, Argued  
May 24, 1984, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 42 E.D. Appeal Dkt. 1982, Appeal from the Judgment of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia, Trial Division, Criminal Section Imposed on Information No. 1442, November Term, 1980


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, by automatic review to the Court pursuant to 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 9711(h)(1), challenged the judgment of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia (Pennsylvania), sentencing him to death. Appellant complained of a number of issues, including a violation of Pa. R. Crim. P. 1100, a warrantless search, marital privilege, prosecutorial remarks, and infliction of cruel and unusual punishment due to the imposition of the death penalty.


OVERVIEW: Appellant allegedly lured a salesman to his house, where the victim was robbed and shot to death. The trial court convicted appellant of first degree murder and sentenced him to death. Appellant challenged the conviction by automatic review to the court, pursuant to 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 9711(h)(1), and the court sustained the trial court conviction of first degree murder, affirmed the death sentence, and rejected all of appellant's claims. The court held that appellant's argument that Pa. R. Crim. P. 1100 was violated because appellee never communicated to appellant that he was wanted in the killing, was frivolous; appellant's U.S. Const. amend. IV rights were not violated by a police search consented to by appellant's 16 year old daughter; the trial court correctly allowed the live-in paramour to testify at trial, because appellant and her did not have a common-law marriage; the prosecutor's comments supposedly improperly disparaging appellant's trial counsel's ethics and trial tactics, under the circumstances, did not prejudice appellant; and the death penalty statute, 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 9711, was not unconstitutionally vague and did not inflict cruel and unusual punishment.


OUTCOME: The court sustained the trial court conviction of first degree murder, affirmed the death sentence, and rejected appellant's claims of a rule violation, a warrantless search, marital privilege, prosecutorial remarks, and the death penalty. The court reviewed the entire record, with all reasonable inferences in favor of appellee commonwealth, and held that the evidence was sufficient to sustain the convictions for murder in the first degree.

In re Jones


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


505 Pa. 50; 476 A.2d 1287; 1984 Pa. LEXIS 251


March 8, 1984, Argued  
May 9, 1984, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 16 M.D. Appeal Docket 1984, Appeal from the Order of the Commonwealth Court dated Feb. 22, 1984, entered at No. 345 C.D. 1984


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant candidate sought review of the order of the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania) that denied appellant the opportunity to run in the senatorial primary for having failed to establish that she was an inhabitant within the district within the meaning of Pa. Const. art. 2, § 5.


OVERVIEW: After the passage of a senate resolution that appellant candidate would not, if elected, be eligible to serve in the Pennsylvania senate, appellant sought review of the order of the commonwealth court that appellant was ineligible. The court ordered the Secretary of the Commonwealth to certify appellant as a candidate. The court held that Pa. Const. arts. 2, §§ 5, 9, placed the exclusive jurisdiction in the legislative body and divested the courts of all jurisdiction in the matter, and because jurisdiction arose from statute rather than common law, it could not be extended by implication. The court distinguished a challenge to appellant's right to run from a challenge to her right to be seated after her election, pursuant to the Pennsylvania Election Code, 25 P.S. § 2937 (1983-84), which was the sole remedy for challenging a person's right to run for political office. The court further found that the senate resolution was improper because the body had the right to determine its composition, and not that of a future senate.


OUTCOME: The court ordered the Secretary of the Commonwealth to certify appellant candidate's ballot position in a senatorial primary election because the courts lacked jurisdiction to entertain a challenge of appellant's right to run for office.

Commonwealth v. Beasley


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


504 Pa. 485; 475 A.2d 730; 1984 Pa. LEXIS 240


October 25, 1983, Argued  
April 18, 1984, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Related proceeding at Commonwealth v. Beasley, 505 Pa. 279, 479 A.2d 460, 1984 Pa. LEXIS 289 (1984)
Post-conviction proceeding at Com. v. Beasley, 377 Pa. Super. 648, 541 A.2d 1148, 1988 Pa. Super. LEXIS 413 (1988)
Habeas corpus proceeding at, Motion denied by Beasley v. Horn, 2009 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 22558 (E.D. Pa., Feb. 17, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 81-3-457, Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas, Criminal Trial Division, Philadelphia County, imposed on Information No. 2175 & 2177 July Session, 1980.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged the judgment of the Court of Common Pleas, Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania), which convicted him of first degree murder and imposed the death sentenced. Appellant was granted a direct appeal, pursuant to 42 P.S. § 9711(h), and contended that a new trial was warranted, primarily due to appellant's claim that the trial court erred in refusing to grant a mistrial, based on alleged prosecutor misconduct.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted of first degree murder and sentenced to death, in connection with the shooting death of a police officer. On direct appeal, pursuant to 42 P.S. § 9711(h), the court reviewed the record for errors at trial in addition to those alleged by appellant, and found none warranting relief. Therefore, the court affirmed the death sentence, holding that the trial court did not err in refusing to grant a mistrial. The court held that the prosecutor, during cross-examination of the only fact witness presented by the defense, discrediting the veracity of the witness, did not warrant the grant of a new trial. The prosecutor's comments did not constitute reversible error, because the unavoidable effect was not to prejudice the jury and form in their minds fixed bias and hostility toward appellant so that they could not weigh the evidence objectively and render a true verdict. Of great significance was the fact that the prosecutor's comments did not relate to the substance of the witness's primary testimony. Constitutional challenges to the death penalty statute, under 42 P.S. § 9711, and Pa. Const. art. I, § 13, were rejected by the court.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court death sentence for a first degree murder conviction, because appellant was not entitled to a new trial. The court held that the trial court did not err in refusing to grant a mistrial when the prosecutor, during cross-examination of the only defense presented fact witness, discredited his veracity. The court rejected other complaints about the prosecutor, plus constitutional challenges to the death penalty.

Heller v. Frankston


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


504 Pa. 528; 475 A.2d 1291; 1984 Pa. LEXIS 245


January 24, 1984, Argued  
April 18, 1984, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 58 M.D. Appeal Docket, 1983, Appeal from the Order of the Commonwealth Court dated August 11, 1983, entered at 460 C.D. 1981, vacating the Order of the Administrator of the Arbitration Panels for Health Care, dated February 3, 1981, entered at No. M76-0009. Pa. Commw.  , 464 A.2d 581 (1983).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant Administrator of the Arbitration Panels for Health Care challenged a decision by the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania), which reversed his decision that an attorney fees award in a health care action settlement was limited by the Health Care Services Malpractice Act, 40 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 1301.101.


OVERVIEW: Appellee attorneys participated in an action for medical malpractice that was covered by the Health Care Services Malpractice Act, 40 P.S. § 1301.101 et seq. (1975). After the appellees agreed to a settlement they petitioned the court of common pleas to approve the settlement and attorney fees. The court of common pleas approved the settlement and attorney fees. Appellant Administrator of the Arbitration Panels for Health Care reviewed the petition and determined that only those attorney fees provided under 40 P.S. § 1301-604(a) (1975) were available to the appellees. The lower court, which reviewed appellant's determination at the request of the state attorney general, vacated the decision of appellant. On appeal, the court affirmed and held that the entire statutory scheme for arbitration, including the provision limiting attorney fees, was struck down by a prior decision that determined that the legislative grant of jurisdiction for the arbitration scheme was void. Therefore appellant's actions were without a jurisdictional basis.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order that vacated the determination by appellant Administrator of the Arbitration Panels for Health Care of attorney because appellant did not have jurisdiction to determine the settlement and attorney fees since the statutory scheme for medical malpractice arbitration had been struck down as unconstitutional.

 

105 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Frey


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


504 Pa. 428; 475 A.2d 700; 1984 Pa. LEXIS 242


October 17, 1983, Argued  
April 13, 1984, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 81-3-393, Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence and the Verdict of Death of the Court of Common Pleas of Lancaster County at No. 169 of 1980


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a judgment from the Court of Common Pleas of Lancaster County (Pennsylvania) convicting him of first-degree murder and sentencing him to death. Defendant contended that an instruction to the jury did not follow the sentencing code, 42 P.S. § 9711(h), 42 P.S. § 9711(e), and that the trial court's instruction on lesser-included crimes introduced arbitrariness into the process.


OVERVIEW: Defendant confessed to hiring a hit man to kill his wife after she filed for divorce. The hit man hired an accomplice and beat and shot defendant's wife to death. At trial defendant's defense was that he paid the hit man money to stop threatening defendant and his family. Defendant was convicted of murder of the first degree and the jury sentenced defendant to death. The trial court in instructions to the jury. failed to precisely tract the language in the sentencing code, 42 P.S. § 9711(e), regarding mitigating factors and charged the jury on lesser degrees of homicide without evidence of the lesser charges. On appeal, the court affirmed the conviction even though the instructions did not track § 9711(e), because the mitigating circumstance law was adequately, accurately, and clearly expressed to the jury and did not inject prejudice into the process that produced the sentence of death. The court also held that the charge to the jury on lesser degrees of homicide did not introduce an element of arbitrariness into the process. The court also held that the evidence was sufficient and that the punishment was proportionate to other homicide cases.


OUTCOME: Defendant's conviction for first-degree murder and sentence of death were affirmed.

 

106 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Mikulan


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


504 Pa. 244; 470 A.2d 1339; 1983 Pa. LEXIS 772


December 1, 1983, Argued  
December 30, 1983, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 55 W.D. Appeal Docket, 1983, Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County, Criminal Division, at No. CC8303691A, dated July 20, 1983 granting Appellee's Motion to Dismiss Information


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The commonwealth appealed an order of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County (Pennsylvania), which granted appellee's motion to dismiss an information based on the unconstitutionality of the drunk driving law, pursuant to 75 P.S. § 3731(a)(4).


OVERVIEW: Appellee was arrested with violation of the motor vehicle code, 75 P.S. § 3731(a)(4), for drunk driving. The trial court dismissed the information against appellee, finding the drunk driving statute, § 3731(a)(4), void for vagueness. On direct appeal, the supreme court reversed and remanded, holding that the vagueness standard required appellee to demonstrate either that the statute failed to give a person of ordinary intelligence fair notice that contemplated conduct was forbidden or that it encouraged arbitrary and erratic enforcement, which appellee failed to prove. The court found that the legislature intended to impose absolute liability for drunk driving, that this met a compelling state interest in furtherance of its constitutional police powers for the protection of citizens, and that discretion of police, judges, and juries were limited by the statute; thus, the statute was not arbitrary, nor did it allow erratic enforcement.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded judgment in favor of appellant commonwealth because the state properly exercised its broad, constitutional police powers in creating the drunk driving statute to protect the safety of the highways and regulation of consumption of alcoholic beverages and because the statute was not void for vagueness.

Kapil v. Association of Pennsylvania State College & University Faculties


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


504 Pa. 92; 470 A.2d 482; 1983 Pa. LEXIS 785


October 25, 1983, Submitted  
December 30, 1983, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 76 E.D. Appeal Docket 1982, Appeal from the Order of the Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania in Case No. 2320 C.D. 1980,   Pa. Commw. Ct.  , 448 A.2d 717 (1982) 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant tenured faculty member sought review of an order of the Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania, which dismissed his complaint against appellees, Commonwealth of Pennsylvania, Department of Education, and Bloomsburg State College, on the basis of sovereign immunity in actions arising out of the denial of appellant's request for sabbatical leave.


OVERVIEW: Appellant tenured faculty member filed a complaint in equity against appellees, Commonwealth of Pennsylvania, Department of Education, and Bloomsburg State College, and others, arising out of appellee college's denial of his request for sabbatical leave. One count sought relief under 42 U.S.C.S. §§ 1983, 1985, for violation of his equal protection and due process rights. The second count averred the refusal to grant the sabbatical was a breach of a collective bargaining agreement. The lower court dismissed the complaint against appellees on the basis of sovereign immunity. On review the primary issue was whether appellees were entitled to sovereign immunity. The court reversed, holding that appellee's cause of action accrued prior to the 1978 passage of Act 152, 42 Pa. Cons. Ann. § 8522, because appellant applied for a sabbatical each year since 1974 and his applications were denied. Thus, sovereign immunity was not a bar to appellant's action. The court dismissed the second count because it put in issue asserted violations of a labor contract and the dispute fell within the public policy of the Public Employee Relations Act, 43 Pa. Cons. Ann. § 1101.101, et seq.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's order which dismissed appellant tenured faculty member's complaint against appellees, Commonwealth, Department of Education, and Bloomsburg State College, because appellees were not entitled to sovereign immunity; appellees were reinstated as defendants. Count I of the complaint was remanded to the lower court for further proceedings. Count II of the complaint was dismissed for lack of jurisdiction.

Kremer v. State Ethics Com


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


503 Pa. 358; 469 A.2d 593; 1983 Pa. LEXIS 795


October 18, 1983, Argued  
December 30, 1983, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 81-3-408, Appeal from Order of the Commonwealth Court entered April 20, 1981 at No. 721 C.D. 1980, 56 PA CMWLTH 160, 424 A.2d 968 

No. 81-3-409, Appeal from Order of the Commonwealth Court entered April 20, 1981 at No. 721 C.D. 1980, 56 PA CMWLTH 160, 424 A.2d 968 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant judge and appellee ethics commission sought review of the decision of the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania), which held that Ethics Law, 65 P.S. § 401 et seq., as applied to judges was an unconstitutional violation of the separation of powers. Because the lower court rejected appellant's contentions that the law violated the constitutional right to privacy, he cross-appealed the lower court's decision.


OVERVIEW: Appellant judge brought an action against appellee state ethics commission challenging the validity and applicability to judges of the financial disclosure requirements of Ethics Law, 65 P.S. § 401 et seq. The lower court held that the Ethics Law was unconstitutional as applied to judges because such application violated the separation of powers. Appellee sought review of the holding and appellant, because he asserted that the law violated the constitutional right to privacy, cross-appealed. On appeal, the court affirmed the judgment of the lower court. The court held that under the doctrine of separation of powers, the legislature could not exercise any power specifically entrusted to the judiciary. Pa. Const. art. V, § 10, provided that the court had the power to supervise the commonwealth's courts. The court held therefore that the financial disclosure provisions of the Ethics Law infringed on its power to supervise the courts, and therefore, was unconstitutional as applied. Because of its finding, the court did not reach the issue of invasion of privacy.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the lower court, which held that the financial disclosure requirements of a state ethics law as applied to judges was unconstitutional. The court held that the law was unconstitutional in that it infringed on the court's power to supervise the commonwealth's courts. Because of its finding, the court did not reach appellant judge's argument that the law was an unconstitutional invasion of the right to privacy.

Denoncourt v. Commonwealth


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


504 Pa. 191; 470 A.2d 945; 1983 Pa. LEXIS 833


October 18, 1983, Argued  
December 30, 1983, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 22 M.D. Appeal Docket, 1983, Appeal from the Opinion and Order entered March 21, 1983 of the Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania at No. 2793 C.D. 1981,   Pa. Commwlth. Ct.  , 457 A.2d 213 (1983).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, incumbent school directors, challenged the Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania summary judgment to appellee, State Ethics Commission, in a class action by appellants for declaratory and injunctive relief from enforcement of the Public Officials Ethics Act, 65 P.S. §§ 404, 405, 409. Appellants contended that the reporting provisions unconstitutionally imposed criminal liability for an inability, not an unwillingness, to comply.


OVERVIEW: The trial court granted summary judgment to appellee, State Ethics Commission, in a class action brought by appellant, incumbent school directors, seeking declaratory and injunctive relief from the enforcement of the Public Officials Ethics Act, 65 P.S. §§ 404, 405, 4099. The law provided that a public official must, as a condition for taking the oath of office, continuing to perform his duties, and to receive payment for his services, file a statement of financial interests, including detailed information about the official and his "immediate family," i.e., a spouse residing in the person's household and minor dependent children. Appellant challenged the holding, and the court reversed, because appellants were entitled to declaratory and injunctive relief in their class action. The court held that the reporting provisions were unconstitutional, in that they violated the due process rights of the public official and the family's right to privacy under Pa. Const. art. 1, § 1. The provisions, in effect, imposed criminal liability for an inability, not an unwillingness, to comply with the requirements of the law; the notion of a married couple's identity of interest was outdated.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court award of summary judgment to appellee, State Ethics Commission, because appellant, incumbent school directors, was entitled to declaratory and injunctive relief in their class action. The court held that the reporting provisions of a statute relating to family members were unconstitutional, because they violated the due process rights of the public official and the family's constitutional right to privacy.

First Pennsylvania Bank, N.A. v. Lancaster County Tax Claim Bureau


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


504 Pa. 179; 470 A.2d 938; 1983 Pa. LEXIS 797


April 20, 1982, Argued  
December 30, 1983, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargued October 24, 1983.


PRIOR HISTORY: No. 461 January Term, 1979, Appeal from the Order of July 18, 1979 of the Commonwealth Court Affirming the Final Decree of the Court of Common Pleas of Lancaster County, 44 Pa. Commonwealth Ct. 301, 404 A.2d 709 (1979) 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant mortgagee challenged the order of Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania), which affirmed an adjudication and decree nisi entered by a judge sitting in equity, in favor of appellee, county tax claim, and dismissing a complaint in which appellant sought to set aside a tax sale. Appellant contended that the notice provision regarding record mortgagees, under the Real Estate Tax Sale Law, 72 P.S. § 5860.602, was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Appellant mortgagee sought to set aside a tax sale made pursuant to the Real Estate Tax Sale Law, 72 P.S. § 5860.101 et seq., to cancel the Tax Claim Bureau deed to appellee county tax claim, the purchaser at the tax sale, and to reinstate and confirm a first mortgage which was discharged by the tax sale. Appellant challenged the order of the trial court, which affirmed an adjudication and decree nisi, in favor of appellee, and dismissed appellant's complaint. The court vacated and remanded, because appellee could not force mortgagees to obtain a deficiency judgment simultaneously with every mortgage foreclosure. The court held that the notice provision regarding record mortgagees, § 5860.602, was unconstitutional. Because of the supremacy clause of U.S. Const. art. VI, § 2, the court was bound to follow the United States Supreme Court holding that, in order to satisfy the requirements of the due process clause of U.S. Const. amend. XIV, when the mortgagee is identified in a mortgage that is publicly recorded, constructive notice by publication must be supplemented by notice mailed to the mortgagee's last known available address, or by personal service.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the trial court order affirming an adjudication and decree nisi, in favor of appellee, county tax claim, and dismissing a complaint in which appellant mortgagee sought to set aside a tax sale, in a dispute over statutory notice requirements. The court remanded for further proceedings, and held that appellee could not force mortgagees to obtain a deficiency judgment simultaneously with every mortgage foreclosure.

Commonwealth v. Crenshaw


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


504 Pa. 33; 470 A.2d 451; 1983 Pa. LEXIS 814


October 24, 1983, Argued  
December 29, 1983, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 80-3-479, Appeal from Judgments of Sentence of Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia - Criminal, entered February 26, 1980, at Nos. 554 and 557-559, September Term, 1976


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged his conviction by the Court of Common Pleas, Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania), for murder of the first degree, robbery, and two counts of burglary. Appellant claimed he was placed in double jeopardy, and that he could not be sentenced to death because the crime took place prior to the effective date of the death penalty statute.


OVERVIEW: The trial court severed charges against appellant of murder, robbery and burglary against three separate victims and appellant was acquitted as to the first victim. As to the other two victims, appellant was found guilty of murder of the first degree, robbery, and two counts of burglary, and received, inter alia, a sentence of death. Appellant challenged his conviction because evidence in the latter trial had been presented in the first trial, U.S. Const. amend. V, and because he was sentenced to death although the offense occurred prior to the effective date of the Act of September 13, 1978, which provided for the imposition of the death penalty. The court vacated the death sentence and imposed a sentence of life imprisonment, and otherwise affirmed judgment. The court held that there was no double jeopardy because the first jury had to determine appellant's guilt or innocence only as to the first victim and not as to the other crimes. The court held that the death sentence statute should have not been applied, where it became effective after the crime, but before the trial began.


OUTCOME: Appellant's death sentence was vacated and a sentence of life imprisonment was imposed because the offenses occurred before the effective date of the act that permitted death sentences to be imposed. The court affirmed appellant's conviction because appellant was not placed in double jeopardy by the introduction of the same evidence in separate trials involving different victims.

Krenzelak v. Krenzelak


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


503 Pa. 373; 469 A.2d 987; 1983 Pa. LEXIS 755

PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] NO. 11 WESTERN DISTRICT APPEAL DOCKET, 1983, Appeal from the Order of the Superior Court of Pennsylvania of December 10, 1982, at No. 1042 Pittsburgh 1981, Reversing the Order Dated October 7, 1981 of the Court of Common Pleas of Washington County, Pennsylvania, Civil Division, No. 7810, In Equity, Book 45, Page 123,   Pa. Superior Ct.  , 453 A.2d 998 (1982) 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, husband and son, challenged an order of the Superior Court of Pennsylvania, reversing the trial court order, sustaining appellants' preliminary objections to an equity complaint filed by appellee wife to nullify a real estate conveyance. Appellants contended that husband had the right and authority to make the conveyance, and the conveyance was in no way affected by the Pa. OVERVIEW: Appellant husband purchased property in his name only, at which time he married appellee wife. Appellee did not contribute to the purchase price of the land nor was the land ever titled in her name. Appellant husband filed for divorce against appellee, and she countersued. XIV, and Pa. Const. art. I, § I.


OUTCOME: The Court vacated the appellate court order, which reversed the trial court order, sustaining appellant husband and son's preliminary objections to an equity complaint filed by appellee wife to nullify a real estate conveyance from appellants. The Court remanded the record to the trial court, and held that the trial court erred in unconstitutionally applying a statute retroactively, which materially altered appellee's vested property rights.
