Official Court Reporters v. Pa. Labor Relations Bd.


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


502 Pa. 518; 467 A.2d 311; 1983 Pa. LEXIS 700; 116 L.R.R.M. 2823


April 26, 1983, Argued  
October 25, 1983, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] NOS. 9 & 10 EASTERN DISTRICT APPEAL DOCKET 1983, APPEAL FROM AN OPINION AND ORDER OF THE COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA ENTERED JUNE 30, 1982, AT NO. 430 C.D. 1981, REVERSING AN ORDER OF THE COURT OF COMMON PLEAS OF PHILADELPHIA ENTERED FEBRUARY 10, 1981, AT NO. 901 APRIL TERM 1979, 67 Pa. Commonwealth Ct. 256, 446 A.2d 1357 (1982) 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, common pleas judges and labor relations board, challenged an order of the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania), which held that appellee competing association of court reporters had standing to appeal the labor relations board order vacating certification of the court reporters' union representative.


OVERVIEW: Following appellant labor relations board certification of court reporters' union representative, appellee competing court reporters' association brought an appeal, in which the common pleas court denied appellee's standing. The commonwealth court reversed the judgment of the common pleas court. On appeal, the court reversed and vacated the commonwealth court order, holding that appellee was not a party aggrieved by the board's decision within the meaning of Pa. R. App. P. 501, that appellee was not a party to the proceedings before the board, and that the right to appeal was not otherwise enlarged by statute. Therefore, appellee lacked standing to appeal. While general labor law concepts of collective bargaining and exclusive representation derogated common law concepts of freedom to contract, the right to appeal from decisions of the board concerning those concepts had not.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the order of the commonwealth court and reinstated the order of the Pennsylvania Labor Relations Board, which vacated certification of the court reporters' union representative. Appellee competing association of court reporters was not a party aggrieved by the board's decision. Thus, appellee lacked standing to appeal because it and its members suffered no immediate harm.

Astemborski v. Susmarski


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


502 Pa. 409; 466 A.2d 1018; 1983 Pa. LEXIS 692


REARGUED: September 12, 1983  
October 21, 1983, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargued Septemper 21, 1983.


PRIOR HISTORY: No. 22 W.D. Appeal Docket, 1982, Appeal from the Revised Opinion and Order of April 12, 1982, of the Court of Common Pleas, Civil Action-Law, of Erie County, Pennsylvania No. 87-NS-1981, as further amended by Order of Court of May 13, 1982, for typographical error.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: On remand from the United States Supreme Court, appellant mother sought reconsideration of the constitutionality of the statute of limitations imposed upon paternity actions, 42 P.S. § 6704(e).


OVERVIEW: More than six years after the child's birth, appellant mother brought an action against appellee father to establish paternity of the child. Appellant successfully argued that the statute of limitations imposed upon paternity actions, 42 P.S. . § 6704(e), was unconstitutional. The court reversed the judgment, and appellant petitioned the Supreme Court of the United States for a Writ of Certiorari. The Writ was granted, judgment was vacated and the case remanded for reconsideration, in light of Pickett v. Brown, 103 S. Ct. 2199, 76 L. Ed. 2d 372 (1983), of whether the statute violated equal protection guarantees of U.S. Const. amend. XIV, and Pa. Const. art. 1, § 26. The court reinstated its original order that appellant's claim was barred and that the statute was constitutional because the court found Pickett supportive of its previous holding. The court held that the statute satisfied two requirements, (1) that the time period was sufficiently long to present a reasonable opportunity for claims on a child's behalf, and (2) was substantially related to the state's interest in avoiding the litigation of stale or fraudulent claims.


OUTCOME: The judgment upholding the constitutionality of the statute of limitations on child paternity actions, and barring appellant mother's claim against appellee father, was reinstated because the period was long enough to provide adequate opportunity for claims, and was avoided litigation of stale or fraudulent claims.

Commonwealth v. Hicks


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


502 Pa. 344; 466 A.2d 613; 1983 Pa. LEXIS 690


September 13, 1983, Argued  
October 19, 1983, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 1 W.D. Appeal Docket, 1983, Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County, Criminal Division, at No. CC8203243A, dated November 30, 1982, granting Appellee's Motion to Quash the Information.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Commonwealth appealed a decision of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County (Pennsylvania), which granted appellee's pre-trial motion to quash an information that charged him with homicide by vehicle under 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 3732.


OVERVIEW: An information alleged that appellee, while intoxicated, entered an interstate route by driving his automobile the wrong way down an exit ramp, and that while travelling north in the southbound lanes of the interstate, appellee's vehicle struck an oncoming vehicle, killing one of its occupants. Appellee was charged with homicide by vehicle in violation of 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 3732. The trial court granted appellee's pre-trial motion to quash the information as far as it charged appellee with homicide by vehicle. On appeal, the court reversed and held that the Pennsylvania legislature's enactment of § 3732 was a constitutional exercise of its lawmaking authority. The judgment by the legislature to borrow from the concepts of tort law in imposing liability under § 3732 bore a rational relationship to the legislature's goal of reducing highway fatalities. By focusing on drivers who knew that they were engaging in conduct constituting a statutory violation of which death was a probable consequence, § 3732 sought only to sanction those persons who should have anticipated that their conduct was likely to produce death.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order, which granted appellee's pre-trial motion to quash and remanded the case for trial because the goal of the homicide by vehicle statute was constitutional as it was rationally related to the goal of reducing highway fatalities.

Martin v. Unemployment Compensation Bd. of Review


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


502 Pa. 282; 466 A.2d 107; 1983 Pa. LEXIS 670


September 23, 1982, Argued  
September 28, 1983, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargued March 7, 1983.


PRIOR HISTORY: NO. 17 WESTERN DISTRICT APPEAL DOCKET 1982, Appeal from the Order of the Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania of January 6, 1982 at No. 1910 CD-1979 affirming the Order of the Unemployment Compensation Board of Review of August 9, 1979 at B-174717 denying unemployment compensation benefits to claimaint, 63 Pa.Cmwlth. 629, 439 A.2d 207 (1982) 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant claimant sought review of an order by the Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania, which affirmed appellee Unemployment Compensation Board of Review's denial of appellant's application for unemployment benefits. Appellant contended the Unemployment Compensation Law, 43 P.S. §§ 801, 804, violated the equal protection clause of U.S. Const. amend. XIV, by the creation of invidious eligibility classifications.


OVERVIEW: Appellant claimant filed a claim for unemployment benefits with appellee Unemployment Compensation Board of Review. Appellant was denied benefits by appellee because appellant's qualifying wages were less than the amount required. Appellant contended before the lower court that the financial eligibility standards and the statutory scheme in the Unemployment Compensation Law, 43 P.S. §§ 801, 804, discriminated against low wage earners and favored higher wage earners. On appeal, the court affirmed because the statutory classification of wage earners had a rational relationship to a legitimate state interest, and was within the legislative power. The court held that the classification served to exclude new entrants into the work force, that it was appropriate for workers to earn their benefits, and established the work-oriented motives of the applicant. Therefore, the court held that the classification scheme enacted by the legislature did not violate the Equal Protection Clause of U. S. Const. amend. XIV.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the denial of unemployment benefits to appellant claimant by appellee Unemployment Compensation Board of Review because the classification system of qualifying wages enacted by the legislature had a rational relationship to a legitimate state interest.

 

117 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


In re Davis


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


502 Pa. 110; 465 A.2d 614; 1983 Pa. LEXIS 677


May 27, 1983, Argued  
September 19, 1983, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 81-3-475, Appeal from the Orders Dated November 13 and 20, 1979 of the Court of Common Pleas, Juvenile, for the County of Berks, at No. 986-S of 197, Dependency, and Order of Superior Court at No. 2542 October Term, 1979, 288 Pa. Super. 453, 432 A.2d 600 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant substitute grandparents of minor child challenged orders of the Superior Court (Pennsylvania), which affirmed the trial court award of child's custody to appellee foster home agency.


OVERVIEW: When mother of a minor child died, the trial court found the child to be a dependent child under 42 P.S. § 6302 and awarded custody to appellee foster home agency for placement, which was affirmed by the superior court. On appeal, the supreme court vacated, holding that the child was properly adjudicated to be a dependent child and that the trial court was required to enter an order of disposition under 42 P.S. § 6351. The court found that the trial court properly considered factors to determine the child's best interest as to placement between appellee and appellant substitute grandparents, including the child's age, preference, race, and bonding, as well as the preference for rearing siblings together. The court remanded for further proceedings to determine how to make the child's transition between homes the least traumatic for the child.


OUTCOME: The court vacated a child custody order as it stood and remanded the case because further hearings were required to impose a schedule for investigation, evaluations, and hearings to determine how to accomplish the transition between homes, minimizing trauma for the minor child.

 

118 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Truesdale


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


502 Pa. 94; 465 A.2d 606; 1983 Pa. LEXIS 667


April 28, 1983, Argued  
September 15, 1983, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 81-3-483, Appeal from Judgments of Sentence of Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia, Criminal Trial Division, Imposed at Information Nos. 131, 134, 135 and 136, July Session 1976


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant convicted felon challenged the order of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia (Pennsylvania), which in his second trial disallowed appellant's impeachment questions and admitted other evidence over his objections. Two death sentences were imposed upon appellant following his conviction for murder, conspiracy, and aggravated assault.


OVERVIEW: Appellant convicted felon was found guilty on retrial of two counts of murder of the first degree, conspiracy, and aggravated assault. The death penalty was returned on both counts of murder. The convictions were challenged and subsequently certified to the court. The court held that even if the trial court erred when it disallowed appellant's impeachment questions the error was harmless because appellant fully explored the witness' motive to testify. A witness' unresponsive answer about threats on his life was opened by appellant on cross-examination and so was within the scope of redirect. A mug shot that was not admitted into evidence or shown to the jury was not an improper suggestion of prior criminal activity on the part of appellant. The photograph had probative value and was not inflammatory. Appellant's counsel was not ineffective for having failed to request a jury instruction as to the photograph's evidentiary value. The court vacated the death sentences and imposed life sentences because the 1978 death penalty statute, 1978 Pa. Laws 756, could not be applied to a defendant's retrial for an offense that was committed prior to the enactment of the statute.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the death sentences and imposed consecutive sentences of life imprisonment because the death penalty statute could not be applied to an offense that was committed before the statute was enacted. The trial court committed no reversible error in its rulings on evidence.

 

119 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Heckendorn v. Consolidated Rail Corp.


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


502 Pa. 101; 465 A.2d 609; 1983 Pa. LEXIS 668


March 10, 1983, Argued  
September 15, 1983, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 28 E.D. Appeal Dkt. 1982, Appeal of Consolidated Rail Corporation from Order of Superior Court entered October 9, 1981, at No. 2177 Philadelphia 1980, affirming Order of Court of Common Pleas of Cumberland County entered August 25, 1980, at No. 3781 Civil 1979, 293 Pa. Super. 474, 439 A.2d 674 (1981).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant third-party tortfeasor sought review of the order of the Superior Court (Pennsylvania), affirming the trial court's order which sustained appellee employer's preliminary objections to the joinder of appellee as a defendant in an action in trespass against appellant arising out of injuries plaintiff employee sustained in the course of his employment.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs, employee and his wife, filed a complaint in trespass against appellant third-party tortfeasor and another company alleging that, while in the course of his employment, plaintiff employee was injured as he was loading a railroad boxcar owned by appellant and leased to appellee employer. When appellant attempted to join appellee as an additional defendant, appellee filed preliminary objections to the joinder. The trial court sustained the preliminary objections and the appellate court affirmed. On review, the issue was whether appellee, although statutorily immune from liability, could nonetheless be joined by appellant for the purpose of apportioning appellee's negligence with the negligence of appellant. The court affirmed, holding that the lower courts properly held that joinder of appellee as a defendant for any purpose was barred by Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 77, § 481 (Supp. 1983). The court found the Comparative Negligence Act, 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 7102, did not reflect a legislative intent to permit joinder of an employer as an additional defendant for the purpose of apportioning fault.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the appellate court's order affirming the trial court's order which sustained appellee employer's preliminary objections to joinder as a defendant because the trial court properly read the Comparative Negligence Act to be consistent with the legislative intent of the Workers' Compensation Act to grant immunity to employers in employee actions against third-party tortfeasors.

Dale Nat'l Bank v. Commonwealth


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


502 Pa. 170; 465 A.2d 965; 1983 Pa. LEXIS 664


May 23, 1983, Argued  
September 15, 1983, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 5 M.D. Misc. Dkt. 1983, Petition for Review of Decision and Order of Board of Finance and Revenue at Board Docket No. C-7088


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner bank sought review of an order of the Board of Finance and Revenue (Pennsylvania), which refused a petition for refund of bank shares tax petitioner paid to respondent state for the tax year 1978.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner bank sought a refund of bank shares tax paid for the tax year 1978, claiming that the bank shares tax imposed improperly reflected a tax on two classes of assets: obligations of the United States, in violation of 31 U.S.C.S. § 742; and state and municipal obligations, in violation of Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 72, § 4752-2 (Supp.1983). The Board of Finance and Revenue refused the petition. The court vacated the order, holding that the bank shares tax assessed against petitioner improperly took into consideration obligations of the United States owned by petitioner in violation of the plain language of 31 U.S.C.S. § 742 because the tax was computed on the basis of equity capital, which in turn was determined, in part, by United States obligations. However, the court held petitioner was incorrect in asserting that its state and municipal obligations could not be taken into consideration because it was clear that the bank shares tax was imposed on capital owned and employed by petitioner in its banking operations, which capital was a property interest separate from the state and municipal obligations themselves.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the order of the Board of Finance and Revenue refusing the petition for refund of improper taxation of petitioner bank's state and municipal obligations because obligations of the United States owned by petitioner were improperly taken into consideration in computing petitioner's bank shares tax; the record was remanded to the board for proceedings consistent with the opinion.

W. Penn Power Co. v. Cohen


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


502 Pa. 25; 463 A.2d 418; 1983 Pa. LEXIS 642


March 7, 1983, Argued  
July 26, 1983, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 29 W.D. Appeal Docket 1982, Appeal from the Order Entered April 22, 1982 and from the Final Order Entered May 21, 1982 in the Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania at No. 1339 C.D. 1980, 66 Pa. Commonwealth Ct. 299, 443 A.2d 1366 (1980) 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant public utility appealed the order of the Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania, which ruled in favor of appellee, Secretary, Pennsylvania Department of Revenue, and denied appellant's request for relief. Appellant contended that the trial court erred in denying mandamus, and in refusing to order appellee to issue formal settlements on tax returns, under the Public Utility Realty Tax Act (PURTA), 72 P.S. §§ 8101-A through 8108-A (1982-83).


OVERVIEW: Pa. Const. art. VIII was amended in 1968 to explicitly subject the real property of public utilities to real estate taxes. The Public Utility Realty Tax Act (PURTA), 72 P.S. §§ 8101-A through 8108-A (1982-83), was first enacted in 1970. Appellant public utility timely filed its PURTA tax returns for the year 1969 through 1973 with appellee, Department of Revenue. No formal settlement was made by appellee on these returns until March 15, 1977, when appellee was advised by the attorney general that 72 P.S. § 8102-A(c) imposed a duty to settle PURTA taxes. Thereafter, appellee began settling all subsequent PURTA tax returns until advised by memorandum of August 6, 1980, that settlements were not required unless the Department of Revenue was unwilling to accept the PURTA taxpayer's self-assessment. Appellee did not settle appellant's PURTA tax returns for the years 1969 through 1973. Appellant challenged the trial court order which denied its request for mandamus relief. The court affirmed, and held that in the absence of any explicit statutory directive, mandamus would not lie against the state or one of its officials to compel a formal settlement of public utility taxes by appellant.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court order that ruled in favor of appellee, Secretary, Pennsylvania Department of Revenue, and denied appellant public utility's request for mandamus relief, in a dispute over the payment of public utility taxes. The court held that, in the absence of any explicit statutory directive, mandamus would not lie against the state or one of its officials to compel a formal settlement of public utility taxes by appellant.

 

123 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Emanuel


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


501 Pa. 581; 462 A.2d 653; 1983 Pa. LEXIS 625


May 27, 1983, Argued  
July 8, 1983, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 81-3-469, An Appeal from the Order of the Superior Court of Pennsylvania at No. 2006 October Term, 1979, Affirming the Decision of the Court of Common Pleas Criminal Divison, Delaware County at No. 4303 of 1978. Appeal allowed September 28, 1981 at No. 215, E.D. Misc. Dkt., 1981, 285 Pa. Super. 594; 428 A.2d 204 (1981) 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state appealed from an order of the Superior Court of Pennsylvania, which affirmed the grant of a motion in arrest of judgment for the state's attorney's failure to meet the signature requirements of Pa. R. Crim. P. 225(b).


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted at trial of theft by unlawful taking and two separate criminal conspiracies. Both the trial and intermediate courts granted defendant's motion for arrest of judgment by holding that an information signed by the rubber-stamped signature of a district attorney was void ab initio. The state appealed the decision, reasoning that the meaning of the word "signed" was ambiguous, and was not dispositive of whether a rubber-stamped signature met the requirements of Pa. R. Crim. P. 225(b). The court granted review and reversed and remanded the lower courts' judgment for sentencing. The court reasoned that the explanation given for rejecting the rubber-stamped signature did not address the dangers that an information may be filed in error or without proper review. The court also stated that allowing defendant's discharge would be an anomaly not because the informations were wrongly filed against him, or that his case's defense was prejudiced by the state's actions, or because there was insufficient evidence to sustain his conviction, but because the state's attorney misinterpreted the requirement for one type of signature over another. Such a result was not justified.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded the lower courts' grant and affirmation of a motion for arrest of judgment. The explanation for rejecting the rubber-stamped signature did not address the dangers that an information may be erroneously filed or not properly reviewed. Further, to allow defendant to be discharged would be anomalous because of a failure to properly interpret the requirement of a specific type of signature over another.

Mezvinsky v. Davis


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


500 Pa. 564; 459 A.2d 307; 1983 Pa. LEXIS 536


April 18, 1983, Argued  
April 26, 1983, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 3 M.D. Misc. Dkt. 1983


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner elector sought review from the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania), challenging the constitutionality of the limited voting provision of 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 3133.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner elector challenged the constitutionality of the limited voting provision of 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 3133 (1981) as in derogation of his constitutional right as an elector to elect the judges of the commonwealth court. The court granted review and found that under § 3133, each political party was limited to nominations of only two candidates, and each voter, in both the primary and municipal elections, was limited to voting for only two candidates. Thus, § 3133 precluded two-thirds of the voters from participating in selection of the candidate who will fill one vacancy. The court held that 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 3133 (1981) was in violation of the Constitution of Pennsylvania. The court held that election officials were directed to notify all county boards of elections and every other party entitled to notice that each political party could nominate three candidates for the office of judge of the commonwealth court in the primary election of 1983, and each elector in the primary election of 1983 and in the municipal election of 1983 could vote for three candidates for judge of the commonwealth court.


OUTCOME: The court granted petitioner elector's application for review, noted the applicable statute precluded two-thirds of the voters from participating in selection of the candidate who would fill one vacancy, and held that the statute was in violation of the Constitution of Pennsylvania.

In re Appeal of M.A. Kravitz Co.


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


501 Pa. 200; 460 A.2d 1075; 1983 Pa. LEXIS 525


January 25, 1983, Argued  
April 25, 1983, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Denied June 15, 1983.


PRIOR HISTORY: No. 81-3-372, Appeal from Order of the Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania dated September 5, 1980 at No. 1283 C.D. 1978 reversing an Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Bucks County Civil Action, Law - entered at No. 77-12230 affirming the decision of the Board of Supervisors of Wrightstown Township


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant board challenged an order of the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania), which reversed the decision of the lower court against appellee builder. Appellant claimed that the ordinance was constitutional and was not exclusionary.


OVERVIEW: Appellee builder petitioned appellant board for a curative amendment to the zoning ordinance and approval of a proposed townhouse development on a parcel of land that it owned. Appellant denied the amendment. The lower court affirmed appellant's decision. The appellate court found that the ordinance was unconstitutional, reversed the lower court, and directed approval of the proposed development. Appellant sought review. The court found that the lower court exceeded its scope of review in giving the ordinance its own interpretation, and failed to apply the correct standards for reviewing zoning ordinances alleged to be exclusionary. The court reviewed evidence that was presented to appellant and found that there was substantial evidence on which to base its finding that the ordinance did not exclude townhouses, that appellant properly determined that the township was not a logical place for rapid growth and development, and that the authorities accepted by appellant gave a balanced and weighted consideration to the factors relevant to local and regional housing needs and development. The court reversed the order of the lower court and reinstated the order of the trial court.


OUTCOME: The order of the lower court was reversed because it exceeded its scope of authority and failed to apply the proper test for reviewing ordinances alleged to be exclusionary, and reinstated the order of the trial court which affirmed appellant board's decision to deny appellee builder's petition for a curative amendment to a zoning ordinance to allow the building of a townhouse development.

Del. River Port Auth. v. Thornburgh


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


500 Pa. 629; 459 A.2d 717; 1983 Pa. LEXIS 514


December 7, 1982, Argued  
April 21, 1983, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 81-3-375, Appeal from Order of Commonwealth Court entered February 4, 1981, at No. 2210 C.D. 1979, 56 Pa. Cmwlth. 459, 425 A.2d 479 (1981) 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant port authority sought review of an order of the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania), which dismissed, for lack of jurisdiction, appellant's petition for review seeking a writ of mandamus against appellees, state agencies and officials.


OVERVIEW: Appellant port authority filed a petition for review that sought a writ of mandamus against appellees, state officials and agencies, to compel appellees to construct a highway to connect to a bridge built by appellant. Appellant alleged appellees' failure to construct the highway violated the Delaware River Port Authority Compact between Pennsylvania and New Jersey. On appeal, the court reversed the lower court's order that dismissed appellant's petition for lack of jurisdiction. The court recognized that the Board of Claims had exclusive jurisdiction to determine all claims over $ 300 against the Commonwealth arising from contract, 72 P.S. § 4651-4 (1983), but the court held that since the validity of appellant's claim turned upon a determination of the parties' rights and obligations under the Delaware River Port Authority Compact, a statutory issue under Pennsylvania, New Jersey, and federal law, jurisdiction properly lied in the Commonwealth Court, and not in an administrative forum such as the Board of Claims.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the lower court's order that dismissed appellant port authority's petition for review filed against appellees, state agencies and officials, for lack of jurisdiction. The lower court, and not an administrative agency, had jurisdiction to determine appellant's claim because the validity of the claim turned upon a determination of the parties' rights and obligations under an interstate compact.

Commonwealth ex rel. Zimmerman v. Officers & Employees Retirement Bd.


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


501 Pa. 293; 461 A.2d 593; 1983 Pa. LEXIS 500


December 8, 1982, Argued  
March 31, 1983, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Granted June 2, 1983.


PRIOR HISTORY: No. 80-3-628, On Appeal from the Order entered May 29, 1980, Court of Common Pleas, Civil Action - Law - Delaware County at No. 79-16641.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, commonwealth, attorney general, and district attorney, sought review of a judgment from the Court of Common Pleas of Delaware County (Pennsylvania), which dismissed appellant's complaint and entered judgment in favor of appellee, retirement board, in a case to terminate pension benefits.


OVERVIEW: The court affirmed the judgment of the lower court that had denied appellant's, commonwealth, attorney general, and district attorney writ of mandamus because it found that that the lower court properly determined that the Public Employees Forfeiture Act (Act), 43 P.S. §§ 1311-1315 did not apply to the former mayor. Appellants sought to have the pension benefits of the former mayor terminated because he had been convicted of a crime three months prior to his resignation. The court found that the Act had not been passed at the time of the mayor's resignation and that the legislature could not require the retroactive application of the Act. The court held that retirement provisions for public employees was in the form of deferred compensation for service actually rendered in the past and that modifications to the retirement system could not be adverse to a member who had met the eligibility requirements. The court held that the contract rights of the former mayor were determined on the date of membership in the retirement system and could not be reached by the retroactive forfeiture provision of the Act, therefore appellee, retirement board, had correctly paid the pension benefits.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's denial of appellant's, commonwealth, attorney general, and district attorney, writ of mandamus and judgment in favor of appellee, retirement board, because the retroactive forfeiture provision of the Public Employees Forfeiture Act did not apply to the former mayor, benefit recipient.

Commonwealth v. Geschwendt


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


500 Pa. 120; 454 A.2d 991; 1982 Pa. LEXIS 693


October 27, 1982, Argued  
December 31, 1982, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Denied February 4, 1983.


PRIOR HISTORY: No. 80-3-365, Appeal from the Judgments of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Bucks County, Nos. 939-944 of 1976 and Order of the Superior Court of Pennsylvania, No. 260 Special Transfer Docket, 271 Pa. Super. 102, 412 A.2d 595 (1979) 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged an order of the Superior Court (Pennsylvania), which affirmed the trial court's judgment of sentence for the conviction of murder. Defendant claimed that he was entitled to the retroactive application of a law that changed the jury instruction as to the law of insanity and, therefore, a new trial.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of murder by reason of insanity and sentence was imposed. Defendant sought review on grounds that he was entitled to benefit from a decision that was pending while his case was on trial but filed after his trial concluded, that changed the law regarding the defense of insanity. The court found that the retrospective application of the decision that changed the jury instruction on the insanity defense was not constitutionally compelled, that the nature of the new rule and the purposes sought to be achieved by its adoption represented a clear break with the former law, and that the new instruction was appropriately applied prospectively. The court found that because defendant's trial preceded the filing of the opinion upon which he relied, the trial court did not err in its refusal to give the requested instructions.


OUTCOME: The judgments of sentence were affirmed because defendant's trial preceded the filing of the court's decision changing the law as to the appropriate instruction to be given in an insanity defense case, and the trial court did not err in failing to give the new instruction.

 

130 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Sorrell


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


500 Pa. 355; 456 A.2d 1326; 1982 Pa. LEXIS 705; 37 A.L.R.4th 293


October 21, 1982, Argued  
December 30, 1982, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Denied March 11, 1983.


PRIOR HISTORY: Nos. 14, 15, 16, 17 E.D. Appeal Docket, 1982, On appeal pursuant to certification by the orders of the Superior Court at No. 2406 October Term, 1978, No. 2451 October Term, 1978, No. 711 Philadelphia, 1980, and No. 1486 Philadelphia, 1980.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a consolidated appeal, defendants appealed decisions of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania), which convicted them of criminal offenses after denying them their rights to waive trial by jury.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were tried and convicted by juries for various criminal offenses. Each had filed motions to waive trial by jury, but were denied their requests. The court remanded, holding that the right to trial by jury was not a substantive right but a right of procedure through which rights conferred by substantive law were enforced. The court held that Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 42, § 5104(c), which contravened Pa. R. Crim. P. 1101, was an unconstitutional infringement upon the procedural rule making authority of the courts conferred by Pa. Const. art. V, § 10. Under Rule 1101, the prosecution could control accused's motion to waive trial by jury. By conferring on the prosecution an absolute right to jury trial, the trial court would be precluded from exercising discretion of Rule 1101 in assessing whether a non-jury trial should be permitted. The court found that the trial court erroneously denied the requests of three of the defendants, but the trial court properly exercised its discretion in denying one in the interest of that defendant obtaining a fair trial, which he probably could not have gotten after the trial court was exposed to his criminal record during pre-trial proceedings.


OUTCOME: The court remanded after determining that the trial court properly denied only one request for the right to waive a jury trial because of pretrial evidence that might have tainted the trial judge, and determining that the trial court erred in denied three other requests. The court found that to give a prosecutor an absolute right to a jury trial would destroy the trial court's discretion in determining whether a defendant could waive his right.

Commonwealth v. Zettlemoyer


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


500 Pa. 16; 454 A.2d 937; 1982 Pa. LEXIS 679


October 25, 1982, Argued  
December 30, 1982, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Denied February 7, 1983.


PRIOR HISTORY: No. 81-2-292, Appeal of Keith Zettlemoyer from the judgment of sentence of death, dated October 23, 1981, by the Honorable John C. Dowling, Judge, Twelfth Judicial District of Pennsylvania, docketed to No. 1818 Criminal Division 1980


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In an automatic appeal pursuant to 42 Pa. Const. Stat. Ann. § 9711(h)(1), defendant challenged the judgment of sentence of death imposed in the Twelfth Judicial District of Pennsylvania. Defendant claimed the procedures for imposition of a sentence of death, set down in 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 9711, were unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: An automatic appeal followed defendant's conviction for murder. Defendant offered a defense of diminished capacity in an attempt to reduce the degree of guilt from murder of the first to murder of the third degree. The court held that the testimony of defendant's grandmother, mother, and other witnesses who came into contact with defendant after the homicide, as well as defendant's expert who testified concerning defendant's inability to control himself under certain circumstances, was inadmissible on defendant's mental capacity to premeditate, deliberate, and form the specific intent to kill. The court found that the jury instructions were proper and that the state proved an aggravating circumstance beyond a reasonable doubt. The court sustained the conviction of murder in the first degree and affirmed the sentence of death. The court held that the sentence of death for murder of the first degree that involved the aggravating circumstance of killing a prosecution witness to a murder or other felony was not excessive or disproportionate to the sentence imposed in similar cases, and not cruel and unusual punishment under U.S. Const. amend. VIII or Pa. Const. art. I, § 13.


OUTCOME: The court held that the death penalty was not cruel and unusual punishment within the proscription of the United States and Pennsylvania constitutions, and that the sentencing procedures adopted by the legislature were permissible. The court sustained the conviction of murder of the first degree and affirmed the sentence of death.

Pechner v. Pa. Ins. Dep't


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


499 Pa. 139; 452 A.2d 230; 1982 Pa. LEXIS 602


April 13, 1982, Argued  
November 10, 1982, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 466 January Term, 1979, Appeal from the Order of the Commonwealth Court dated October 26, 1979 at 2242 C.D. 1977, No. 467 January Term, 1979, Appeal from the Order of the Commonwealth Court dated October 26, 1979 at 972 C.D. 1977


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant coal haulers sought review of orders from the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania), which sustained the preliminary objections of appellees, commonwealth and insurers, denied appellants' request for equitable relief, dismissed all legal claims against appellee commonwealth on the basis of sovereign immunity, and denied counsel fees.


OVERVIEW: The lower court denied appellant coal haulers' request for equitable relief against appellees, commonwealth and insurers, dismissed all of appellant's legal claims against appellee commonwealth on the basis of sovereign immunity, and remanded appellant's remaining legal claims against appellee insurers. Stat. Ann. § 8522) to bar appellant's cause of action in trespass against appellee commonwealth was unconstitutional under Pa. Const. art. I, § 11. Appellants' prayer for equitable jurisdiction was premature because appellants had not exhausted their administrative remedies concerning the premiums charged for black lung disease insurance prior. The court affirmed the order denying counsel fees to appellants.

The statute creating sovereign immunity could not be retroactively applied to preclude the trespass claims. The order denying appellants counsel fees was affirmed and the cases were remanded to the lower court.

Astemborski v. Susmarski


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


499 Pa. 99; 451 A.2d 1012; 1982 Pa. LEXIS 596


September 23, 1982, Argued  
November 4, 1982, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 22 W.D. Appeal Dkt. 1982, Appeal from the revised Order of April 12, 1982 of the Court of Common Pleas of the Sixth Judicial District at No. 87-NS-1981, as further amended by Order of May 13, 1982.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant alleged father sought review of a decision of the Court of Common Pleas of the Sixth Judicial District (Pennsylvania), which denied appellant's motion to dismiss appellee mother's complaint for support of her child and held unconstitutional the applicable statute of limitations, 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 6704(e).


OVERVIEW: The trial court concluded that appellee mother's complaint for support of her child was not timely filed, but denied appellant alleged father's motion to dismiss the complaint on the basis that the applicable statute of limitations, 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 6704(e), was invalid. The court reversed the order because the trial court erroneously held the statute of limitations barring appellee's claim for support to be unconstitutional. The court concluded that the support opportunity accorded to appellee's child was not so truncated as to be merely illusory. The court further concluded that the statute of limitations was not constitutionally infirm under U.S. Const. amend. XIV even though it operated to deprive appellee's child of its right to make a claim for support beyond the six-year limit. The legitimate state interest in elimination of stale or fraudulent claims was substantially served by the limitation imposed.


OUTCOME: The order against appellant alleged father was reversed because the trial court erroneously held the statute of limitations barring appellee mother's claim for support to be unconstitutional.

Miller & Son Paving v. Wrightstown Township


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


499 Pa. 80; 451 A.2d 1002; 1982 Pa. LEXIS 593


April 15, 1982, Argued  
October 28, 1982, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] NO. 470 January Term 1979, AN APPEAL FROM ORDER OF THE COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA DATED AUGUST 10, 1979 AT NO. 1873 C.D. 1978 AFFIRMING AN ORDER OF THE COURT OF COMMON PLEAS, BUCKS COUNTY, CIVIL ACTION, LAW - ENTERED AT NO. 77-6551-09-5


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant quarry operation challenged the judgment of the Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania, which affirmed the order of the lower court that upheld the validity of appellee township's setback requirements.


OVERVIEW: Appellant quarry operation challenged the lower court's judgment that upheld the validity of appellee township's setback requirements and sought that local zoning ordinances be declared to not impose setback requirements on quarrying operations. On appeal, the court affirmed, holding that the term "superseded" of 52 P.S. § 1396.17 referred to the displacement of all local regulations relating to surface mining which were in existence as of the effective date, except for zoning ordinances. The local setback requirements were not arbitrary, capricious and confiscatory, as all zoning involved a "taking" in the sense that the owner was not completely free to use his property as he choose, but such a taking did not entitle the owner to relief, unless the owner's rights had been unreasonably restricted. Appellant failed to meet its burden of proving either that the setback requirements bore no substantial relationship to the health, safety, morals or general welfare of the community or that the setback requirements were so patently unreasonable to be invalid as arbitrary, confiscatory and a deprivation of property without due process of law.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's judgment that upheld the validity of appellee township's setback requirements. The court held that a property owner did not have a vested right under the doctrine of natural expansion to extend a non-conforming setback beyond that which existed at the time the new ordinance was passed.

In re St.


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


499 Pa. 26; 451 A.2d 427; 1982 Pa. LEXIS 580


September 20, 1982, Argued  
October 20, 1982, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 50 E.D. Appeal Docket 1982, Appeal from the Order of the Commonwealth Court at No. 1403 C.D. 1982, filed July 15, 1982.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant independent candidate challenged an order of the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania), which found in favor of appellee incumbent that appellant's substituted nomination certificate was invalid and set it aside. Appellant claimed that it was unconstitutional to bar the substitute nomination of any person who had already been nominated by any other political body for the same office.


OVERVIEW: Appellant independent candidate for the office of Representative in Congress was nominated by papers filed by a political body. Subsequently, the nominated Republican Party candidate formally withdrew his candidacy and a substituted nomination certificate was filed by the Republican Party naming appellant as the Republican Party's substitute nominee. Appellee incumbent and Democratic Party nominee's petition challenging the validity of the substituted nomination certificate and requesting that the certificate be set aside was granted. Appellant sought review. The court found that the provisions of 25 P.S. § 2939 did not attempt to define the associational rights of any political party, that appellant's independent candidacy afforded ample opportunity for the Republican Party's exercise of its U.S. Const. amend. I rights, that the state had a legitimate interest in fair and orderly elections, and that 25 P.S. § 2939 was a legitimate exercise of legislative authority. The court affirmed the order of the trial court.


OUTCOME: Order of the trial court was affirmed in favor of appellee incumbent because appellant independent candidate did not establish that the election statute's prohibition against being nominated by or appearing on the ballot of two political party's violated either the First or Fourteenth Amendment, and because the statute properly reflected a legitimate state interest in avoiding voter confusion.

Gilbert Assocs. v. Commonwealth


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


498 Pa. 514; 447 A.2d 944; 1982 Pa. LEXIS 558


May 19, 1982, Argued  
June 28, 1982, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 81-2-251, Appeal from the Order of the Commonwealth Court at No. 2593 C.D. 1978.



DISPOSITION:  The court vacated the decision for appellee commonwealth in appellant foreign corporation's action for a franchise tax refund. The court held that appellant's equal protection rights were violated if it was not afforded an option equivalent to the option to use either the single-factor or three-factor apportionment tax formula that was afforded to domestic corporations.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant foreign corporation sought review of a decision for appellee commonwealth from the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania) in an action for a franchise tax refund.


OVERVIEW: Appellant foreign corporation challenged appellee commonwealth's determination not to provide an option to appellant in regards to how its franchise tax was calculated. Appellee claimed that because domestic corporations had a choice to use either the single-factor or three-factor apportionment tax formula under either 72 P.S. §§ 1896 or 7602(a) (Supp. 1982-1983), appellant should not have been limited to the three-factor apportionment tax formula of § 7602(a). Appellee denied the refund, which was affirmed. The court reversed and found that pursuant to U.S. Const. amend. XIV, § 1 and Pa. Const. art. 8, § 1, appellant was entitled to choose a formula, just as domestic corporations were allowed to do. The court found that there was no justification to tax appellant differently based solely on its place of incorporation and to do so was a violation of appellant's equal protection rights.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the decision for appellee commonwealth in appellant foreign corporation's action for a franchise tax refund. The court held that appellant's equal protection rights were violated if it was not afforded an option equivalent to the option to use either the single-factor or three-factor apportionment tax formula that was afforded to domestic corporations.

In re Estate of Janney


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


498 Pa. 398; 446 A.2d 1265; 1982 Pa. LEXIS 538


April 19, 1982, Argued  
June 25, 1982, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 492 January Term, 1979, Appeal from the November 26, 1979 Decree of the Court of Common Pleas of Monroe County, Orphans Court Division at No. 45-74-143


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant executrix sought review of a decision of the Court of Common Pleas of Monroe County (Pennsylvania), which declared intestacy as to the proceeds of the New Jersey property covered by a will in which appellant was an attesting witness and the beneficiary.


OVERVIEW: Appellant executrix's sister, a domiciliary of Pennsylvania, executed a will leaving her entire estate to appellant. Appellant subscribed as an attesting witness. The estate was various assets in Pennsylvania and New Jersey. The property was sold and intestate heirs excepted to distribution of the proceeds. The trial court sustained the exceptions, holding that since testator died in 1974, the law then in New Jersey denied an attesting witness benefit under the will. Thus, intestacy as to the proceeds of the New Jersey property was declared. On appeal, the court reversed. The court held that both Pennsylvania and New Jersey agreed that the testator's intent was of primary importance. The testator clearly stated that her intention was to give the property to appellant and that intent was to be fulfilled. New Jersey was not denied its inherent right to regulate title. Only the proceeds were left to be distributed. No policy of either state was offended by giving testator the intent of her will. The will itself was not contested as to testatrix' intent. The property passed without restraint and the proceeds were collected, and appellant was entitled to the proceeds.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the trial court, which declared intestacy as to the proceeds of property in New Jersey in testator's will that benefitted appellant executrix, who was an attesting witness to the will, because the testator's intent was of primary importance, even though the law in New Jersey at the time of the will's execution denied an attesting witness benefit under the will.

Commonwealth v. Stanley


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


498 Pa. 326; 446 A.2d 583; 1982 Pa. LEXIS 518


January 19, 1982, Submitted  
June 1, 1982, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 350 January Term, 1979, Appeal from the judgment of the Superior Court (October Term, 1977, No. 297) Affirming the judgment of sentence at May Term, 1976, Nos. 2177, 2178, 2180 of Philadelphia County, Court of Common Pleas, Criminal Trial Division.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the order of the Superior Court (Pennsylvania), which affirmed his convictions entered by the trial court for escape offenses, possessing an instrument of crime, and possessing a prohibited offensive weapon, 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. §§ 5721, 5722, 907(a), 908.


OVERVIEW: After escaping from prison, police arrested defendant in the home of a friend pursuant to a valid arrest warrant. Defendant was convicted of escape offenses, possessing an instrument of crime, and possessing a prohibited offensive weapon, 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. §§ 5721, 5722, 907(a), 908. Defendant was sentenced to two and one-half to nine years' imprisonment. The appellate court affirmed. In affirming the appellate court's order, the court ruled that the trial court had not erred in admitting a revolver found next to defendant into evidence because the revolver was within an area of defendant's immediate control that was properly searched incident to a lawful arrest. Thus, no search warrant or probable cause was necessary. The court also held that where police had announced their identity and waited for up to one minute before entering the dwelling, communication of their purpose was unnecessary. The court further found that defendant failed to establish a defense to the escape offense because alleged overcrowding and poor medical care in prison did not constitute force against defendant's person under 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 309.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the appellate court that affirmed defendant's convictions.

Bellomini v. State Employees' Ret. Bd.


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


498 Pa. 204; 445 A.2d 737; 1982 Pa. LEXIS 506


March 2, 1982, Argued  
May 28, 1982, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 81-1-36, Appeal from the Order of Commonwealth Court dated June 27, 1980, at No. 313 C.D. 1979, No. 3 M.D. Appeal Docket, 1982, Appeal from the Order of Commonwealth Court dated February 3, 1982, at No. 792 C.D. 1981.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, State Employees' Retirement Board, sought relief from an order of the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania), which granted retirement benefits to appellee retired state employees, after appellant previously denied the benefits under the Public Employee Forfeiture Act, 43 P.S. § 1311 et seq.


OVERVIEW: Appellees, retired state employees, challenged the denial of their retirement benefits by appellant, State Employees' Retirement Board. Appellant denied the benefits to appellees because of their dismissal from their jobs for criminal activity. Appellees filed a petition for review, invoking the original jurisdiction of the trial court and attacking the constitutionality of the Public Employee Forfeiture Act (the Act), 43 P.S. § 1311 et seq., under which they had been denied benefits due to their criminal activity while employed by the state. The trial court directed appellant to enforce appellees' retirement benefits. The court held that appellees had vested retirement rights prior to July 8, 1978 that were unaffected by the retroactive application of a law that was not in effect at the time of appellees' wrongdoings and that to apply such a law to appellees was unconstitutional. The court held that the Act did not create a condition precedent of faithful service but merely clarified its scope by listing specified crimes. Appellees had obviously met all of the requirements necessary to receive retirement pay under the Act.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's grant of retirement benefits to appellees, retired state employees, and held that appellees had vested retirement rights, and that the legislature, by retroactively denying them benefits under the Public Employee Forfeiture Act (Act) due to their criminal conduct, acted unconstitutionally to violate their contractual rights. Appellees whose rights vested prior to the Act's enactment were entitled to benefits.

Commonwealth v. Molyneaux


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


498 Pa. 192; 445 A.2d 730; 1982 Pa. LEXIS 513


January 21, 1982, Argued  
May 26, 1982, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 435 January Term, 1979, Appeal from the Order of Commonwealth Court, Filed July 9, 1979 at 1541 C.D. 1978 Reversing the Adjudication and Order of the Department of Public Welfare


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant Department of Public Welfare challenged the decision of the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania), which reversed the hearing examiner's affirmance of the action that discontinued Aid to Families with Dependent Children assistance to appellee mother and her two minor children.


OVERVIEW: Appellant Department of Public Welfare discontinued appellee mother's Aid to Families with Dependent Children (AFDC) grant, which the children had been receiving, because the father returned to the household. The lower court vacated appellant's order, and held that appellant had improperly created an unconstitutional irrebuttable presumption that a father's income was available to his children living in the same household for AFDC purposes. On appeal, the court reversed and held that because AFDC eligibility was a creation of federal law, the exclusion was permissible. The court held that the provisions of the Social Security Act (Act), which established that only two classes of dependent children were eligible for AFDC assistance, those who were deprived of parental support by reason of the death, absence, or physical or mental incapacity of a parent, and those who were deprived of parental support by reason of the unemployment of one of their parents, were constitutional. The provisions of 55 Pa. Code § 183.44 that established procedures for the calculation of AFDC grants conformed to the Act and did not violate the due process requirements of U.S. Const. amend. XIV.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and reinstated the order of appellant Department of Public Welfare, which had discontinued Aid to Families with Dependent Children (AFDC) assistance to appellee mother and her two minor children. The court held that the classifications of dependent children who were eligible for AFDC assistance were constitutional.

Bacchetta v. Bacchetta


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


498 Pa. 227; 445 A.2d 1194; 1982 Pa. LEXIS 514


January 18, 1982, Argued  
May 26, 1982, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 81-3-411, Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Chester County at No. 427 October Term, 1979, dated April 23, 1981.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant wife sought review of the order of the Court of Common Pleas of Chester County (Pennsylvania) that refused her request pursuant to the Pennsylvania Divorce Code (Code), 23 P.S. 101 et seq. (Supp. 1981), for equitable distribution of marital property acquired before the Code's effective date in this divorce action against appellee husband.


OVERVIEW: In this action for divorce governed by Pennsylvania's new Divorce Code (Code), 23 P.S. § 101 et seq. (Supp. 1981), the trial court refused the request of appellant wife, pursuant to 23 P.S. § 401(d) (Supp. 1981), for equitable distribution of property acquired before the Code's effective date on the belief that the requested distribution would have unconstitutionally deprived appellee husband of his property rights. In reversing the order and remanding the case, the court held that there was no legislative basis for restricting the applicability of the equitable distribution requirement to only that marital property acquired after the effective date of the Code. The court also concluded that where equitable distribution of property acquired during the marriage only occurred upon divorce, only in cases where it was justified, and only in amounts warranted by the facts, the application of the distribution provision to property acquired prior to the Code's effective date was constitutional.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order refusing appellant wife's request for equitable distribution of marital property against appellee husband because it found that the provision in the Divorce Code (Code) for such distribution applied to marital property acquired before the Code's effective date and that such an application was constitutional. Thus, the court remanded the record.

Fid. Bank v. Pa. Tpk. Com


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania, Middle District


498 Pa. 80; 444 A.2d 1154; 1982 Pa. LEXIS 460


January 18, 1982, Argued  
May 5, 1982, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 81-2-303, Appeal from the Orders of the Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania, entered December 14, 1981 and December 15, 1981 at No. 1774 C.D. 1981.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant Pennsylvania Turnpike Commission and appellee bond trustee challenged the order of the Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania, which dismissed as moot appellee's petition for declaratory judgment seeking the relief that appellant be permitted to conduct business with fewer than three commissioners. The order also reinstated a commissioner who had been suspended after being indicted on criminal charges.


OVERVIEW: Appellant Pennsylvania Turnpike Commission, which was to consist of five members but had only four at the relevant time, issued bonds to raise funds for the construction of the turnpike. One commissioner resigned and another was suspended for an indictment, and appellee bond trustee attempted to avoid a default on the bonds by filing a petition for declaratory judgment. Appellant sought the relief that it could lawfully conduct business with fewer than three commissioners. 36 P.S. § 652d. The lower court ordered that the suspended commissioner be reinstated, and dismissed appellee's petition as moot. On appeal, the court vacated the order of reinstatement and dismissal. The court held that the consideration of the reinstatement in ruling on appellee's petition was improper and did not effectuate the purpose of the Declaratory Judgments Act, 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 7541, to "afford relief from uncertainty." Because there were only two commissioners remaining, the court decreed that appellant could conduct the necessary business to avoid default until the lower court rendered a decision on the petition for declaratory judgment. The Commonwealth Attorneys Act was constitutional.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the order reinstating the commissioner and dismissing the declaratory judgment action, and remanded. The court decreed that the two remaining commissioners could conduct business to prevent a default on the bonds until the lower court decided the declaratory judgment action. The court affirmed the constitutionality of the Commonwealth Attorneys Act as applied to the Pennsylvania Turnpike Commission.

Zimmerman v. O'Bannon


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


497 Pa. 551; 442 A.2d 674; 1982 Pa. LEXIS 425


January 18, 1982, Argued  
March 11, 1982, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 103 Misc. Docket, 1981, Appeal from the Order of the Commonwealth Court denying Application for Stay, No. 2142 C.D. 1981, Dated September 11, 1981.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant attorney general sought review of the order of the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania), in favor of appellee Secretary of the Department of Public Welfare, denying appellant's application for a stay of promulgation of a proposed rule or regulation under 71 P.S. § 732-204(b) of the Commonwealth Attorneys Act, 71 P.S. § 732-101 et seq.


OVERVIEW: Appellant Attorney General concluded that appellee Secretary of the Department of Public Welfare's regulations were without proper statutory authority. Despite appellant's disapproval, appellee had the regulations published and they immediately became effective. The court held that the legislature conferred upon appellant the right to a stay or supersedeas upon a showing of compliance with the procedural requirements of 71 P.S. § 732-204(b), and that there was no conferral of judicial discretion to grant or deny a stay. If a stay was not granted, and the trial court agreed that the regulation was improper, that finding could provoke a lawsuit against the agency by those adversely affected by the regulation, and appellant would have established the liability of his own client. Here, the procedural prerequisites were met, so the court reversed the trial court's order and granted a stay pending final resolution of appellant's petition for review.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's order in favor of appellee Secretary of the Department of Public Welfare, denying the application of appellant Attorney General of the Commonwealth of Pennsylvania for a stay of promulgation of a proposed regulation, and granted a stay pending resolution of the petition for review, because there was compliance with the procedural requirements, and there was no judicial discretion to grant or deny a stay.

OUTCOME: The court affirmed appellant's conviction for statutory rape, holding that it was not unconstitutional that mistake was not a defense to the charge and that there was sufficient evidence to show appellant was over age 18 when the crime occurred.

In re Reapportionment Plan for Pennsylvania General Assembly


W. D. Misc. Docket Nos. 331, 333, 337, 339, 342, 343, 346, 347, 348, 1981, M. D. Misc. Docket Nos. 118 and 119, 1981, E. D. Misc. Docket Nos. 535, 542, 556, 558, 584, 588, 590, 591, 594, 595, 596, 597, 599, 601, 602, 605, 606, 607, 1981


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


497 Pa. 525; 442 A.2d 661; 1981 Pa. LEXIS 1167


December 7, 1981, Argued  
December 29, 1981, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Denied January 18 and January 29, 1982.


PRIOR HISTORY: W.D. Misc. Docket Nos. 331, 333, 337, 339, 342, 343, 346-348, 1981, M.D. Misc. Docket Nos. 118 and 119, 1981, E.D. Misc. Docket Nos. 535, 542, 556, 558, 584, 588, 590, 591, 594-597, 599, 601, 602, 605-607, 1981.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, interested individuals, sought review, by consolidated appeal, of the Final Legislative Reapportionment Plan (final plan) filed by appellee Pennsylvania Legislative Reapportionment Commission. Appellants contested the final plan pursuant to Pa. Const. art. II, § 17(d), contending that the final plan violated the requirement of Pa. Const. art. 11, §16, that districts be compact and political subdivisions not unnecessarily divided.


OVERVIEW: Appellants, interested individuals, challenged appellee Pennsylvania Legislative Reapportionment Commission's Final Legislative Reapportionment Plan (final plan), claiming that district deviations from compactness and subdivision boundaries were constitutionally only permissible under Pa. Const. art. II, § 16 if the deviations were absolutely necessary to survive federal equal protection analysis. The court found that appellants had the burden of establishing that appellee's final plan did not meet constitutional requirements. The court held the final plan complied with Pa. Const. art. II, § 16, and declared it to have the force of law under Pa. Const. art. II, § 17(e), holding that the final plan achieved an equality of population closer to the constitutional ideal of "one person, one vote" than any previous reapportionment plan in the history of the commonwealth, because the range of deviation between the highest and lowest populated districts was only 1.9 percent from the ideal Senate district and 2.8 percent from the ideal House district. Thus, there was no doubt that the final plan satisfied equal protection requirements.


OUTCOME: The court found that appellants, interested individuals, failed to establish that appellee Pennsylvania Legislative Reapportionment Commission's Final Legislative Reapportionment Plan (final plan) did not comply with federal equal protection requirements or state constitutional provisions, because the final plan achieved an acceptable equality of population among legislative districts and did not unnecessarily divide political subdivisions.

Commonwealth v. Story


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


497 Pa. 273; 440 A.2d 488; 1981 Pa. LEXIS 1145


September 15, 1981, Argued  
December 28, 1981, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Denied February 5, 1982.


PRIOR HISTORY: No. 80-1-154, Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County, Criminal Division, CC7405836, Entered July 21, 1980.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged the judgment of sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County (Pennsylvania), which sentenced appellant to death following retrial following appellant's conviction for murder of the first degree.


OVERVIEW: Appellant had been tried and convicted of murder in the first degree. He appealed the judgment of sentence of death. The court set aside the death sentence and imposed a sentence of life imprisonment because appellant was subject to punishment only under the law preceding 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 9711.The previous law governing the case had been declared unconstitutional insofar as it authorized the death penalty. It would have been contrary to due process to subject appellant to the death penalty on retrial where he had originally had been sentenced to life imprisonment. It would have been an impermissible penalty for the exercise of appellate rights. The sole permissible maximum punishment for appellant's crime committed prior to application of the new death penalty statute was life imprisonment. A statute was required to be construed to avoid unconstitutional results. Where the application of the death penalty statute on retrial would have violated equal protection and due process, the statute had to be construed not to apply.


OUTCOME: The court set aside the trial court's judgment of sentence of death and imposed a sentence of life imprisonment after appellant was convicted of murder in the first degree. The court held that the application of the death penalty statute on retrial would have violated appellant's equal protection and due process rights and was an impermissible penalty for the exercise of appellate rights.

Beckert v. Warren


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


497 Pa. 137; 439 A.2d 638; 1981 Pa. LEXIS 1147


October 28, 1981, Argued  
December 22, 1981, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 1 E.D. Misc. Docket 1981, Action in Equity and Mandamus under this Court's Plenary Jurisdiction.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff court officials brought this action in the Court of Common Pleas of Buck County (Pennsylvania), and then sought plenary jurisdiction of the court, which remanded the matter to the intermediate court. Plaintiff's sought an injunction to stop the adoption of a budget after their budget negotiations failed with defendant county commissioners, who declined to fund several new court staff positions.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff court personnel requested that defendant county commissioners provide funding in their new budget for more staff positions which were based upon the court's immediate and anticipated future needs. After negotiations failed, plaintiff sought a grant of plenary jurisdiction which was granted along with remanding the matter for an expedited hearing before the intermediate court. The intermediate court did not render a judgment but made findings of fact which included determinations that plaintiff had met the burden of proof showing reasonable necessity as to many of the new staff positions, but not all. Both parties challenged the intermediate court's findings. Upon review, the court maintained ins disinterested status and modified the findings of fact to varying degrees, mainly in favor of plaintiff. It found that plaintiff correctly had the burden of proving reasonable necessity and had mostly maintained that burden. Defendants claimed that the intermediate court erred in denying them their right to a jury trial, but this was dismissed as plaintiffs' action was based in legal and equitable principles, and as such, no jury trial right existed.


OUTCOME: The court modified the findings of fact of the intermediate court to various degrees, but generally ruled in favor of plaintiff court personnel's requests to budget for more staff positions and its request for cost of living wage increase. Further, the court found that plaintiff had carried its burden of proof and that defendants were properly denied a jury trial because the legal and equitable nature of the action did not require such a trial.

Commonwealth v. Senk


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


496 Pa. 630; 437 A.2d 1218; 1981 Pa. LEXIS 1134


October 19, 1981, Submitted  
December 18, 1981, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No 80-30-714, Appeal from the Order entered August 21, 1980 by the Court of Common Pleas of Columbia County, Criminal Division, to No. 2, January Term, 1978


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of a decision of the Court of Common Pleas of Columbia County (Pennsylvania), which denied his second Post Conviction Hearing Act following a jury trial that resulted in his conviction for murder of the first degree.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was charged and convicted for murder in the first degree. After filing a petition under the Post Conviction Hearing Act (PCHA), 19 P.S. § 1180-1 et seq. (amended 1981), and appealing to the supreme court, appellant filed a second PCHA petition that was denied by the lower court. Appellant appealed and the court affirmed. The court noted that issues already litigated could not be relitigated by appellant by raising them in his PCHA petition. The court held that the issue of appellant's confession had already been considered by the court; thus the issue could not be relitigated. The court noted that appellant did not offer any evidence to establish that any extraordinary circumstance existed which would have excused his failure to raise this issue at an earlier proceeding, nor did appellant offer any evidence to rebut the presumption that his failure to raise the issue was knowing and understanding. Therefore, the court held that appellant had waived this issue and his PCHA petition was properly denied.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's decision denying appellant's Post Conviction Hearing Act (PCHA) petition. The court held that the PCHA prevented issues that had already been litigated from being relitigated by appellant. The court further held that appellant waived all other issues included in his petition because he had not previously raised them, and because there was no showing of extraordinary circumstance to prevent such waiver.

Carroll v. County of York


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania
