November 6, 1981, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargued September 17, 1981. Reargument Denied December 14, 1981.


PRIOR HISTORY: No. 152 W.D. Misc. Dkt. 1980, Assumption of Plenary Jurisdiction by the Supreme Court of Pennsylvania by Order dated September 2, 1980 over Complaint in Trespass and Preliminary Objections now pending in the Court of Common Pleas of York County at No. 79-S-1326 -- Civil Action.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff estate challenged the constitutionality of Pennsylvania's Political Subdivision Tort Claims Act, 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 8541 et seq., after defendant county asserted immunity in appellant's wrongful death action brought in the Court of Common Pleas of York County (Pennsylvania).


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff estate's decedent committed suicide while in a county detention home. Plaintiff brought wrongful death and survival claims against defendant county and defendant asserted immunity from suit under the Political Subdivision Tort Claims Act (the Act), 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 8541 et seq. Plaintiff sought plenary review of the constitutionality of the Act from the Supreme Court. The court held that the Act was constitutional and dismissed plaintiff's complaint. Pa. Const. art. I, § 11, provided that actions could be brought against the state in such manner and in such cases as the legislature directed. The court held that this provision allowed governmental immunity. The court also held that the same constitutional provision applied to political subdivisions.


OUTCOME: The court held that the Political Subdivision Tort Claims Act was constitutional and dismissed plaintiff estate's complaint, holding that the state constitution gave the legislature the right to choose cases in which the state should be immune.

 

151 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Ballou v. State Ethics Com


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


496 Pa. 127; 436 A.2d 186; 1981 Pa. LEXIS 1171


May 20, 1981, Argued  
October 30, 1981, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Application for Reargument Denied December 1, 1981.


PRIOR HISTORY: No. 81-3-374, Appeal from the Order of the Commonwealth Court dated February 4, 1981, at No. 1013 C.D. 1980.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant ethics commission sought review of the judgment of the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania), which declared that appellee private practitioner was not subject to the financial disclosure requirements of the Ethics Act, 65 P. S. § 401 et seq. (Supp. 1981-1982).


OVERVIEW: Appellee private practitioner filed an action for declaratory and injunctive relief from the application of the financial disclosure requirements of the Ethics Act (Act), 65 P.S. § 401 et seq. (Supp. 1981-1982), which appellant ethics commission had by regulation declared to apply to appellee. The trial court overruled appellant's preliminary objections and granted relief to appellee. The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court and held that appellee, in his capacity as a solicitor, was neither a public employee, nor a public official within the scope of the Act and, therefore, was not statutorily compelled to comply with the Act's financial disclosure requirements. Absent a clear expression of legislative intent to treat municipal solicitors differently from others who served government in an advisory capacity with neither powers of sovereignty, nor attributes of a public employee, the court concluded that the financial disclosure provisions of the Act did not apply to appellee, who served his municipal clients just as any privately employed attorney served his clients.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court, which declared that appellee private practitioner was not subject to the financial disclosure requirements of the Ethics Act, and held that appellee was not obligated to make financial disclosures under the Ethics Act because appellee only served in an advisory capacity and retained neither powers of sovereignty, nor attributes of a public employee.

 

152 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Laudenberger v. Port Authority of Allegheny County


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


496 Pa. 52; 436 A.2d 147; 1981 Pa. LEXIS 1000; 40 A.L.R.4th 127


March 2, 1981, Argued  
October 29, 1981, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 80 - 1 - 160, Appeal from the Judgment entered 7/30/80 in the Court of Common Pleas, Civil Division, of Allegheny County, at No. G.D. 78-5062.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant injured challenged the judgment of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County (Pennsylvania) that held that Pa. R. Civ. P. 238, which pertained to prejudgment interest, was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The Supreme Court of Pennsylvania promulgated Pa. R. Civ. P. 238, which provided for prejudgment interest in certain instances. The trial court held the rule to be unconstitutional. The supreme court reversed. The court noted that pursuant to Pa. Const. art. V, § 10(c), the court was empowered to prescribe procedural rules if they did not abridge, enlarge, or modify the substantive rights of litigants. However, the line between procedure and substantive rights could be drawn only in light of the rule's purpose. The court stated that federal diversity and conflicts of law cases, where interest as an element of damages had been characterized as substantive law, did not control. Pa. R. Civ. P. 238, when viewed from the perspective of its purpose and goal to aid in the orderly and efficient administration of justice, was procedural and within the power of the court to promulgate. The rule violated neither equal protection nor substantive due process, because it advanced the objective of the rule in a manner consistent with the precepts of equal protection, and it was rationally related to the legitimate state goal of encouraging settlement offers in tort litigation.


OUTCOME: The supreme court reversed the judgment of the trial court, which had held that the prejudgment interest rule was unconstitutional. Promulgation of the rule was within the constitutional powers of the court, and the rule violated neither equal protection nor substantive due process.

 

153 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Snider v. Thornburgh


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


496 Pa. 159; 436 A.2d 593; 1981 Pa. LEXIS 968


May 22, 1980, Argued  
September 29, 1981, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 51 May Term, 1979, Appeal from the Order of the Commonwealth Court filed August 31, 1979, at 55 C.D. 1979


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, elected school directors, challenged the judgment of the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania), which held that the Public Officials Ethics Law, 65 P.S. 401 et seq. (Supp. 1980-1981), did not violate the constitutions of Pennsylvania or of the United States.


OVERVIEW: The Public Officials Ethics Law (Act), 65 P.S. 401 et seq. (Supp. 1980-1981), required that candidates for public office, elected officials, and certain appointed officials file financial disclosure statements. Appellants, elected school directors, filed an action against appellees, governor and other state officials, and alleged that the Act was unconstitutional. The trial court dismissed the petition. The court held the legislation was constitutional, except for 65 P.S. 402, which the court severed. That section exempted from the disclosure requirements appointed officials who received no compensation other than reimbursement for expenses. The court noted that because all school board members served without compensation, the Act distinguished between board members who were elected and those who were appointed. The court held that the classification was not reasonable, but was arbitrary, and that it acted upon a difference having no fair and substantial relation to the object of the act. However, the Act did not violate the constitutional right to privacy because its intrusion into appellants' private affairs was reasonably tailored to fit a legitimate legislative function.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the trial court, insofar as the trial court held that certain provisions that granted exemption from financial disclosure requirements under the Public Officials Ethics Law were constitutional, but otherwise affirmed the judgment of the trial court, which held that the Public Officials Ethics Law did not violate the constitutions of Pennsylvania or the United States.

McKenna v. State Employees' Retirement Bd.


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


495 Pa. 324; 433 A.2d 871; 1981 Pa. LEXIS 1180


January 19, 1981, Argued  
July 16, 1981, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Denied September 10, 1981.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant Pennsylvania State Employees' Retirement Board challenged the decision of the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania), which granted appellees, a class of state court judges, relief from the actions of the Commonwealth Compensation Commission and the provisions of the 1974 State Employees' Retirement Code, 71 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 5101 et seq., which impaired appellees' benefits under the State Employees' Retirement Code of 1959.


OVERVIEW: Appellant Pennsylvania State Employees' Retirement Board challenged the decision of the trial court, which granted appellees, a class of state court judges, relief from the 1972 final average salary freeze of the Commonwealth Compensation Commission and the reduction of multipliers contained in the 1974 State Employees' Retirement Code, 71 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 5101 et seq. On appeal, the court affirmed, holding that appellees had complied with the conditions necessary to receive retirement under the State Employees' Retirement Code of 1959 (1959 Code), where all had 10 years or more of service and had either reached the age of 60 or were assured of a retirement allowance upon reaching the age of 60, and therefore alteration of their benefits package was precluded. The court held that the challenged actions impaired appellees' previously established right to have their retirement benefits computed in accordance with the formula mandated by the 1959 Code, which entitled them to a calculation of benefits based on the highest salaries they received during their years of service and the multipliers specified in the 1959 Code.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision granting appellees, a class of state court judges, relief from the Commonwealth Compensation Commission's final average salary freeze and the 1974 State Employees' Retirement Code's reduction of multipliers, where appellees had complied with the service requirements and other conditions necessary under the State Employees' Retirement Code of 1959, and alteration of their benefits package was therefore precluded.

 

157 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Hack v. Hack


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


495 Pa. 300; 433 A.2d 859; 1981 Pa. LEXIS 848


January 22, 1981, Argued  
July 14, 1981, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 516 January Term, 1978, Appeal from the Order of the Superior Court of Pennsylvania, entered October 2, 1978 at No. 756 October Term, 1978 affirming the Order of the Court of Common Pleas, Montgomery County, entered December 27, 1977 at No. 73-05091 - Civil Action Law.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant injured wife sought review of a decision from the Superior Court of Pennsylvania, which affirmed a decision granting appellee husband's motion for summary judgment based upon interspousal immunity, thereby protecting him from recovery by appellant for injuries she sustained as a result of his negligence in an automobile accident.


OVERVIEW: Appellant injured wife sought damages from appellee husband for injuries she sustained in an automobile accident as a result of his negligence. Appellee motioned for summary judgment based upon interspousal immunity, which was granted by the trial court. The lower court's determination was affirmed on appellate review, but appellant continued to challenge the findings. The court, after lengthy review of historical statutes and the Married Persons Property Act, 48 P.S. § 1-1 et seq., determined that interspousal immunity should be abolished and reversed the lower courts. In so holding, the court determined that the spousal role in modern society had changed and to rely on an antiquated doctrine was illogical and failed to serve public policy. The court reasoned that family harmony was not served and, especially in light of insurance indemnification, the otherwise resulting financial hardship would only serve as a detriment to such harmony. The court also noted the potential methods of indirect recovery in support of its decision to abolish the doctrine.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the lower court's affirmation of the granting of appellee husband's summary judgment. The court found in favor of appellant injured wife, determining that interspousal immunity was an antiquated doctrine which had lost usefulness in modern society and that appellant could recover for injuries sustained as a result of appellee's negligence.

 

158 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Chester v. Commonwealth


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


495 Pa. 382; 434 A.2d 695; 1981 Pa. LEXIS 849


October 22, 1980, Argued  
July 14, 1981, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 376 January Term, 1979, Appeal from the Order of the Commonwealth Court at No. 1977 C.D. 1977, reversing the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Delaware County at No. 9309 of 1974.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city challenged an order of the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania), which reversed the trial court's order confirming a report by the board of viewers in a condemnation of a bridge by appellee, department of transportation. Appellant argued that the lower court erred by denying appellant's right to a trial de novo when it usurped the factfinding function of the trial court.


OVERVIEW: Appellee, department of transportation, condemned a bridge for highway use within appellant city. Appellant's complaint to the board of viewers was confirmed by the trial court after a de novo trial, but was reversed by the appellate court. Appellant claimed that the appellate court's decision denied it the right to a trial de novo by usurping the factfinding function of the trial court. The court held that the appellate court did not exceed its authority, but that it had resolved the matter improperly. Thus, it reversed the appellate court's order. The court disagreed with the appellate court's decision that appellee was not required to pay appellant for the cost to replace the condemned bridge. Although the court determined that such costs were includable in a damage award, it was unable to make the cost determination from the record before it because the condemned bridge had no ascertainable fair market value. Therefore, the court remanded for an evidentiary hearing. The court held that the Pennsylvania constitution did not allow appellee to escape its financial obligation to a public condemnee for property taken.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the appellate court's reversal of the trial court's decision after de novo trial confirmed a report of the board of viewers in appellee, department of transportation's, condemnation of a bridge. The court held that the appellate court did not exceed its authority, but decided in error. Appellee was required to compensate appellant city for the bridge, but because the record did not show the bridge's value, the court remanded.

Estate of Dulles


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


494 Pa. 180; 431 A.2d 208; 1981 Pa. LEXIS 901; 17 A.L.R.4th 1279


January 27, 1981, Argued  
July 2, 1981, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Nos. 98, 103, 104 January Term, 1979, Appeal from Final Decree of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia, Orphans' Court Division, dated February 13, 1979, at No. 1123 of 1951.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: All parties appealed a decree of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia (Pennsylvania), declaring that appellant, grandchild born out of wedlock, was entitled to share trust income equally with appellants, other two grandchildren. Appellant trustee requested a determination of whether the lower court had fashioned an appropriate remedy.


OVERVIEW: A settlor died leaving a trust for the benefit of her grandchildren. A dispute arose as to whether appellant, grandchild born out of wedlock, was entitled to share the trust income with appellants, other two grandchildren. Appellant trustee had reserved a portion of trust income against the possibility that appellant, grandchild born out of wedlock, might be entitled to share. The court modified the decree of the lower court, and as modified, affirmed it. The court held that, although all three grandchildren were to share future trust income equally, on the record before the court, appellant, grandchild born out of wedlock, could recover only the amount of past trust income which appellant trustee had reserved pending final adjudication of the parties' claims. The lower court properly held unconstitutional the canon of construction on the "burden of inertia" which barred appellant, grandchild born out of wedlock, from the enjoyment of trust income. Neither 20 P.S. § 180.14(7) nor 20 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 2514(8) had any rational relationship to a legitimate state purpose, and therefore both were constitutionally infirm under the equal protection clause of U.S. Const. amend. XIV.


OUTCOME: The court modified the lower court's decree, and as modified, affirmed it. Appellant, grandchild born out of wedlock, was entitled to share income from a trust held by appellant trustee, equally with appellant, other two grandchildren, because statutes barring such children from sharing fathers' estates were unconstitutional as violative of equal protection. The "burden of inertia" canon of construction was unconstitutional.

In re Determination of Priority of Commision Among Certain Judges


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


493 Pa. 555; 427 A.2d 153; 1981 Pa. LEXIS 824


October 15, 1980, Argued  
March 24, 1981, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 97 W.D.Misc.Dkt. 1980


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner judges commenced an action for declaratory judgment seeking to determine seniority in their judicial positions. The court then assumed extraordinary jurisdiction pursuant to 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 726.


OVERVIEW: Several judges simultaneously assumed office. Petitioner judges were appointed prior to assuming elected office. Petitioners requested a declaratory judgment that they be granted seniority over other judges that assumed elected office simultaneously. The petition was originally in a lower court, but the court assumed extraordinary jurisdiction under 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. §726. The court rejected petitioners' argument that Pa. Cons. art. V, § 10(e) (1968), contained language abrogating the traditional method of determining seniority among contemporaneous judges by the casting of lots, and held that legislative history underlying the section envisioned the casting of lots.


OUTCOME: The court held that seniority among judges who assumed office at the same time should be determined by the casting of lots because legislative history showed that such was the intent behind a redrafting of the portion of the Pennsylvania Constitution addressing the issue.

Commonwealth v. One 1976 Ford Truck Van


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


492 Pa. 541; 424 A.2d 1323; 1981 Pa. LEXIS 673


October 22, 1980, Argued  
February 5, 1981, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 406 January Term, 1978, Appeal from Order dated October 3, 1978, Court of Common Pleas, Criminal Division, Lehigh County, No. 53-M of 1977.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The commonwealth appealed an order of the Court of Common Pleas, Criminal Division, Lehigh County (Pennsylvania), in the commonwealth's forfeiture action against defendant's truck under the provisions of the Controlled Substance, Drug, Device and Cosmetic Act, 35 P.S. §§ 780-128 and 780-129.


OVERVIEW: The commonwealth brought a forfeiture action against defendant's truck under the provisions of the Controlled Substance, Drug, Device and Cosmetic Act, 35 P.S. §§ 780-128 and 780-129 (the Act). The trial court found that the forfeiture provisions of the Act violated defendant's right to due process of law under U.S. Const. amend. XIV, § 1 and the applicable provisions of the Pennsylvania Constitution, in that they failed to mandate notice or an opportunity to be heard prior to forfeiture. The commonwealth appealed on the grounds that defendant lacked standing to challenge the constitutionality of the forfeiture provisions of the Act. The court, without reaching the constitutional issues involved, reversed, holding that defendant did not have standing to question the constitutionality of the Act absent a showing that his constitutional rights were violated by its enforcement. The court noted that even if the forfeiture provisions of the Act were unconstitutional on their face, any violation of defendant's rights that would have resulted from strict compliance with the Act had been prevented by the actual notice and opportunity for hearing that defendant received.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgment, holding that defendant did not have standing to question the constitutionality of a forfeiture law on due process grounds because he had been given notice and a chance to be heard.

Hayes v. Erie Ins. Exchange


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


493 Pa. 150; 425 A.2d 419; 1981 Pa. LEXIS 684; 14 A.L.R.4th 752


October 2, 1980, Argued  
February 5, 1981, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 80-1-127, Appeal from the Order of the Supreme Court at NO. 455 April Term, 1979, affirming the Judgment of the Erie County Court of Common Pleas at No. 1886-A-1976.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant injured sought review of an order of the Supreme Court (Pennsylvania), which affirmed the judgment of the Erie County Court of Common Pleas (Pennsylvania), which entered summary judgment against appellee insurance company in appellant's initial suit for damages for no-fault insurance benefits, but denied appellant's motion for assessment of damages at 18 percent interest and attorney's fees.


OVERVIEW: Appellant injured was involved in a motor vehicle collision with an automobile insured by appellee insurance company. Appellant did not carry insurance covering no-fault benefits on her own vehicle. Appellee refused to pay no-fault benefits, and appellant filed suit. After the trial court ordered payment of benefits, appellee paid the benefits to appellant, but attorney's fees were not included in the award because appellee had not acted in bad faith in contesting payment of no-fault benefits. Appellant sought payment of both attorney's fees and an 18 percent penalty for interest as required by 40 Pa. Stat. § 10009.106(a)(1). The court affirmed the denial of attorney's fees because the lower court had found that appellee had not acted in bad faith in contesting payment of the no-fault benefits. The court reversed the refusal to award the 18 percent interest on the benefits, and the court held that, irrespective of good faith on the part of appellee, the 18 percent interest provisions of the statute applied.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the lower court, which denied attorney's fees to appellant injured in her claim for no-fault insurance benefits against appellee insurance company, because the trial court found appellee had not acted in bad faith in contesting the payment of benefits. The court reversed the denial of 18 percent interest because appellee was required to pay this amount irrespective of good faith pursuant to statute.

Central Dauphin School Dist. v. American Casualty Co.


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


493 Pa. 254; 426 A.2d 94; 1981 Pa. LEXIS 598; 21 A.L.R.4th 884


May 19, 1980, Argued  
February 4, 1981, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Denied March 13, 1981.


PRIOR HISTORY: No. 80-2-259, Appeal from the Order of the Superior Court at No. 229 March Term, 1978, dated October 19, 1979, affirming the Judgment of the Court of Common Pleas of Dauphin County at No. 1700 September Term, 1975, dated September 29, 1978.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant insurer challenged a decision of the Superior Court (Pennsylvania), which affirmed the judgment of the lower court, which found the insurer liable to appellee school district for unlawfully collected taxes.


OVERVIEW: Appellee school district sought to recover from appellant insurer the amount that the district had been required to return to taxpayers against whom the district imposed an unlawful tax. The reviewing court reversed the lower court's judgment, holding that "loss" had to be construed in view of Pennsylvania public policy. Since public policy would have been offended by permitting a political subdivision to use public funds to purchase "insurance" against court-ordered and statutorily-mandated refunding of taxes collected unlawfully, the refunds had to be paid out of budget appropriations of public funds. Because Pennsylvania's public policy did not permit a school district to make unlawful taxation as revenue-productive as lawful taxation, it was concluded that a political subdivision's return of tax monies to its taxpayers collected by an unlawful tax was uninsurable.


OUTCOME: Reversed and remanded because Pennsylvania public policy did not permit unlawfully collected taxes to be insured.

Commonwealth v. Ward


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


493 Pa. 115; 425 A.2d 401; 1981 Pa. LEXIS 688


March 3, 1980, Submitted  
February 4, 1981, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 109 March Term, 1979, Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Beaver County, Pennsylvania, Criminal Division at No. 291 of 1979, denying the Appellant's Motion to Dismiss the First Degree Murder Charge


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought interlocutory review of an order of the Court of Common Pleas of Beaver County, Criminal Division (Pennsylvania), which denied defendant's pretrial motion to dismiss the charge of first degree murder under an indictment for murder generally.


OVERVIEW: Defendant entered a plea bargain to charges of murder generally, with the stipulation that the degree of murder adjudicated would rise no higher than murder of the second degree. Defendant challenged the plea bargain, and the reviewing court held that the plea colloquy was inadequate, and granted defendant a new trial. Prior to retrial, defendant filed a pretrial motion in which he contended that the prosecution's intention to proceed against him on a charge of first degree murder would violate double jeopardy. The trial court denied the motion. On interlocutory appeal, the court affirmed, holding that the trial court's approval of defendant's plea bargain to lesser charges did not constitute an implied acquittal on the greater charges or give the trial court a full opportunity to return a verdict on the greater charges. Further, the due process clause was not offended by the possibility of increased punishment upon defendant's retrial because there was no realistic likelihood of vindictiveness. The court held that where defendant revoked his plea bargain, it was not vindictive to require defendant to assume his pre-agreement status.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the denial of defendant's pretrial motion to dismiss the first degree murder charge, because defendant's retrial after he successfully appealed his plea bargain did not violate double jeopardy.

 

167 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Futch


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


492 Pa. 359; 424 A.2d 1231; 1981 Pa. LEXIS 789


October 3, 1980, Argued  
January 30, 1981, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 80-1-93, Appeal from the order of the Court of Common Pleas, Allegheny County, Criminal Division, at CC7501862A, May 2, 1980.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The commonwealth challenged an order of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County (Pennsylvania) which held that the statute authorizing peremptory challenges was unconstitutional as applied in the selection of jurors for defendant's trial. The court entered its order after a hearing on defendant's motion to prevent the commonwealth from exercising its peremptory challenges without restriction to strike black prospective jurors.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, a black man, was accused of the murder of a white inmate in the prison where he was incarcerated. At trial, the commonwealth challenged the first venireman, who was black. Defendant requested an evidentiary hearing and the trial court found prima facie case of discrimination in the commonwealth's using peremptory challenges on black jurors. The court reversed the order of the trial court and remanded. Defendant could not establish a prima facie case of discrimination merely by producing evidence of a past exclusionary practice by the commonwealth. There was no proof that the challenged practice was been applied to defendant, thereby causing deprivation of a constitutional right. The prosecutor's peremptory challenge of one black prospective juror, the only venireman to be questioned on individual voir dire, accompanied by opinion evidence as to the practice of some individual assistant district attorneys in past trials, was insufficient to raise the inference of improper use of the peremptory challenge by the commonwealth. The trial court erred in finding that defendant had established a prima facie case of discriminatory exclusion of black prospective jurors.


OUTCOME: The court concluded that defendant did not establish that the commonwealth impermissibly exercised its peremptory challenges to strike black prospective jurors in the trial of his case. Defendant failed to show that the challenged practice applied to him and was used in his trial. The court vacated the order of the trial court and remanded the case for trial.

Commonwealth v. Wadzinski


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


492 Pa. 35; 422 A.2d 124; 1980 Pa. LEXIS 793


April 14, 1980, Argued  
September 22, 1980, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 354 January Term, 1979, Appeal from the Order of the Superior Court of Pennsylvania, entered May 4, 1979, at No. 734 October Term, 1975


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed an order of the Superior Court of Pennsylvania, which upheld the constitutionality of 25 P.S. § 3234 (Supp. 1978-1979) and affirmed defendant's conviction for violating the section that required candidates for public office to give written notice before publishing advertisements referring to opponents.


OVERVIEW: Defendant campaigned for the office of mayor and lost. On the day before the election, he caused to be broadcast on the radio a political advertisement in which he referred to his opponent and raised issues of character. He did not send written notice of the advertisement to his opponent before the broadcast. Defendant was charged with a violation of 25 P.S. § 3234 (Supp. 1978-1979). That statute required candidates running political advertisements that referred to their opponents within 48 hours of an election to give their opponents written notice of the advertisements in sufficient time for a reply advertisement to be published. Defendant was found guilty and challenged his conviction. The lower court affirmed his conviction and upheld the constitutionality of the statute. On appeal, the court reversed the order of the lower court and struck down the statute as unconstitutional. The court held that 25 P.S. § 3234 violated U.S. Const. amends. I and XIV because the governmental interest that it advanced was inadequate to justify such a substantial burden on protected speech rights.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the lower court and ruled that the statute was unconstitutional because the governmental interest advanced by the statute was inadequate to justify the substantial burden it imposed on protected speech.

Wajert v. State Ethics Com


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


491 Pa. 255; 420 A.2d 439; 1980 Pa. LEXIS 804


May 22, 1980, Submitted  
September 22, 1980, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 80-2-250, Appeal from Order of Commonwealth Court at No. 1108 C.D. 1979


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant State Ethics Commission sought review of an order from the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania), which entered judgment in favor of appellee former judge and declared that 65 P.S. § 403(e) was of no force and effect to judges of the courts of common pleas. 65 P.S. § 403(e) provided that no former official should represent a person before the governmental body with which he was associated for one year after he left that body.


OVERVIEW: Appellant State Ethics Commission opined that appellee former judge was barred by 65 P.S. § 403(e) from representing any person before the court with which he was associated for a period of one year following resignation or retirement, but the lower court reversed the opinion and entered an order in favor of appellee. The court disagreed with the lower court's determination that a court of law was not within the definitional purview of "governmental body," and that therefore 65 P.S. § 403(e) did not apply to appellee. The court found that 65 P.S. § 403(e) applied to appellee and other common pleas judges who resigned or retired. However, the court found the statute was unconstitutional insofar as it was applicable to a former judge. The court affirmed the lower court's ruling in favor of appellee, holding that application of the statute to a former judge constituted an infringement on the state supreme court's inherent and exclusive power to govern the conduct of those privileged to practice law in the state.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's judgment in favor of appellee former judge and found that appellee was not barred from representing any person before the court with which he had been associated. The court found the statute was unconstitutional because its application constituted an infringement on the state supreme court's inherent and exclusive power to govern the conduct of those privileged to practice law in the state.

 

171 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Wagner v. National Indem. Co.


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


492 Pa. 154; 422 A.2d 1061; 1980 Pa. LEXIS 822


May 19, 1980, Argued  
September 22, 1980, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Denied December 19, 1980.


PRIOR HISTORY: No. 54 May Term, 1979, Appeal from Order of the Superior Court dated May 16, 1979, at No. 210 March Term, 1978


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant administratrix sought review of the order of the Superior Court (Pennsylvania), which denied appellant's claim against appellee indemnity carrier for damages as the result of decedent's death pursuant to the Pennsylvania No-Fault Motor Vehicle Insurance Act, 40 P.S. § 1009.101 et seq.


OVERVIEW: Appellant administratrix filed an action in assumpsit against appellee indemnity carrier for damages received by decedent while at work in a fatal truck accident under Pennsylvania No-Fault Motor Vehicle Insurance Act, 40 P.S. § 1009.101 et seq. Appellee, the no-fault automobile insurance carrier of decedent's employer, denied the claim and asserted that it was entitled to the employer's immunity from suit under the Workmen's Compensation Act, 77 P.S. § 481(a). The trial court dismissed, the appellate court affirmed the dismissal, and appellant sought review. The court affirmed the appellate court's judgment because decedent was injured in the scope of his employment, and therefore appellant could recover only workmen's compensation benefits from the employer or the employer's workmen's compensation carrier. The court held that 77 P.S. § 303 afforded the exclusive remedy to appellant for decedent's injuries unless the employer voluntarily relinquished the protection of the liability. Because no-fault coverage was statutorily mandated, there was no voluntary relinquishment by providing no-fault coverage.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's dismissal of the claim by appellant administratrix against appellee indemnity carrier for damages resulting from decedent's death because appellant was limited to filing a workmen's compensation claim against decedent's employer or workmen's compensation carrier. The court found that decedent's employer did not voluntarily relinquish the protection of liability by providing no-fault coverage to his employees.

Mattos v. Thompson


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


491 Pa. 385; 421 A.2d 190; 1980 Pa. LEXIS 775


June 24, 1980, Argued  
September 22, 1980, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 124 E.D. Misc. Docket 1979, Plenary Jurisdiction Assumed by the Supreme Court over a Petition for Declaratory Judgment filed in the Commonwealth Court at No. 1262, C.D. 1979, Plenary Jurisdiction Assumed by the Supreme Court over an Appeal Pending in the Superior Court at No. 1372, October Term, 1979 from the Order of June 5, 1979, of the Court of Common Pleas of Northampton County, Civil Trial Division, at No. 1979-C-2679


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner wife appealed an order of the Court of Common Pleas of Northampton County (Pennsylvania), which dismissed her action for trespass against respondent doctor under the Pennsylvania Health Care Services Malpractice Act, 40 P.S. § 1301.101 et seq.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner wife had filed an action in trespass against respondent doctor seeking damages for the death of her husband resulting from respondent's negligence. The action was dismissed because the Pennsylvania Health Care Services Malpractice Act, 40 P.S. § 1301.101 et seq. (Act), deprived the trial court of subject matter jurisdiction. Petitioner appealed to have the court assume plenary jurisdiction over her appeal of the dismissal and of another action for declaratory judgment. The court consolidated both actions and transferred them for an evidentiary hearing. The hearing revealed high percentages of cases which had been filed under the Act but had not been resolved. The court granted the declaratory judgment for petitioner, reversed the order dismissing the complaint in trespass, and remanded. The court declared that 40 P.S. § 1301.309, giving health care arbitration panels original, exclusive jurisdiction over medical malpractice claims, was unconstitutional. The court found that the lengthy delay, caused by the arbitration system, burdened the right of a jury trial with onerous conditions, restrictions, or regulations which made the right practically unavailable.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order dismissing petitioner wife's complaint in trespass against respondent doctor, granted a declaratory judgment for petitioner, and remanded, holding that malpractice legislation was unconstitutional because its arbitration system burdened the right of a jury trial with onerous conditions, restrictions, or regulations which made the right practically unavailable.

Allstate Ins. Co. v. Heffner


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


491 Pa. 447; 421 A.2d 629; 1980 Pa. LEXIS 784


April 17, 1980, Argued

PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 80-3-372, Appeal from the Order of the Superior Court of Pennsylvania at No. 2145, October Term 1977, Reversing the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County at No. 3406 November Term 1976 In Assumpsit, No. 73 May Term, 1979, Appeal from the Order of the Superior Court of Pennsylvania dated July 3, 1979, docketed to No. 80 March Term, 1978, reversing the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Dauphin County of April 7, 1978, to No. 2702 S, 1977


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a consolidated action, appellant insurance companies sought review of a decision of the Superior Court of Pennsylvania that reversed the orders of the trial court and held that both survivor's and work loss benefits were available to appellees, survivors of automobile accident victims, under the No-fault Motor Vehicle Insurance Act.


OVERVIEW: Two separate appellees, who were both survivors of automobile accident victims, sought to recover benefits from two separate appellant no-fault insurance companies. The court affirmed the order, holding that appellees were entitled to survivor's and work-loss benefits under the Act. The court found that the decedents' contribution of income under survivor's loss recovery should be excluded from the amount recoverable under work loss benefits so that both basic loss benefits complimented rather than duplicated each other.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order and held that appellees, who were survivors of automobile accident victims, were entitled to both survivor's and work loss benefits under the No-fault Motor Vehicle Insurance Act.

Richards v. Commonwealth


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


491 Pa. 162; 420 A.2d 391; 1980 Pa. LEXIS 801


March 4, 1980, Argued  
September 22, 1980, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 143 March Term, 1979, Appeal from the Order of the Commonwealth Court at No. 573 C.D. 1977, affirming the decision of the Unemployment Compensation Board of Review, Appeal No. B-SUA-76-7-L-441, Decision No. B-139427.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant applicant sought review of an order of the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania), which affirmed the decision of appellee Unemployment Compensation Board of Review denying benefits to appellant under the Unemployment Compensation Act, 43 P.S. § 802(b)(2)(I).


OVERVIEW: Appellant applicant was denied unemployment compensation benefits by appellee Unemployment Compensation Board of Review under the Unemployment Compensation Act, 43 P.S. § 802(b)(2)(I). Appellee determined that appellant voluntarily terminated her employment to join her spouse who had moved 90 miles away from her employer. The lower court affirmed appellee's decision. The court reversed and remanded for computation of benefits, holding that appellant met her burden of proof under § 802(b)(2)(I). The court found that appellant demonstrated that her desire to join her spouse was not the predominant reason for terminating her employment and that she was compelled to re-locate by economic necessity. The court noted that appellant's husband had been fired from his job, and, as a result, the family was evicted from a rent-free home and that their only viable option was to move to a city 90 miles away, where they were allowed to stay in another rent-free house. Appellant testified that she left her employment because she was unable to commute the 90 miles to and from her former employer every day.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of appellee Unemployment Compensation Board of Review which denied benefits for appellant applicant, holding that appellant demonstrated that she terminated her employment and re-located by economic necessity because her husband was fired from his job, the family was evicted from their home, and they were allowed to stay in a rent-free house 90 miles away. The court remanded for computation of benefits.

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant injured and his wife challenged a judgment by the Commonwealth Court in Pennsylvania, which dismissed their action in trespass against appellee department of public welfare and state hospital on the ground that 1978 Pa. Laws 788, § 1 et seq. barred the action.


OVERVIEW: Appellant employee and his wife alleged that as a result of negligence and reckless and wanton conduct by appellees, the department of public welfare and a state hospital, a former state hospital patient was able to gain access to the administration building of the hospital and shoot appellant, a hospital employee. The trial court dismissed appellants' action on the ground that 1978 Pa. Laws 788, § 1 et seq., which created statutory sovereign immunity in certain categories, barred the action. The court vacated the judgment and remanded for further proceedings. OUTCOME: The court vacated the trial court's judgment and remanded for further proceedings. The court held that a statute could not be constitutionally applied to bar the action by appellant employee and his wife against appellee, the department of public welfare and a state hospital, because the action accrued and was in existence prior to the passage of the law.

Abramovich v. Pennsylvania Liquor Control Bd.


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


490 Pa. 290; 416 A.2d 474; 1980 Pa. LEXIS 713


May 19, 1980, Argued  
July 3, 1980, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 42 May Term, 1979, Appeal from Order of Commonwealth Court dated April 5, 1979 at No. 37T.D. 1978, wherein the Commonwealth Court affirmed the Opinion of the Court of Common Pleas of Dauphin County at No. 3661 Equity 1977.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant liquor distributor sought review of an order from the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania), which affirmed the trial court's decision approving a contract arbitration award in favor of appellee Pennsylvania Liquor Control Board (PLCB). Appellee attorney served as the arbitrator and, unknown to appellant, was also assigned counsel to appellee PLCB.


OVERVIEW: Appellant liquor distributor contracted with appellee Pennsylvania Liquor Control Board (PLCB) to distribute liquor. Appellant and appellee PLCB submitted to arbitration a contract dispute, and appellee attorney served as the sole arbitrator. Unknown to appellant, appellee attorney was also the deputy attorney general assigned as counsel to appellee PLCB and was intimately associated with the agreement he reviewed. In addition, appellee attorney had inappropriately discussed several aspects of the particular case with members of appellee PLCB. The court vacated appellee attorney's arbitration award in favor of appellee PLCB, noting that procedural due process required that a fair hearing be conducted by one not involved with a party to the proceeding.


OUTCOME: The court vacated appellee attorney's arbitration award in favor of appellee Pennsylvania Liquor Control Board (PLCB) in a contract dispute with appellant liquor distributor because procedural due process required that a fair hearing be conducted by one not involved with a party to the proceeding. Because appellee attorney was assigned counsel to appellee PLCB, his award violated appellant's right of procedural due process.

Commonwealth v. Pa. Power Co.


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


490 Pa. 399; 416 A.2d 995; 1980 Pa. LEXIS 743; 14 ERC (BNA) 1796; 10 ELR 20941


March 10, 1980, Argued  
July 3, 1980, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 113 March Term 1978, Appeal from the Order of the Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania at No. 892 C.D. 1976, Reversing in Part the Adjudication of the Environmental Hearing Board of Pennsylvania at No. 72-428-CP-C.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, the Department of Environmental Resources, challenged an order of the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania), finding the assessment of penalties for appellee power company's sulfur dioxide emission violations was an unconstitutional violation of appellee's property interests.


OVERVIEW: Appellant, the Department of Environmental Resources, filed a civil penalty petition against appellee power company for its failure to file a plan with appellant that would assure compliance with emission standards. A decision assessing penalties for particulate matter emission violations was upheld but that portion assessing penalties for sulfur dioxide emission violations was reversed as an unconstitutional violation of appellee's property rights. On review, the court reversed as to the finding of unconstitutionality and remanded. The court found the imposition of civil penalties, was not done as punishment for a willful disregard of an agency or court order, but rather as an incentive to urge the development of procedures that would eventually eliminate the pollutant. The court held that the state had the authority to force termination of an industrial activity for noncompliance with technological impossible air quality standards. The court held that such so called technology forcing, by the imposition of civil penalties, was reasonably related to the reduction of pollution and, therefore was not a constitutional violation of appellee's property interests.


OUTCOME: The court reversed that portion of the order declaring the imposition of civil penalties unconstitutional and remanded for consideration of appellee power company's remaining claims because the imposition of civil penalties was reasonably related to the reduction of pollution.

Commonwealth v. Field


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


490 Pa. 519; 417 A.2d 160; 1980 Pa. LEXIS 755


May 19, 1980, Argued  
July 3, 1980, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 80-1-31, Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County, Pennsylvania, Criminal Division, at CC7906073A, entered January 31, 1980, granting appellee's Motion to Dismiss Information.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The commonwealth challenged an order from the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County (Pennsylvania), which dismissed the information charging defendant with homicide by vehicle on the ground that the statute under which defendant was charged, 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 3732, was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, driving a tractor-trailer rig, fatally injured a news cameraman while attempting to exit an oil company terminal, the exit to which was congested with picketers and spectators. Defendant was charged with driving on the sidewalk, a violation of 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 3703, and with homicide by vehicle, a violation of 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 3732. Section 3732 defined "homicide by vehicle" as a death caused by any person's conduct violating law or municipal ordinance applying to vehicles or traffic regulation. The court concluded that § 3732 was not unconstitutional due to vagueness. The court disagreed with defendant's contention that § 3732 unconstitutionally imposed criminal liability without fault and found that § 3732 required the commonwealth to establish that it was defendant's conduct which caused death. This requirement of personal responsibility precluded defendant's constitutional challenge. The court reversed the lower court's order declaring § 3732 unconstitutional, ordered the charge of homicide by vehicle reinstated, and remanded the case for appropriate proceedings.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the lower court's order, which declared the homicide by vehicle statute unconstitutional, ordered the charge of homicide by vehicle reinstated, and remanded the case for appropriate proceedings because the statute required the commonwealth to establish that it was defendant's conduct that caused death. The requirement of personal responsibility precluded any constitutional challenge to the statute.

Commonwealth v. Pounds


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


490 Pa. 621; 417 A.2d 597; 1980 Pa. LEXIS 744


March 3, 1980, Submitted  
July 3, 1980, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 129 March Term 1978, Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas, Criminal Division, Allegheny County, at No. CC7506139A


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the judgment of sentence imposed by the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County (Pennsylvania) and claimed his right to a speedy trial had been violated by a two-year delay between the jury's verdict and imposition of sentence and that the court erred when it refused to instruct the jury on the defense of alibi.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of first-degree murder and sentenced to life imprisonment, which he appealed. The court reversed the judgment and ordered a new trial and held that the delay of almost two years did not extend beyond the collective maximum term of imprisonment for the offenses which defendant was convicted and therefore the period of delay did not render the sentencing invalid. The court further held that the delay was neither purposeful nor oppressive and therefore defendant's right to a speedy trial had not been violated. However the court also held that the evidence that placed defendant at locations distinct from vicinity of the crime was sufficient to raise an alibi defense and although there was substantial evidence of defendant's guilt on the record, defendant was entitled to an alibi instruction and the absence of such an instruction was reversible error.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment and ordered a new trial and found defendant's right to a speedy trial was not violated because delay did not exceed the term of imprisonment for offense defendant was convicted of, but that trial court erred when it failed to provide jury with alibi instruction because defendant presented sufficient evidence to raise reasonable doubt as to his presence at the scene of the crime.

Commonwealth v. Coder


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


490 Pa. 194; 415 A.2d 406; 1980 Pa. LEXIS 686



June 10, 1980, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 224 JANUARY TERM, 1978, Appeal from the Order of the Superior Court at No. 318, April Term, Reversing the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Mercer County, at No. 6, August Term, 1974.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The commonwealth sought review of an order of the Superior Court (Pennsylvania), which reversed an order of the trial court directing appellee mayor to pay the costs of his prosecution for wiretapping conspiracy and false swearing, including the costs incurred in a change of venue.


OVERVIEW: Appellee mayor's motion for a change of venue due to extensive pretrial publicity was granted in a prosecution for wiretapping conspiracy and false swearing. Following conviction, the commonwealth sought recoupment of the necessary costs of prosecution, as authorized by 19 P.S. § 1223. The trial court taxed appellee with costs, including those accruing from the change of venue. The superior court reversed the order and held that appellee was liable only for the amount of necessary costs if trial had taken place in the county where the complaint was filed. On the commonwealth's appeal the court held that recoupment of the costs accruing from a change of venue did not constitute a penalty and did not impose a chilling effect on appellee's constitutional right to a fair trial. The superior court's order was reversed and the case was remanded for a hearing at which the burden would be on the commonwealth to demonstrate which costs were necessary; if any costs were attributable solely to charges which were dismissed on appellee's motion, apportionment of costs would be required.


OUTCOME: The court held that recoupment of the costs of prosecution, including the costs accruing from a change of venue, did not constitute a penalty and did not impose a chilling effect on appellee mayor's constitutional right to a fair trial. The superior court's order was reversed and the case was remanded for an evidentiary hearing at which the burden would be on the commonwealth to show that the costs for which recovery was sought were necessary.

Gibson v. Commonwealth


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


490 Pa. 156; 415 A.2d 80; 1980 Pa. LEXIS 636


March 11, 1980, Argued  
June 2, 1980, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 155 March Term, 1979, No. 156 March Term, 1979, No. 157 March Term, 1979, No. 161 March Term, 1979, No. 172 March Term, 1979., Appeals from the Orders of the Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania, entered on September 25, 1979 at Nos. 1594, 1644, 1669, 1693 and 2317 C.D. 1978.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant flood victims sought review of the orders of the Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania, which, under the sovereign immunity act 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 5110, granted appellee commonwealth's and department of environmental resources' motion to dismiss the consolidated negligence cases appellants filed against them.


OVERVIEW: Appellant flood victims suffered loss of life, limb and property when a dam flooded their community in July 1977. Various trespass actions were filed by appellants against appellee commonwealth and department of environmental resources between July and October 1978, alleging negligent supervision of the dam. In September 1978 the legislature passed a sovereign immunity act codified at 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 5110. The lower court consolidated the cases pursuant to Pa. R. Civ. P. 213(a). Appellees filed a motion to dismiss on the basis of immunity, and the motion was granted. The court held that appellants' cause of action accrued on the date of the flood and thus had accrued before the sovereign immunity act was implemented. The court held that application of the act to appellants' action was not constitutional. It held that the prior case law that abrogated sovereign immunity was applicable to the case. It reversed the orders and remanded the consolidated case for further proceedings.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the orders because in a case of first impression it found that the application of the sovereign immunity act in 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 5110 to appellant flood victims' causes of action was improper. Appellants' negligence claims against appellee commonwealth and department of environmental resources accrued before the effective date of the act. The consolidated case was remanded for further proceedings.

Commonwealth v. Bonadio


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania

March 7, 1980, Argued  
May 30, 1980, Decided

CC7901508AM CC7901507A, CC79-01486A and CC7901489A.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The commonwealth sought review of an order of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County (Pennsylvania), which granted defendants' motion to quash the information charging them under the Voluntary Deviate Sexual Intercourse Statute, 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. §§ 3101, 3124 (1973).


OVERVIEW: Defendants were arrested at an "adult" pornographic theater on charges of voluntary deviate sexual intercourse and conspiracy to perform the same under the Voluntary Deviate Sexual Intercourse Statute, 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. §§ 3101, 3124 (1973). The trial court granted defendants' motion to quash an information on the ground that the statute was unconstitutional. The court affirmed, holding that the statute exceeded the proper bounds of police power because the only possible purpose of the statute was to regulate the private conduct of consenting adults. 183 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Burt


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


490 Pa. 173; 415 A.2d 89; 1980 Pa. LEXIS 651


March 12, 1980, Argued  
May 30, 1980, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 61 March Term, 1979, Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of the 37th Judicial District of Pennsylvania, Warren County Branch, Criminal Division at No. 449 of 1978, entered March 19, 1979, Homicide by Vehicle.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state challenged a judgment by the Court of Common Pleas of the 37th Judicial District of Pennsylvania, Warren County Branch, which dismissed the charge of homicide by vehicle, as provided in 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 3732, against defendant on the ground that the statute was unconstitutionally vague.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged, along with other crimes, for the first-degree misdemeanor offense of homicide by vehicle, 75 Pa. Const. Stat. § 3732. The trial court held that § 3732 was unconstitutionally vague and dismissed the charge. The state filed a pre-trial challenge to the order. The court reversed, reinstated the charge, and remanded for further proceedings. The court held that the fact that the legislature might have chosen clear and more precise language equally capable of achieving the end which it sought did not mean that the statute which was in fact drafted was unconstitutionally vague. Rather, the court held that the requirements of due process were satisfied if the statute in question contained reasonable standards to guide the prospective conduct. The court held that § 3732 contained entirely reasonable standards. The court held that § 3732 clearly defined what constituted "homicide by vehicle" and did not employ ambiguous words, archaic classifications, or words with numerous and varied meanings.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's dismissal of the charge of homicide by vehicle against defendant, reinstated the charge, and remanded for further proceedings. The offense, as it was defined by statute, was not constitutionally vague because the statute contained clear words and definitions and reasonable standards to guide prospective conduct.

 

184 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Hayes


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


489 Pa. 419; 414 A.2d 318; 1980 Pa. LEXIS 577; 6 Media L. Rep. 1273


March 11, 1980, Argued  
May 1, 1980, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 63 Western District Miscellaneous Docket, 1980, Petition for Extraordinary Jurisdistion and Summary Reversal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Intervenor news media petitioned for exercise of plenary jurisdiction and summary reversal of an order from the lower court (Pennsylvania), which denied intervenor's motion for an open suppression hearing to be held in connection with the prosecution of defendant for sexually assaulting and supplying drugs to a high school student.


OVERVIEW: As an initial matter, the court noted that the instant closure order had been entered pursuant to Pa. R. Crim. P. 323(f) at the express request of the defense and concurred in by the state. The court then rejected defendant's contention that, although sequestration would protect his U.S. Const. amend. VI rights, defendant's right of privacy would have been jeopardized by the use of sequestration in the instant case. The court found that the accepted remedy for the protection of the privacy right was the application of the doctrine of exclusion, noting that defendant was urging for a more stringent remedy without sufficient justification of the need for such additional protection. The court accordingly granted the request for extraordinary jurisdiction, reversed the denial of intervenor news media's motion for an open suppression hearing, and remanded the cause for further proceedings. The court concluded that in light of the availability of sequestration, which fully protected defendant's fair trial rights, did not impede the case's orderly resolution or unduly increase the administrative costs, the trial court lacked jurisdiction to deny public access to the hearing.


OUTCOME: The court granted intervenor news media's petition for extraordinary jurisdiction and reversed the order denying the motion to open the suppression hearing in question. It was improper to close the proceedings where defendant's fair trial considerations were fully satisfied by a means which did not intrude upon the public's access to criminal proceedings and the prosecution and the orderly administration of justice were not adversely affected.

Fabio v. Civil Serv. Com


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


489 Pa. 309; 414 A.2d 82; 1980 Pa. LEXIS 617; 9 A.L.R.4th 600


October 16, 1979, Argued  
April 30, 1980, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 715 January Term, 1977, Appeal from Order of the Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania dated May 16, 1977, No. 1775 C.D. 1975, affirming the Order of the Philadelphia Court of Common Pleas, No. 2864, June Term, 1974, which affirmed the Order of the Civil Service Commission.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant police officer challenged an order of the Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania which affirmed a lower court's affirmance of appellee commission's holding that appellant had been guilty of conduct unbecoming an officer in violation of Art. I, § 1.75 of the Philadelphia Police Duty Manual and ordered his dismissal from the Philadelphia Police Department.


OVERVIEW: Appellee commission held that appellant police officer was guilty of conduct unbecoming an officer, in violation of art. I, § 1.75 of the Philadelphia Police Duty Manual and ordered his dismissal from the Philadelphia Police Department. Appellant had arranged for his wife to have an affair with another officer and appellant had an affair with his wife's 18-year-old sister. The court held that the provision was not unconstitutionally void for vagueness on its face or as applied because it found that the scheme and theme of art. I of the Police Department Duty Manual provided ample warning as to the types of conduct which would not be condoned. The court found the courts had made clear that a police officer has a duty to maintain an honorable official, as well as private life. The court held that appellant's dismissal did not violate his constitutionally protected right to privacy because the courts have held police officers to a higher standard of conduct than other citizens and because the government had a compelling state interest in the maintenance of public respect for police officers. Accordingly, the court affirmed the order the lower court.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the lower court which affirmed a lower court's affirmance of appellee commission's finding that appellant police officer was guilty of conduct unbecoming an officer and dismissing him and held that the provision of the Police Department Duty Manual at issue was not unconstitutionally void for vagueness and appellant's privacy rights were not infringed.

Commonwealth v. Mastrangelo


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


489 Pa. 254; 414 A.2d 54; 1980 Pa. LEXIS 621


January 21, 1980, Argued  
April 28, 1980, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 203 January Term, 1978, Appeal from Judgment of the Superior Court of November 30, 1977 at No. 1234 October Term, 1977, Affirming the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas, Criminal Division, Chester County at No. 0346 of 1976.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of his convictions for disorderly conduct in violation of 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 5503 and obstructing administration of law or other governmental function in violation of 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 5101 by the Court of Common Pleas of Chester County (Pennsylvania), attacking the constitutionality of § 5503 and alleging that the evidence was insufficient to sustain either conviction.


OVERVIEW: After screaming epithets at a meter maid who was performing her official duty, appellant was convicted of disorderly conduct in violation of 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 5503 and obstructing administration of law or other governmental function in violation of 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 5101. Appellant sought review of his convictions, alleging that § 5503 was facially invalid on the ground of vagueness, or, in the alternative, was invalid as applied to him. Appellant also challenged the sufficiency of the evidence. The court held that the disorderly conduct statute, § 5503, was not facially invalid for vagueness because a man of common intelligence would understand what was and what was not made criminal under the statute, especially given that the statute could not be used to punish anyone exercising a protected right under U.S. Const. amend. I. The court held that § 5503 was not invalid as applied to appellant because he was not exercising any constitutionally protected right when he loudly hurled epithets, which met the definition of fighting words, at the meter maid. The court affirmed appellant's convictions, holding that the evidence was more than sufficient to sustain them.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed appellant's convictions for disorderly conduct and obstructing administration of law or other governmental function because the evidence was sufficient to sustain the convictions, and the disorderly conduct statute was not facially invalid for vagueness or unconstitutionally applied to appellant.

 

187 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Green


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


488 Pa. 611; 413 A.2d 651; 1980 Pa. LEXIS 603


March 3, 1980, Argued  
April 25, 1980, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Post-conviction proceeding at Commonwealth v. Green, 315 Pa. Super. 564, 462 A.2d 736, 1983 Pa. Super. LEXIS 3257 (1983)
Post-conviction proceeding at, Decision reached on appeal by Commonwealth v. Green, 2014 Pa. Super. Unpub. LEXIS 4121 (2014)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 71 March Term, 1979, Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Erie County, Pennsylvania, No. 880, 881 of 1978 Criminal Division


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged the judgment of sentence of the Court of Common Pleas, Erie County (Pennsylvania), in an action involving the consolidation of separate informations, use of photographs as evidence, appellant's statements about the use of drugs, and the right to due process of law in the application of 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 302.


OVERVIEW: Appellant entered the 87-year-old victim's residence. In order to prevent her from later identifying him, he struck her in the face with an isometric exercise bar. He was found guilty of murder of the first degree and burglary. On direct appeal, the court affirmed, holding that the consolidation of separate informations would not be disturbed because the charges were interrelated and arose out of one criminal episode. Police photographs of the blood-stained curtain, wall, and the position of the lower portion of the body and a recent photograph of the victim with her grandchildren, were relevant in that they corroborated commonwealth's version of the homicide and showed whether the victim would have been able to flee as appellant described. Appellant's statements concerning the sale and use of drugs made less than an hour before the crime were an integral part of the events and of his apprehension; they were outside the scope of the general rule of exclusion. Instructing the jury on 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 302, which disallows voluntary intoxication as a defense, did not deny appellant his right to due process of law; "intoxication" was not unreasonably vague or standardless.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed, because consolidation of the information for murder of the first degree and for burglary was proper where there was only one criminal episode; admission of photographs that were not excessively gruesome nor inflammatory was not error; statements regarding drug use were admissible to establish appellant's identity; and statutory use of "intoxication" to disallow defense of voluntary intoxication was not unconstitutional.

Nat'l Wood Preservers v. Commonwealth Dep't of Envtl. Res.


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


489 Pa. 221; 414 A.2d 37; 1980 Pa. LEXIS 569; 14 ERC (BNA) 1487; 10 ELR 20724


December 10, 1979, Argued  
April 24, 1980, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Nos. 66 & 67 January Term, 1979, No. 68 January Term, 1979, Appeals from orders of the Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania, dated May 24, 1978 Docket Nos. 1389, 1390, 1395 and 1415 C.D. 1976, affirming in part and reversing in part the orders of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania Environmental Hearing Board dated July 30, 1976, at Docket Nos. 73-253-D, 73-249-D, 73-256-D, 73-346-D.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant property owners sought review of orders from the Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania, which upheld the administrative orders from the hearing board for appellee Department of Environmental Resources that directed appellants, pursuant to 35 P.S. § 691.316, first to conduct drilling and water sampling to determine the precise amount and dispersion of the pollutant pooled under the subject property, and then to remove it.


OVERVIEW: On review, the appellate court held that 35 P.S. § 691.316 clearly and unambiguously authorized appellee Department of Environmental Resources to require the correction of water pollution causing conditions without regard to the source of the pollution, noting that the legislative history also supported such an interpretation. The court then found that a consideration of the history of The Clean Streams Law compelled the court to reject appellant property owners' limited view of the statute's broad purpose in combating water pollution generally. The court determined that the challenged statute was a permissible exercise of the police power under Pa. Const. art. I, § 10, noting that the interests of the public required the interference authorized by the statute, and that the means used by appellee were reasonably necessary for the accomplishment of the statutory purpose, and not unduly oppressive upon individuals. The court affirmed, concluding that the validity of the challenged exercise of police power over appellants' land had depended little upon appellants' responsibility for causing the condition that gave rise to the pollution.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the orders that required appellant property owners to correct the conditions that were causing water pollution on their property, concluding that appellee Department of Environmental Resources was properly authorized, under a valid exercise of police power, to require the correction of such pollution without regard to its source or the lack of appellants' fault in creating the condition that had given rise to the pollution.

 

189 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Bronson v. Commonwealth Bd. of Probation & Parole


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


491 Pa. 549; 421 A.2d 1021; 1980 Pa. LEXIS 536


December 13, 1979, Argued  
March 20, 1980, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Denied November 14, 1980.


PRIOR HISTORY: No. 213 January Term, 1979, Appeal from the Order of the Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania at 141 Miscellaneous Docket No. 2 denying a Petition for Review of an action by the Pennsylvania Board of Probation and Parole revoking Appellant's parole at Indictment No. 3287, April Term, 1971, of Allegheny County.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant parolee sought review of the order of the Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania that denied his request for court-appointed appellate counsel to assist him and denied his petition for review of the order of appellee parole board revoking his parole.


OVERVIEW: While on parole, appellant parolee was convicted of various charges. Appellee parole board then revoked his parole and appellant petitioned for review by the commonwealth court. His petition was summarily denied, but he thereafter filed another petition without the benefit of counsel. He was again denied relief and he appealed. The case was remanded to the commonwealth court for resolution of the issue of court-appointed counsel. Treating his petition as one for mandamus, the commonwealth court found appellant was not entitled to appointed counsel and denied him relief. He again appealed. In reversing, the supreme court ruled that appellant's petition was not for mandamus, but was an appeal of right from an administrative agency, guaranteed by Pa. Const. art. V, § 9. Also, an indigent parolee was entitled to appointed counsel in parole revocation proceedings before appellee because it was a critical stage at which a substantial right might be affected. It followed, then, that appellant was constitutionally entitled to appointed counsel in the appeal from revocation proceedings.


OUTCOME: The order denying appellant relief was reversed and the case was remanded for appointment of counsel and further proceedings because appellant's action before the commonwealth court was not a mandamus action, but was a constitutionally guaranteed appeal from the action of an administrative agency, and he had a constitutional right to appointed counsel for both revocation proceedings before appellee and appeals from such proceedings.

Commonwealth v. Meyer


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


488 Pa. 297; 412 A.2d 517; 1980 Pa. LEXIS 542


January 21, 1980, Argued  
March 20, 1980, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 388 January Term, 1977, Appeal from the order of Court of Common Pleas of Cumberland County, at No. 280 Criminal, 1975, dated May 4, 1976, Sustaining Defendant's Application for Pretrial Relief.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The commonwealth appealed from an order of the Court of Common Pleas, Criminal Division, Cumberland County (Pennsylvania), suppressing both the pre and post-arrest statements obtained from appellee as well as the results of a post-arrest breathalyzer test in a driving under the influence case.


OVERVIEW: Appellee was arrested and charged with driving under the influence of alcohol. The trial court, based upon pre-trial motions, ordered the suppression of both pre and post-arrest statements the police obtained from appellee as well as the results of a post-arrest breathalyzer test. The court held that the trial court correctly suppressed appellee's pre-arrest statement because the police failed to read appellee his Miranda rights. The court affirmed the order insofar as it suppressed appellee's pre-arrest statement. However, the court found that the trial court suppressed the post-arrest statements and results of the post-arrest breathalyzer test on an erroneous theory that appellee's arrest was unlawful. Accordingly, the court vacated the trial court's decision to the extent it suppressed the post-arrest statements and the results of the post-arrest breathalyzer test.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed in part because the suppression of appellee's pre-arrest statement that was made before he was charged with drunken driving was proper because he was not informed of his Miranda rights. The suppression of post-arrest statements and breathalyzer test was improper.

Commonwealth v. Patterson


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


488 Pa. 227; 412 A.2d 481; 1980 Pa. LEXIS 526


October 12, 1979, Submitted  
March 20, 1980, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Nos. 665 & 668 January Term, 1977, Appeal from Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas, Criminal Trial Division, of Philadelphia, at Nos. 0061, 0062, 0065 March Term, 1977. Nos. 77-03-0058-0059


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of his conviction of three counts of first degree murder by the Court of Common Pleas, Criminal Trial Division, Philadelphia (Pennsylvania).


OVERVIEW: Appellant and three others were questioned about the murders of the family of one of appellant's friends. Appellant was questioned, then voluntarily waited overnight at the police station until police finished questioning his girlfriend. While waiting, appellant confessed that he was present at the murders, but only participated in the murder of one of the victims. Appellant took a polygraph, then gave a signed confession to police. At appellant's arraignment on three counts of murder, appellant admitted that he murdered one of the victims. Appellant was then tried and convicted of the murders. Appellant sought review and the court affirmed the conviction. The court held that the trial court properly refused to suppress appellant's statements, that there was no unnecessary delay which would have required appellant's statements to be suppressed, that appellant's waiver of his rights was knowing and intelligent, and that the trial court properly refused to remove a juror who recognized a witness. The court reversed appellant's death sentence as unconstitutional, and remanded for a new sentence.


OUTCOME: Appellant's murder conviction was affirmed because there was no error in refusing to suppress appellant's statements, and because appellant's waiver of his rights was knowing and intelligent. There was no unnecessary delay which would have required appellant's statements to be suppressed. However, the court reversed appellant's death sentence as unconstitutional, and remanded for a new sentence.
