Tsarnas v. Jones & Laughlin Steel Corp.


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


488 Pa. 513; 412 A.2d 1094; 1980 Pa. LEXIS 547


September 25, 1979, Argued  
March 20, 1980, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 47 March Term, 1979, Appeal from the Order of the Superior Court at No. 820 April Term affirming the order of the Court of Common Pleas Civil Division of Allegheny County at No. GD7618134


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant company sought review of an order of the Superior Court (Pennsylvania), which affirmed the trial court's decision which sustained appellee employer's preliminary objections and entered judgment in favor of appellee.


OVERVIEW: A worker employed by appellee employer was injured at work when a vertical steel sag bar became detached from its fasteners and caused the worker to fall and sustain injuries. The worker filed an action against several defendants, including appellant company. Appellant then filed an action to join appellee as an additional defendant. Appellee filed preliminary objections to appellant's complaint on the grounds that the action was barred by § 303(b) of the Workmen's Compensation Act, 77 P.S. § 481(b). Appellant argued that § 303(b) was unconstitutional. The trial court found that § 303(b) had been constitutionally established and sustained appellee's objections. Appellant sought review, and the appellate court affirmed. Appellant against sought review, and the court affirmed and held that § 303(b) did not violate Pa. Const. art. I, § 11, because the purpose of § 303(b) was to restrict an injured worker's remedy, that it did not violate Pa. Const. art. III, § 18, because it did not limit the amount of recoverable damages, and that it did not violate the equal protection clause of U.S. Const. amend. XIV because it did not affect a fundamental right or involve a suspect class.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the appellate court's decision because the Workmen's Compensation Act did not violate the Pennsylvania Constitution because it did not limit the amount of recoverable damages, or the Fourteenth Amendment because it did not affect a fundamental right or involve a suspect class.

Gaito v. Pennsylvania Bd. of Probation & Parole


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


488 Pa. 397; 412 A.2d 568; 1980 Pa. LEXIS 549


September 24, 1979, Submitted  
March 20, 1980, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Denied March 25, 1980.


PRIOR HISTORY: No. 70 March Term, 1979, Appeal from Order and Opinion of the Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania at No. 21 Miscellaneous Docket No. 2, Dismissing Appellant's Petition for Review, dated October 19, 1978.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After appellant's parole was revoked by appellee parole board, he filed a petition for review with the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania). He challenged the court's order dismissing his petition.


OVERVIEW: The court upheld the lower court's order denying appellant's petition for review of appellee parole board's revocation of his parole. It rejected his argument that the firearms conviction that led to his recommitment was based upon an illegal arrest, writing that the lower court did not have jurisdiction to inquire into an arrest's legality. It held that appellee was not required to apply appellant's time spent on parole against either his original sentences or his firearms sentence, writing that the matter had been settled by earlier cases. It found no merit to his claims that his revocation hearing was held in an untimely manner or that his incarceration in a diagnostic and classification center after his recommitment was cruel and unusual punishment. Finally, it held that how appellant's time spent in custody prior to sentencing on the firearms violation should be credited depended on whether he had been able to satisfy the bail requirements on the new charges. Because the record did not indicate whether appellant had satisfied such requirements, it remanded the case for a determination of the matter and any necessary recomputation.


OUTCOME: Finding that the lower court had no jurisdiction to consider whether the conviction that led to appellant's parole revocation by appellee parole board was based upon an illegal arrest, the court upheld its order denying appellant's petition for review of the revocation. It remanded the case to determine whether appellant had satisfied his bail requirements so that his time spent in custody prior to sentencing could be credited accordingly.

In re Application of Biester


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


487 Pa. 438; 409 A.2d 848; 1979 Pa. LEXIS 761



December 26, 1979, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 91 E. D. Misc. Dkt. 1979, No. 98 E. D. Misc. Dkt. 1979


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Pennsylvania attorney general requested an order directing that a multicounty investigating grand jury with statewide jurisdiction be summoned. Petitioner taxpayer filed an application to intervene, and the attorney general argued that petitioner was without standing as an intervenor.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner taxpayer attempted to intervene in an action brought by the Pennsylvania state attorney general to request an order directing that a multi-county investigating grand jury be summoned. As intervenor, he sought dismissal of the attorney general's application, and the attorney general argued that the taxpayer was without standing as an intervenor or as a petitioner. The court initially granted petitioner's application to intervene, because it said he had standing as a taxpayer seeking to prevent waste of tax revenue for allegedly illegal expenditures. The court found that the purpose of the standing requirement was to protect against improper plaintiffs. To meet the standing requirement, a plaintiff had to prove his interest in the action's outcome surpassed the common interest of all citizens in procuring obedience to the law. The court concluded that petitioner's interest was not immediate, because the detriment to the taxpayer was too remote and he was not directly affected by the loss. In vacating the order granting intervention, the court held that the taxpayer did not have standing.


OUTCOME: The court vacated its order granting intervention to petitioner taxpayer and dismissed the petition for review. It held that petitioner was without standing to advance the actions.

196 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


In re Adoption of E.M.A.


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


487 Pa. 152; 409 A.2d 10; 1979 Pa. LEXIS 758


September 18, 1979, Argued  
December 21, 1979, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 7 March Term, 1979, Appeal from the Decree dated January 11, 1979, of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County, Pennsylvania, Orphans' Court Division, at No. 368 of 1977.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, who obtained natural mother's consent to adoption of her child and natural father's qualified consent, appealed from order of Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County, Pennsylvania, denying appellant's adoption petition on ground natural father's consent did not meet requirements of Adoption Act, 1 P.S. §§ 101 et seq.


OVERVIEW: Appellant filed a petition to adopt a child. The child's natural mother consented to her adoption by appellant. The natural father executed a qualified consent, retaining his parental rights. The trial court denied appellant's petition for adoption, holding the natural father's consent did not meet the statutory requirements for adoption by a nonspouse. The court affirmed. The Adoption Act, 1 P.S. §§ 101 et seq. (1978), required, as a prerequisite to adoption, either the unqualified consent of natural parents or the termination of their parental rights. The only provision for a qualified consent was contained in 1 P.S. § 503. By its express terms, section 503 was limited to adoption by the spouse of a natural parent. Only in such intra-family adoptions could a natural parent execute a valid consent retaining parental rights. And only in such a husband-wife relationship was the qualified consent legally sufficient for the spouse seeking to become an adopting parent. Appellant was not the natural father's spouse. The court rejected appellant's alternative claim that section 503 was unconstitutional, holding it did not discriminate against unmarried persons who wished to adopt.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the denial of appellant's adoption petition. A qualified consent to adoption was only sufficient under the Adoption Act in the case of an adoption by the spouse of a natural parent and here appellant was not the spouse of the natural father.

Young v. Commonwealth Board of Probation & Parole


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


487 Pa. 428; 409 A.2d 843; 1979 Pa. LEXIS 803


October 9, 1979, Submitted  
December 21, 1979, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 556 January Term, 1977, Appeal from Order of the Commonwealth Court at No. 203 Miscellaneous Docket, Dismissing Appellant's Complaint in Mandamus; Lehigh County Court of Common Pleas Nos. 281 through 283, 1971, and Nos. 2393, 2395 and 2397 of 1975.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of a decision of the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania), which sustained preliminary objections in the nature of a demurrer and dismissed his complaint against appellee Parole Board. Appellant asserted an unlawful recomputation of his prison sentence following his conviction for a second offense committed while he was on parole.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was arrested and convicted of a second offense while on parole for a previous offense. After a parole violation hearing, appellee Parole Board recommitted appellant for the balance of his original sentence, without giving him credit for the time he spent on parole. Appellant alleged that this was an illegal enhancement of his sentence because its order by the executive branch of government violated the doctrine of separation of powers. The court held that allowing appellant to have the benefit of "street time" during which he ignored the conditions of his parole would render parole ineffective as a corrective device, and this was not an enhancement of his sentence. The court found that the decision of the Parole Board to deny credit for "street time" was in accord with the constitutional provisions of 61 P.S. § 331.21a, and it dismissed appellant's complaint.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the lower court sustaining the preliminary objections and dismissing appellant's complaint against appellee Parole Board. The court held that the Board's decision to recommit appellant, following his conviction for a second crime, for the balance of his original sentence without giving credit for the period of time while he was on parole did not encroach upon judicial sentencing power and was not unconstitutional.

George v. George


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


487 Pa. 133; 409 A.2d 1; 1979 Pa. LEXIS 804


September 26, 1979, Argued  
December 21, 1979, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of an order of the Court of Common Pleas, Allegheny County (Pennsylvania), which dismissed her counterclaim against appellee in his divorce action.


OVERVIEW: Appellee brought a divorce action against appellant, and appellant's counterclaim sought a decree of divorce from bed and board as provided by 23 P.S. § 11.The trial court dismissed appellant's counterclaim on the grounds that § 11 was unconstitutional under the Equal Rights Amendment, Pa. Const. art. I, § 28, because § 28 allowed wives but not husbands to obtain such divorces when marital misconduct occurred. Appellant sought review of the order. The court ruled that the proper disposition was to apply § 28 in such a way as to read it as providing for reciprocity of remedies for spouses. The court reversed the order dismissing appellant's counterclaim, concluding that a finding of unconstitutionality was an alternative no longer available by virtue of 1 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 2301, which provided that it was the intent of § 28 that where, in any statute previously enacted, there was a designation restricted to a single sex, the designation was deemed to refer to both sexes unless the designation did not operate to deny or abridge equality of rights under the law of the commonwealth because of the sex of the individual.


OUTCOME: An order dismissing appellant's counterclaim against appellee was reversed where there was no exception in the area of domestic relations to the commonwealth law providing that equality of rights under the law not be denied or abridged in the commonwealth because of the sex of the individual.

Commonwealth v. Crowson


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


488 Pa. 537; 412 A.2d 1363; 1979 Pa. LEXIS 739


October 12, 1979, Submitted  
December 21, 1979, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Reconsideration Denied April 29, 1980.


PRIOR HISTORY: January Term, 1977 No. 503, Appeal from Judgment of Sentence, Philadelphia Common Pleas Court, Criminal Section, Trial Division, January Term, 1975, No. 1571


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of the judgment of the Philadelphia Common Pleas Court (Pennsylvania) after he was convicted of murder in the first degree and sentenced to death, on the grounds that the evidence was insufficient to support the conviction and that the sentence was illegal.


OVERVIEW: The victim's uncle was awakened, in the apartment he shared with the victim, to gunshots, followed by someone sliding something metal across the floor to the hall closet. Upon entering the apartment, which was bolted from the inside, the police found appellant and the victim, who had several gunshot wounds. The police recovered a weapon and ammunition, which was marked with a "D," from the hall closet. Ammunition later discovered at appellant's home was also marked with a "D." Ammunition marked in this manner was not found in general circulation in the United States. Appellant was convicted of murder in the first degree and sentenced to death. The court affirmed the conviction, holding that the evidence was sufficient to support the conviction, as appellant admitted his presence at the scene, appellant possessed the same rare type of ammunition found in the weapon at the crime scene, and the apartment doors had been bolted from the inside. The sentence was vacated and a sentence of life imprisonment was imposed, as the only valid penalty for murder in the first degree at the time of appellant's trial was life imprisonment, under 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 1102.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment, holding that the evidence was sufficient to support appellant's conviction for murder in the first degree because, inter alia, appellant possessed the same rare type of ammunition found at the crime scene; appellant's death sentence was vacated and a life sentence was imposed, as the death penalty statute had been declared unconstitutional.

 

200 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Evans


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


488 Pa. 38; 410 A.2d 1213; 1979 Pa. LEXIS 742


October 19, 1979, Argued  
December 21, 1979, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Denied February 4, 1980.


PRIOR HISTORY: Nos. 356 and 431 January Term, 1977, Appeals from Judgments of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas, criminal Trial Division, of Philadelphia, at Nos. 1506, 1510, 1516 and 1517 January Term, 1976


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of the judgment of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia (Pennsylvania) after he was convicted for murder in the first degree, two counts of robbery, and criminal conspiracy and sentenced to death for murder, on the ground, inter alia, that evidence obtained from a search of the apartment where he was staying should have been suppressed.


OVERVIEW: Appellant and others robbed a bar and killed a patron. Appellant was described as wearing a rabbit fur coat. A piece of paper with a telephone number was found in an attache case left behind by the assailants. The police went to the address where they discovered that a man wearing a rabbit fur coat was staying in an apartment in the house. The owner of the house gave them entry into the apartment she rented to a tenant. The police saw scuff marks on the wall and bits of plaster on the floor below a crawl space. In the crawl space, The police obtained a search warrant and seized, among other things, the weapon and the rabbit fur coat. Appellant was convicted of murder in the first degree, two counts of robbery, and criminal conspiracy and sentenced to death for murder. The court affirmed the convictions, holding, inter alia, that the search was legal, as the police had reasonable grounds to believe contraband would be found on the premises based on the statements from eyewitnesses and the paper in the attache case. The sentence was vacated, as the statute allowing the death penalty for murder had been declared unconstitutional. The case was remanded for resentencing.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment, holding that a search of the apartment where appellant was staying as a guest was legal because the police had reasonable grounds to believe that the weapon used by the assailants would be found there based on descriptions of appellant and evidence obtained at the scene of the murder. The death sentence for murder was vacated, as the statute allowing it had been declared unconstitutional.
